THE RED PAGE.
WHEN THE DEVIL WAS SICK
THE devil a saint would be. But Saint
Oscar !—the deformed transformed !
O sancta pudicitia ! Black’s white ;
Wilde’s tame ; and the chapel of Read-
ing gaol shall have a stained glass window to
the Sorrowful Martyr, with his tins in one
hand, his polishing rag t in the other,
and his ecstatic eyes fixed earnestly
on the blessed pages of the prison Bible.
“Every morning,” writes the new British
saint, “after I had qleaned my cell and
polished my tins, I read a little of the
Gospels, a dozen verses taken by chance
anywhere. It Is a delightful way of opening
the dag.”
Poor devil!
The British Nation takes this seriously ;
the Nonconformist conscience is stirred to
its sad depths. De Profundis is ‘ ‘ irresis-
tibly fascinating and helpful,” says The Echo.
* ‘ Society has vindicated itself towards
this sinner, and can afford to be Christian to
his memory,” urges The Westminster Gazette.
(You will find Christian British Society in
Pigs in Glover and Algernon Gasterton, or in
the last scandalous success of Dolf Wyllard
or Violet Hunt.) And The Daily News, the
“organ of the great middle classes,” the
ascetic journal of cocoa and renunciation,
admitting no base profits from sporting
advertisements, adds its weighty word. “He
Profundis passes immediately to its place
amongst those confessions in which men,
through great bitterness and the experience
of the raw edges of misery and shame, are
able to testify to a meaning and purpose in
it all.”
That settles it. Now Oscar Wilde’s books
may be read, his plays may be Staged again.
It is clear that there is moral profit in them,
and monetary. Some British people were
' beginning to grow uneasy at tlie thought of
monetary profit missed. Now, in the nick
of time, comes De Profundis to reconcile
Mammon and God once more, launching the
Unmentionable Artist down admiring British
ages as a Great Moral Lesson.
Mr. William Watson should write a sonnet
about it.
De Profundis is Oscar Wilde’s just-pub-
lished book of his meditations in Reading
gaol. It is very sad in itself, and very funny
in its relationships. Wilde saw the sadness,
but he did not see the fun : he had no sense
of humour. Like all fine artists, he took
nothing seriously but himself, and himself
he took very seriously. Comes moral Britain
to this gay butterfly, fluttering hither and
thither irresponsibly, and breaks it on the
bitterest wheel. The whimsical Irishman
under prison regulations, the aesthete pick-
ing oakum, the apostle of New Greece shut
up with the Gospels : these are some of
Fate’s tremendous ironies. And the irony
has entered into Wilde’s soul.
Is he sincere ? does he deceive himself ?
At least he does not deceive himself all the
time. But his new pose carries him away—
him weak, him dazed, him broken by
disaster. If it were profoundly calculated
for his public—“a perishing actor’s last
ambiguous gesture of salutation to the dense,
triumphant world, ” as E. Thomas calls it :
still, the actor may forget himself in his
part. Wilde has forgotten. He is there in
the depths of woe, in prison, with the prison
Bible to preserve his sanity. And he is
struck with the likeness between two Men of
Sorrows. He discusses Christ as the
precursor of the Romantic ‘ Movement
in Art—-he the follower. ; “Christ and
Me ” is the text of De Prof undis :
varied occasionally to “Me ' and Christ.”
One Can see The Daily News doubting
whether to dub the author Saint or Blas-
phemer. Says Wilde :
I see in Christ not merely the essentials of the
supreme romantic type, but all the accidents, the
wilfulness even, of the romantic temperament
also. He was the first person who ever said to
people that they should live “flower-like lives.”
He fixed the phrase. He took children as the
type of what people should try to become. He
held them up as examples to their elders, which I
myself have always thought the chief use of chil-
dren, if what is perfect should have a use.
And St. Oscar lived a flower-like life. The
satirist saw him walking down Piccadilly
“ Avith a rose-bush or a lily in his medieval
hand.” And St. Oscar held up children as
examples. If Britain had not canonised him,
the conjunction would be .. . curious. But
to a saint all things are saintly.
One remembers little Emmy telling Diana
of the Crossways about the beauty and utility
of sorrow. Wilde embroiders the theme in
the intervals of pounding oakum. “Sorrow,
be thou my Joy ! ” he cries in effect.
“Pleasure for the beautiful body, but pain
for the beautiful soul,” says the poor sick
devil that would be or appear to be saint.
And the spectacle of this shattered artist
on the edge of religious insanity fills the
British middle classes with pious joy. Where
it is earnest,' De Profuhdis yeads like the last
faint cry of an infidel expiring at the Chris-
tian stake. There is “a meaning and pur-
pose in it all', ” says The Daily Neivs. The
meaning is the pleasure that the sufferings
of heretics give to the holiest heart.
WHEN THE SAINT WAS HEEL
It is better to be beautiful than to be good, but
it is better to be good than to be ugly.
Twenty years of romance make a woman look
like a ruin ; but twenty years of marriage make
her something like a public building.
It is always a silly thing to give advice, but to
give good advice is.fatal.
Women have a wonderful instinct about things.
I hey discover everything except the obvious.
I never quarrel with actions, my one quarrel is
with words. That is the reason I hate vulgar
realism in literature. The man who could call a
spade a spade should be compelled to use one. It
is the only thing he is fit for.
It takes a thoroughly good woman to do a
thoroughly stupid tlaing.
There are three kinds of despots. There is the
despot who tyrannises over the body. There is
the despot who tyrannises over the soul. There
is the despot who tyrannises over soul,and body
alike. Ihe first is called the prince. The second
is called the pope. The third is called the people.
Shallow sorrows and shallow loves live on. The
loves and sorrows that are great are destroyed by
their own. plenitude.
Moderation is a fatal thing. Nothing succeeds
like excess.
In art there is no such thing as a uniyersal
truth. A truth in art is that whose contradictory
is also true.
One’s days are too brief to take the burden of
another’s sorrows on one’s shoulders. Each man
lives his own life, and pays his own price for living
it. The only pity is that one has to pay so often
for a .single fault. One has to pay over and over
again, indeed. In her dealings with man, Destiny
never closes her accounts.
Pleasure is Nature’s test, her sign of approval.
ben we are happy we are always good, but
n we are good we are not always happy.
. ne people who love only once in their lives are
really the shallow people. What they call their
loyalty and their fidelity I call either the lethargy
or custom or their lack of imagination.
Plain women are always jealous of their hus-
bands; beautiful women never "are. They never
have time. They are always so occupied in being
jealous of other people’s husbands.
A man who moralises is usually a hypocrite,
and a woman who moralises is invariably plain.
There is nothing in the whole world so unbecom-
ing to a woman as a nonconformist conscience.
And most women know it, I am glad to say.
It was a fatal day when the public discovered
that the pen is mightier than the paving-stone
and can be made as offensive as a brickbat.
Musical people are so absurdly unreason-
able. They always want one to be perfectly dumb
at the very moment one is longing to be abso-
lutely deaf.
Wicked women bother one. Good women bore
one. That is the only difference between them.
The soul is born old, but grows young. That is
the comedy of life. The body is born young, and
grows old. That is life’s tragedy.
The past is of no importance. The present is
of no importance. It is with the future that we
have to deal. For the past is what men should not
have been. The present is what men ought not
to be. The future is what artists are.
Life is simply a “mauvais quart d’heure”
made up of exquisite moments.
The two weak points in our age are its want of
principle, and its want of profile.
There are only two kinds of people who are
really fascinating—people who know absolutely
everything and people who know absolutely
nothing.
Women give to men the very gold of their
lives. Possibly ; but they want it back in such
very small change.
Define woman as a sex ?—Sphinxes without
secrets.
There is no secret of life: Life’s aim, if it has
one, is simply to be always looking for tempta-
tions. There are not nearly enough of them ; I
sometimes pass a whole day without coming across
a single one.
There are two ways of disliking art. One is to
dislike it, and the other is to like it rationally.
The Renaissance was great because it sought to
solve no social problem, and busied itself not about
such things, but suffered the individual to develop
freely, beautifully, and naturally, and so had great
and individual artists and great and individual
men.
When one is in love one begins by deceiving one-
self, and one ends by deceiving others. That is
what the world calls a romance.
I adore simple pleasures. They are the last
refuge of the complex.
There is nothing like youth. The middle-aged
are mortgaged to life. The old are in life’s lumber
room. But youth is the lord of life. Youth has
a kingdom waiting for it. Everyone is born a
king, and most people die in exile—like most
kings.
Society, civilised society at least, is never very
ready to believe anything to the detriment of
those who are both rich and fascinating. It in-
stinctively feels that manners are of more im-
portance than morals, and in its opinion the
highest respectability is of much less value than a
good chef. And, after all, it is a very poor con-
solation to be told that the man who has given
one a bad dinner or poor wine is irreproachable in
his private li/e. Even the cardinal virtues cannot
atone for half-cold entrees.
We are all in the gutter, but some of us are
looking at the stars.
Men always want to be a woman’s first love.
That is their clumsy vanity. Women have a more
subtle instinct about things. What they like is
to be a man’s last romance.
All men are married women’s property. That
is the only true definition of what married
women’s property really is.
All women become like their mothers : that is
their tragedy. No man does : that is his.
Women are a fascinating, wilful sex. Every
woman is a rebel, and usually in wild revolt
against herself.
All art is immoral.
One should absorb the colour of life, but one
should never remember its details. Details are
always vulgar.
The one charm of the past is that it is the past.
But women never know when the curtain has
fallen. They always want a sixth act, and as soon
as the interest of the play is entirely over they
propose to continue it.
What is a cynic ? A man who knows the price
of everything and the value of nothing.
What is the difference .-between literature and
journalism ? Journalism is unreadable and litera-
ture is ui*read. *
What nonsense people talk about happy mar-
riages ! A man can be happy with any woman
so long as he does not love her.
Modern morality consists in accepting the
standard of one’s age. I consider that for any
man of culture to accept the standard of his age is
a form of the grossest immorality.
Sin is a thing that writes. itself across a man’s
face. It cannot be concealed. People talk some-
times of secret vices. There are no such things.
If a wretched man has a vice, it shows itself in
the lines of his mouth, the droop of his eyelids, the
moulding of his hands even.
The only, artists I have ever known who are
personally delightful are bad artists. Good artists
exist simply on what they make, and consequently
are perfectly uninteresting in what they are.
The tragedy of old age is not that one is old,
but that one is young.
The “Peerage” is the one book a young man
about town should know thoroughly, and it is the
best thing in fiction the English have ever done.
The only difference between the saint and the
sinner is - that every saint has a past and every
sinner has a future.
Exainifiations are pure humbugs from beginning
to end. If a man is a gentleman he knows quite
enough, and if he is not a gentleman, whatever he
knows is bad for him.
The way of paradoxes is the way of truth. To
test reality, we must see it on the tight-rope.
When the verities become acrobats we can judge
them.
Nothing can cure the soul but the senses, j ust as
nothing dm cure the senses but the soul.
The aim of life is self-development. To realise
one’s nature perfectly—that is what each of us' is
here for.
Every great man nowadays has his • disciples,
and it is invariably Judas who writes the bio-
graphy.
Many a young man starts in life with a natural
gift for exaggeration, which, if matured in con-
genial and Sympathetic surroundings, or by the
imitation of the.best models, might' grow into
something really great and wonderful. But, as a
rule, he comes to nothing. He either falls into
careless habits of accuracy, or takes to frequent-
ing the society of the aged and well-informed.
Both things are equally fatal to his imagination.
Men always fall into the absurdity of endea-
vouring to develop the mind, to push it violently
forward in this direction or in that. The mind
should be receptive, a harp waiting to catch the
winds, a pool ready to be ruffled, not a bustling
busybody for ever trotting about on the pavement
looking for a new bun shop.
All bad art comes from returning to life and
nature, and elevating them into ideals. Life and
nature may sometimes be used as part of art’s
rough material, bu| before they are of any real ser-
vice to art they must be translated into artistic
conventions. The moment art surrenders its
imaginative medium it surrenders everything. As
a method realism is a complete failure, and the
two things that every artist should avoid are
modernity of form and modernity of subject-
matter.
London is too full of fogs and serious people.
Whether the fogs produce the serious people or
whether the serious people produce the fogs, I
don’t know.
3Vlan can believe the impossible, but he can
never believe the improbable.
Education is an admirable thing, but it is well
to remember from time to time that nothing that
is worth knowing can be taught.
Whenever a man does a thoroughly stupid thing
it is always from the noblest motives.
Nature hates mind.
The only difference between a caprice and a
lifelong passion is that the caprice lasts a little
longer.
Conscience and cowardice are really the same
things. Conscience is the trade name of the firm
—that is all.
I never take any notice of what common people
say, and I never interfere with what charming
people do.
You know I am not a champion of marriage.
The real drawback to marriage is that it makes
one unselfish, and unselfish people are colourless
—they lack individuality. Still there are certain
temperaments that marriage makes more complex.
They retain their egotism, and add to it many
Other egos. They are forced to have more than
one life. They become more organised, and to be
highly organised is, I should fancy, the object of
man’s existence. Besides, every experience is of
value, and whatever one may say against marriage
it is certainly an experience.
One knows so well the popular idea of health.
The English country gentleman gallops along
after a fox —the unspeakable in pursuit of the un-
eatable.
There is no such thing as a moral or an immoral
book. Books are well written or badly written—
that is all.
All art is at once surface and symbol.
Virtue is generally merely a form, of deficiency,
just as vice is an assertion of intellect.
One should never trust a woman who tells one
her real age. A woman who would tell one that,
would tell one anything. t
The world taken “ en., masse ” is a monster,
crammed with prejudices", packed with prepos-
sessions, cankered with What it calls virtues, a
Puritan, a prig. And the ait of life is the ait pf
defiance. To defy—that is wln>t we ought to live
for, instead of living, as we do, to acquiesce.
The world divides actions into three classes:
good actions, bad actions that you may do, and
bad actions that you may not do. If you stick to
the good actions you are respected by the good.
If you .stick to the bad actions that you may do,
you are respected by the bad. But if you perform
the bad actions that no one may do, then the good
and the bad set upon you, and you are lost indeed.
O. W.
THE DAY OF JUDGMENT.
Stars falling in thousands-; birds on the shoulders
of angels
Singing a mystical jargon; Negroes washt up by
the sea ; •
Arrow-heads pecking the valleys; prisms of pold
and of purple
Swarming with cherubim, children; d/ragons aU
over the.earth
Robbing the''sepulchred coffins... Ships with the
vapours of Hell
Swelling their sails and careening... Lanterns, seen
under the Ocean
Streamy, then hurriedly shrouded... Heads, of their
bodies dissevered,
Threatening each other with curses,, rolling blind
eyes in their sockets.
Mumbling the heels of the lover who gropes with his
feet on their faces*
To take to his arms a forlorn one.
Rivers of Money go streaming—straight from the
mines in the mountains
Smoking of Death and Destruction, and wreathing
icith sulphurous garlands
The harlot , as high as her bosom submerged in the
torrent...
Tigers at play by the ' light of a comet, held in the
:■ hand of a prisoner 'G gaunt
Whose gyves were forged at the stithy of Vulcan
under the earth by order of Mammon
Millions and millions of aeons of ceons ere ever the
seed of Knowledge and Evil.
Was .planted in Eden. -
N.S.W.
H. McC.
Wmtes “Beaumont S.”
Will Ashton, returned to Adelaide after several years’
study under Julius Olsson and Talmage at St. Ives,
Cornwall, and Boug-uereau, Basghet, and Schomnier at
Paris, has been exhibiting his work at his father’s
studio. He belongs to the New School, and has
exhibited in the Royal Academy and New Salon. There
is merit in his work, but not 'a dozen people in Ade-
laide are educated in an artistic sense to see it.
His exhibition has been fairly successful in sales, but (as
he says) “it’s heart-rending for a man to come up to;
you and offer a tenner for a picture that cost you about
12b to paint.”
Of Australian artists Ashton knew personally, he
says:—“ David Davies is down Cornwall way, doing good
work, for which he has a. market, There is something
particularly striking about his twilight and moonlight
effects. Phillips Fox is getting orders now for por-
trait work, and had. three pictimes in the Academy
last year. Louis Grier, who left Australia very
young, but still reckons- himself an. Australian,
came out here some years ago and held an. exhibition,
over which (so he tells me) he lost 000 b He has a peculiar
style of decorative work, and he sells-a good deal of it. A
very promising artist is Arthur Burgess, others are Pater-
son and Meldrum. I have named these few men in parti-
cular because their progress is seldom recorded, or their
names mentioned, in the press out here.”
H. Lawson supplies- a personal- paragraph :
Oh, Scotty, have you visited the Picture Gallery, and
did you see the portraits of the King and Queen and me?
The portraits made by Longstaff, and the pictures done
by Jack, of the King and Queen and Lawson and the lady
all in black ? The King is robed in Royal State, with
medals on his breast, and like the mother Queen, she is
Her Majesty is dressed.. The lady’s dressed in simple
black and sports no precious- stones, and I a suit of reach-
me-downs I bought from Davy Jones.. We ’re strangers
two to two-, and each unto the other three —I do not know
the lady and I don’t think, she knows me- We ’re strangers
to each other here, and to the other two, and they them-
selves are strangers yet if. all we hear is true. I s’pose
we’re just as satisfied as folks have ever been : the lady
would much rather be her own self than the Queen ; and,
though I’m down and precious “stiff ” and I admire King
Ned, I’d sooner just be Harry, with his follies on his head.
We four may meet together—stranger folk, have met, I
ween, than a rhymer and a monarch and. a lady and a
queen. Ned and I might talk it over on the terrace,
frank and free, with cigars, while Alexandra and the lady’s
having tea. Anyway, we’ll never quarrel while we’re
hanging on the wall—Friends ! we all have had our trou-
bles—we are human, one and all! If by chance we hang
together—hang together “on the line,” and the thing
should shock, the Godly—then, it’s- Longstaff 'a- fault, not
mine.
One begins to doubt all. scientific opinion in
seeing how learned doctors go astray over the
Australian aboriginal. A Saturday Review-e r of
Baldwin and Spencer and Howitt despairs- of the
Australian aboriginal. He was “ unable to till the
ground, bnild a nut, or even store up food against
bad seasons.” In Britain, where The Saturday
is published, it is necessary to do these things, for
reasons of soil and climate; therefore the
Briton measures the Australian by the rule
of liis own necessity, and . decides that he
falls short of British virtue. So did the
mythical Adam in the Garden of Eden. Why
till the ground where the pleasant earth brings
forth fruit abundantly ? or build huts in a sunny
clime where a shelter of bushes- suffices against
rain ? or store up food against bad: seasons that in
primitive Australia came so rarely that they came
never ? Really and truly, the primitive Australian
lived in Paradise : that is why he was- so unpro-
gressive, since progress is bred of discontent. Since
bis days were happy and long in the land, what
need to work at “living in houses and going to
offices”? These are the things done by “the
white slaves of England ” and others, because it
seems they must do. > And when a whole race
escapes from'the burden of British civilization, the
Briton shakes his wise head and commiserates—
the other fellow !

Various Verses.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Night’s Disquiet.
I will not hear her —no, I will not hear
Her voice that through the years still softly falls ;
I will not see her though she falter near.
Nor answer her, though sad through tears she calls:
My heart is shut against her, and my door
(Cold, cold the night, with wind and beating
rain) —
But though from night she .call for evermore,
I will not hear her —no, not once again.
Oh, hide those eyes, tear-dimmed; hide that
white cheek ;
Oh, hush the tremors of that pleading tone :
I will not look nor he ir, I will not speak ;
Lone as my heart, you, too, shall move alone . . .
Ah, tearful face, dear voice of long ago,
Once coming, loved and loving, stol’n away !
Why will you break my peace ? why haunt me so
Till sighs and tears and wretchedness blind night
and day ?
11. Ij.
JOHANNES C. ANDERSEN.
We Wonder.
“ We wonder,” said the children, “where it is the
gold sun goes
When the silver stars come out to play,
And the dainty moon comes gliding across the
high peaks’ snows —
Now, we wonder, would he rather stay ?”
“ We wonder,” said the children, “ when the South
wind softly stills,
Why the old folks’ faces sadder grow ;
In their eyes are always shadows when they look
across the hills—
Now, we wonder, and would like to know ?”
“We wonder,” said the children, “of what the
grown folk think,
As they sit beside the fire so late
And watch the red coals darken and the fluttering,
thin flames sink—
We wonder, O we wonder, why they wait?”
“We wonder,” said the children, “when the
night has come to stay,
If the great God sleeps so far above ;
We wonder if He listens, if He’s angry, when we
pray,
In the dark, to the little God we love ?”
“ We wonder,” said the old folk, “ if the children
ever dream
How heavy are our hearts with past regret?
They are happy by the creek’s side, where the
gold cups gleam.
0, at life, they cannot wonder yet! ”
n.s.w.
CARO.
When Night Comes.
I bate the noise of silence
In the creeping time of night,
When darkness hides my courage
And my faith away from sight.
Then memory sows the current
Of the night with snags of sin,
And memory tears the blackness,
Letting light of sorrow in.
Oh, God ! wben passeth night-time ?
Its silence fills my ears —
And a curlew’s scream is mus.c,
That scatters ghastly fears.
I hate the dreadful silence;
Its thunders bruise my soul,
When its desolating waters
On the shore of memory roll.
Heaven ! when cometh morning ?
I want the quiet day,
And the saving press of people
That drives the noise away.
Ah, Lord ! Ah, Lord ! the silence
Is fraught with ghostly din,
And Darkness stalks my threshold—
And beckons it w.tlun.
I hate the noisy silence—
Alive within my head !
How shall I face that greater
Silence of the dead ?
S.A.
SYDNIY PAETRIDGE.
The Little White Wraith.
She comes when the shadows fall,
With hope gone out of her eyes,
Waking the pain in my heart
With pitiful memories.
In the hush and the gloom of the night
I wait for the fall of her feet;
And I watch her entering in
From the dark of the rain-swept street.
Ah ! little white wandering wraith,
The dreams that we dreamt were vain ;
The years that the locust hath eaten
Can never be ours again.
And your white hands stretch for warmt.r
To a flame that has lost its fire,
And your deep eyes search my own
For a spark of a past desire.
Ah, sweet girl-wraith of the night,
The old love-fire burnt low—
Only a heap of ashes the passion of long ago;
And yet, if, somewhere, some day,
Your soul and mine should meet,
Could we kindle those ashes, I wonder,
Oh wraith of the wind-swept street ?
Q.
K. SMYTH.

WHAT IS CATARRH?
sdgh
MEDICATOR.
Catarrh is inflammation of
the lining membrane of the
nose and adjoining passages.
If this inflammation is not
arrested it invades the pas-
sages which lead from the
nose to the head, ears, throat
and lungs. It injures the
sight and hearing, destroys
ihe sense of taste and smell,
renders the breath offensive,
breaks down the affected tis-
sues, consumes the nasal car-
tileges, and rots away the
small frontal bones of the
skull. The discharge, passing
through the lungs and sto-
mach, causes dyspepsia, also
consumption. Do you want
relief and cure? If so, try
our great remedy.

A BALMAIN TRIBUTE
Many Failures, Final Success.
The Case of Mr. J, BERLIN.
(By a Special Keportcr.)
'At the bootmaking premises of Mr.
John Berlin, which are situated at
No 357 Darling-street, Balmain, a re-
porter bad an "interview of sufficient
interest to the public to justify its
appearance in these columns. Mr. Ber-
lin’s remarks were :
“For a considerable period prior to
two years ajo I was unfortunate in
having a ser.ous turn of ill health, and
now That I am well again it is my
desire to impart to other sufferers
smeh information as will enable them
to get rid of their ailments also, for
nothing is so hard as to be ill and
not know what to take to get better.''
“What was annss with you, Mr. Ber-
lin queried the writer.
“Everything, 1 think ; for I seemed
to have gone wrong in every conceiv-
able shape and lorm, and what caused
me to feel my position so keenly was
the fact that 1 could gain no benefit
from anything 1 took. The remedies J
tried were so numerous that 1 icallv
cannot remember them all, and be-ides
those things 1 drank a lot of physic
which my doctors prescribed for me.
They said my kidneys and liver were
out of order, and that i was thorough-
ly run down as well, and, goodness
knows, they were right, lor my vi-
tality was so low that 1 hadn’t
enough energy to do a stroke of work.
Many a day' i felt so weary, so ut-
terly downcast and fatigued, that 1
couid not remain at my shop any
longer, and J would knock oil : but
even then J did not better myself, as
1 could tind neither peace nor enjoy-
ment in any direction. What a differ-
ence there was in me then compared
to the time when 1 landed in Sydney
from Sweden—which was on the day
that the New South Wales troops ar-
rived from the Soudan. J was then so
hearty and vigorous that i hardly
knew" my own strength, but what a
sorry wreck 1 was a little over two
years ago. 1 was wretched with a
feeling as if something dr'eadful was
going to happen—as if 1 had done
something wrong and. was going to be
paid back in my own coin, and I took
cpiite a dislike to mixing in company.
1 felt that I always wanted to be
alone—that the world was a sham,
and that there was nothing in it to
live for ; and, true enough, there was
nothing in it for me to live for, un-
less it was to suffer pain and discom-
fort all day long. My nerves were
very bad—as weak as they could be ;
ami on the slightest provocation they
would vibrate and twitch dreadfully,
i used to sit and mope about by my-
self. thinking of all kinds of unhappv
things, and even wandering in my
mind<; and it surprised those who
knew how even-tempered I used to be
to tind that 1 became cross and ill-
grained over nothing. But that all
comes from the liver, I believe, for over
since mine was put right by Clements
Tonic 1 have been an entirely different
man.”
“What induced you to' resort to the
use of Clements 'lonic ? Was it recom-
mended to you ?”
“Yes; somebody was telling me
about it, and I thought it would not
be a bad plan to see if what was said
was true, and I soon discovered that
1 had not been misled, for by the time
1 had taken about three bottles of
Clements Tonic 1 knew instinctively
that my recovery was assured. And
when you hear that J had gono from
thirteen stone to ten stone you can
guess how delighted ] was to know
that 1 had got something at last that
would surely save me. Yes; there was
httle more than the frame of me> left,
and I was just asi feeble as I looked.
Flow could L expect to be otherwise wit!'-
the appetite 1 had 7 Why, some days
I could not eat anything at all, and
would actually sicken at the sight of j
food ; but 1 cannot say I Was any-
thing like that alter Clements Tonic
had been given a chance of showing
what it was made of. JNo, indeed , the
tables were turned in a very decided
manner in that respect, for I could
soon eat anything that was put before
me, and didn’t 1 feel easier after meals
compared to what t used to ? JNo feel-
ings in the stomach as if it were full
of wind, no cramping pains there, and
l didn't feel as if my clothes were too
tight for me, as 1 “ had done. But
those were really small matters when
placed in line with the severe head-
aches that I had to endure every day
until it was my good fortune to be
brought in touch with Clements Tonic;
and when that happened—well, the re-
lief 1 gained, was wonderful. It Was
not only relief from headaches that I
got, though, but, later on, an abso-
lute cure, and another thing Which
made me glad J stuck to Clements
Tonic lor a while was the fact that I
lost all those tits of giddiness that
used to attack me. yes, 1 used to
feel awfully strange sometimes, as if
the room was spinning round, and fdt
a brief space 1 would remembet noth-
ing. Once Clements Tonic checked
those horrible sensations they have
never emce occurred again, and I can
truthfully say the same of the smo-
thering sensations in my chesit, which
seemed to get so tight that i could
not draw my breath. It was l quite a
general thing for me to have hurtful
sensations between the shoulders and
i nght across my loins, but Clements
|'lonic did not fail me there either—
no, indeed, it brought ease to every
, Part ot my body, even to my eves,
| which had had a heavy, lumpy leeling
i m them for months, and my sight got
, dear a.s well. Upon my life, it was
like being born again, for whereas pre-
vious to taking Clements Tonic I
could never get a night's rest, after-
wards J could sleep almost as soon as
my head got settled on the pillow, and
1 h,nd none ol the old dreams to dis-
turb me as formerly. And, another
thing, Clements lonic did away with
the objectionable taste that had per-
vaded my mouth, besides removing
from the tongue all the slimy coating
that it had been covered with. Whilst
all this was going on my nervous sys-
tem was undergoing a beneficial
change. I became evenly balanced in
mind and steady in nerves, which
showed that Clements Tonic streng-
thens the brain as well as the ’body,
and that accounts for my sleeping so
well, and also for ceasing to worry
and to get irritable at trifles. Those
of my friends who had seen me in
a .-?, n -Y so Jong, and who used to give
pitiful glances at me when 1 was wasl-
ing away, were spellbound with amazo-
ment at the work Clements Tonic had
done ; and the best of it is that 1
havo remained cured ever since.”
“Have 1 your permission to print
this statement ?”
“You may publish it in any way it
pleases you, for I’ve said noi.hing but
the plain truth.”
STATUTORY DECLARATION.
I, JOHN BERLIN, of 357 Darling-
street, Balmain, in the State of New
South Wales, Commonwealth of Australia, do
solemnly and sincerely declare that I have
carefully read the annexed document, consist-
ing of five folios and consecutively numbered
from oue to five, and that it contains and
is a true and faithful account of mv ill-
ness and cure by Clements Tonic and also
contains my full permission to publish in any
way my statements—which I give voluntarily,
without receiving any payment : and I make
uhis solemn declaration conscientiously biliev-
ng the same to be true, and by virtue of the
tovisions of tbe Oaths Act, 1900.
Subscribed and declared at Balmain this
eighth day of November, one thousand nine
hundred and four, before me,
GEO. M. MARSH, J.P.

THE FINISH.
This is not a general committee meeting of a bowling club. It is the last meeting (years and years hence) of the Commonwealth Tariff Commission. Th.
Commission has at last got to the last item of the tariff, which is zymax-a malted flour that is said to be very good for aged digestions.

THURSDAY, APRIL 13, 1905.
RELIGIOUS REVIVALISM.
There is some pathos in the return to these shores
of “General” Booth, promoter of corybantic
religion and sweating philanthropy. The old
man who has failed is always pathetic, however
poor his cause, and allowing to Mr. Booth credit
for some real sincerity, some idea beyond that of
merely making money, he must realise now the
failure of his life-work. The Salvation Army still
grinds the faces of the poor, and as an institution
of sweating philanthropy, survives. But as a
religious force it is dead. Wrote Mr. John
Manson, the other day, in the course of a critical
and severely just review of the Army’s work:—
Anyone who has cared to interest himself in the open-
air work of the Army of late years cannot but have been
struck by two tilings : the increasing- respectability of
officers and soldiers, both male and female, and their
increasing inability to interest grown persons of any class
to the point of getting them to march with them to
barracks.
The London Daily News, as the result of its
recent census of church-goers, came to one inter-
esting conclusion, “the extraordinary weakness
of the Salvation Army., .its wretchedly inade-
quate total after all these years of work.” In no
borough of London did the Army total, as shown
by the News census, reach one-half per cent, of
the population. Australian experience endorses
that observation. Here, too, the blare of the
Army’s cornet dwindles, and with it the “fire”
of the meetings which once swept the weak-
minded off their feet and translated hysteria into
hallelujahs.
* * * *
It is a very good thing indeed that this should
he so, for the Salvation Army adds to the objec-
tionable features which all religious revivals have
special evils of its own—firstly, the gigantic
sweating organisation by which it gathers into its
homes the unemployed, to use their unpaid labor
in competition with the employed; and secondly,
as regards Australia, its position as a gigantic
absentee landlord drawing tribute from the indus-
try and charity of this continent for export
to Europe. But, apart from all considera-
tion of these economic features, the task
will be an interesting one to this gener-
ation of watching the final stages of the
Army, and the uprise of the next hysteria, which
is destined to WT3 its place in the domain of
revivalism. Possibly Evan Roberts, who is now
storming the emotions of the Welsh miners so
that they not only sob and howl at prayer-meet-
in "s, but also drink less beer and hew more coal
(vide press reports), is to be the next “General
Booth, and Australia will welcome him on the
top-wave of his hysteria as it welcomed Booth,
and sympathise with him on its failure, as now
there is a call to sympathise with Booth
* « * *
All religious “revivals” are true to type in their
origin, their growth, and their end. .Religion is
the means by which the aspiration of man towards
the ethic finds expression. Its natural develop-
ment is gradual and reasonable, having its begin-
mngr with the canonisation of the man who sets the
fashion against cannibalism, and its natural end,
one day, m the beatification of the man who goes
into politics and remains perfectly honest, sincere
atK j upright. Progress to be stable must be slow,
and the human race must be gradually weaned of its
egoisms, its passions and its violences. With this
noimal development, as with most other evolu-
tionary processes, there is now and again a violent
disturbance Certain impatient spirits are not
satisfied with the sum of progress or its rate, and
preach what they regard as a counsel of perfection,
lhey are in a hurry to arrive at the Golden Age,
angry at the slow growth which Nature
imposes on religion—it being no more possible
for a religion to rise superior to the general
average of the people _ professing if, than for
gum giants to grow up in a night, or sea beaches
to be made in a minute. The dissatisfied start a
revival. With the progress of civilisation
revivals have lost much of their fierceness, just
as with the ageing of the earth it is supposed that
volcanic outbreaks have lessened in number and
fury. But these religious outbreaks still remain
true to type, and even in minute details show
their common origin. When the Salvation Army
of to-day seizes on the music-hall ditties, making a
hymn of fa-ra-ra-boom-de-ay,” and captures the
slang, chanting that the Deity is a “ bosker,” the
method is precisely that of Arius, whose “revival”
drenched the Christian world in blood during the
first and second centuries. Arius, it is related
by Athanasius, captured the tunes and form
of that most licentious poet, Sotades, to set
to them his theological views for popular
hymns. Civilised nations do not get in this
day such sects as the Circumcelliones, who
went abroad with clubs to convert people to a true
knowledge of God, the alternative to con version
being death. But that is because the police are
better organised. The spirit of fanatic religion is
s £dl willing, but the flesh is not so strong. From
the Donatists of the first century down to Evan
Roberts, the revival is always intrinsically of the
same type, differing only in the method of expres-
sion due to the changes in manners made by civili-
sation. Indeed, in parts of Russia, where educa-
tion is still backward, the modern “revival” can
come close to those foul creeds which festered in
the Libyan desert in the early centuries of the
Christian era. Within the memory of the present
generation there have arisen sects in Russia be-
lieving in self-mutilation of the most disgraceful
kind as a means to salvation.
* * » *
The “revival,” in its first phases, always
appeals to the poor. It professes to have a quicker,
surer way of getting to Heaven than any of the
established religions, and, just as quack medicines
are taken by those who are ignorant and hope-
less of health, so the “revival” attracts such
folk as are so miserable in this life as to
wish for any change, and so ill-developed
in intelligence as to believe in any means of
change, however abs ird. In proportion as the
leaders of the ‘ ‘revival” are genuinely fanatical and
earnest, or artful and ambitious, the movement
spreads, and as it spreads, begins to be something
that must be feared and may be used by the selfish.
If the promoters are fanatical and not artful, people
come in from the outside to turn the movement
from its first purpose ; if, as is often the case, the
promoters have simulated rather than felt a zeal
for religious change, and have really worked upon
rather than shared feelings of fanaticism, they lead
the forces which they have collected to the
advancement of their personal ends. The move-
ment which has been radical becomes respectable,
and revolution merges into re-valuation.
* * » *
But the “revival ” must end in failure whether its
{iromoters be sincere or selfish. Supposing a re-
igious “revival” to originate with genuine passion,
it is yet impossible for it to long remain true to
its first character. Built on a foundation of
hysteria, its stability is always threatened. If
there is anything in its preaching of real value,
any advance in Church discipline and organisation
or in ethical principle that is suitable for
the time, that much is promptly adopted by
the old-established religious organisations; and
then the restof the “revival” platform isleftto the
fate which must attend anything out of step with
the slow march of the human mind to its appointed
end. The “revival ’’has then nothing left of belief
peculiar to itself, but still a large organisation of
people. That organisation is promptly seized upon,
from within or without, to serve some worldly
object. The Salvation Army, for instance, has
proved useful as a social anodyne. The Army has
proved so good a barrier against the Submerged
Tenth as to warrant the patronage of the King,
the nobility, and all the respectable classes of
Britain interested in keeping that “ tenth ’
quietly submerged. The Army, if it has
not filled empty bellies with drum-resonance,
cornet blare, and epileptic mouthings, has at
least made empty bellies to be satisfied.
None will sorrow more in England for the waning
of the Salvation Army than those landlords, ware-
house lords, and factory lords, who wouldn’t put
on a red guernsey for a thousand pounds, but
would willingly give a thousand and more pounds
that the proletariat should comfort their hungry
bellies with such a chromatic outrage, and thus
cease to think over things. It is people thinking
over things who make revolutions and reforms.
The Salvation Army did much, still does a little,
to keep the slums from thinking.
* * * *
But now someone else must take up the burden.
It is time for Evan Roberts, or some
other revivalist, to step into the shoes of
General Booth and grow great enough to
be received by the King; and then dwindle
away in the natural course of things. A
new religion can only live in the first case by its
appeal to the miserable. When it comes to be
used by the. respectable as a means to keep the
miserable quiet, it gradually loses its hold on the
miserable, and therefore its utilityy'and perishes.
“ There is a gratifying improvement in.-the social
condition of those applying for officers’ positions,”
the Salvation Army now claims. And therein
writes its epitaph.
A FEDERAL PENSION SCHEME.
That the hard-up pioneer should not be allowed
to starve is so generally admitted that practically
the only question at issue with regard to old-age
pensions is now one of ways and means. N.S.
Wales found this nut so hard to crack that,
characteristically, it shirked the job, and decided
to issue pensions without providing the means to
pay them. The result has been a deficit every
year since then; and as that deficit has been made
up by borrowing, it means that this State has
been paying pensions out of loans, and the present
generation has passed on the duty of keeping the
aged poor to a future generation, which presum-
ably will have aged poor of its own to keep. In
Victoria, where the same easy policy of drift was
adopted, affairs fell into the hands of men of another
sort, the icicle sort; and, in order to make a good
impression on Cohen, who had a bill of Victoria’s
falling due, the old people’s dole was cut down
shamelessly, and a surplus on general revenue
account was triumphantly produced. It is when
times are bad, and the old man can’t get his odd
jobs to do, that the old-age pension is most needed ;
and it is just then that it is in most danger of
being abolished or whittled away unless the means
to pay it are definitely provided by law.
* • * *
Of the Commonwealth States only N.S. Wales
and Victoria pay pensions; and a continuous
residence of 25 years in the State is one of the
qualifying conditions. This bars out probably one-
third of the most deserving persons. Just as the
flower of the old countries pushed their way out
to pioneer the coastal settlements of Australia, so
the flower of Australians have pushed their way
out West and North as pioneers of the back coun-
try. To a very great extent the Westralian gold-
fields saved the Australian situation after the
bankers and syndicate-mongers had made the
appalling mess of things in the early ’nineties; yet
the Victorian and N.S. Wales men who opened up
Coolgardie and the Murchison in those years, and
eased the strain upon Victoria, and provided
S. Australia with a market, and generally rescued
the continent, are rewarded by being barred from
even an old-age pension. If, when they had lost
their billets, they had mooched into the Melbourne
Treasury Gardens or the Sydney Domain, and
sent the “ old woman ” out to earn a living at the
washtub, the conditions of continuous residence
would have been fulfilled. This is a state of
things which it is impossible to tolerate. We are
Australians now, and the old-age pension must
come by virtue of long service, not to any State,
but to Australia.
« » * •
But how ? It has been seen already that it isn’t
either the safe or the manly thing to simply drift
into the business, and«trust to luck to pull through
somehow. Last session the Federal Parliament
appointed a commission to inquire into the matter
and say how; and a fillip has just been given
to the public interest in its work by the publica-
tion of N.S. W. Statist Coghlan’s views. Always
worth consideration, what he says on the matter
has more interest even than usual, because he has
been the principal officer administering the N. S.
Wales old-age pension law. Basing his estimate on
N.S. Wales experience, he finds that in order to pay
10s. per week to persons over 60 and otherwise
entitled, an annual £1,630,000 would be called
for; 10s. per week to those over 65, £1,476,000;
8s. per week to those over 65, £1,180,000. The
Commonwealth holds about 3,960,000 people, so
that even to pay 8s. per week to the indigent old
person of 65 would call for 6s. per head per annum
of the whole population. Mr. Cogxilan believes
that no more than this can be asked for at present,
though it would still be open to any specially
philanthropic State to supplement the Federal
dole. To provide this money he adopts the sug-
gestion of duties on tea and kerosene, to produce
£800,000, and then accepts, though not very
definitely, a suggestion that the other necessary
£428,000 be raised by a twopenny tax on wages.
* * * *
As to this suggested wage-tax — a bitterly
unjust class tax — only one thing could f be
•aid in its favor : it" might-' induce the old
person who, though desperately I) and nip, will not
accept charity, to come forward’* and claim his
pension as an annuity which he* has- personally
bought and paid for. No doubt there are men
and women who look upon the present State dole
as charity; but it is not at all likely" that 'they
would look upon a pension in any other
if a wage-tax were levied, so long as only theptbr
came forward to get the benefit. If every-'
person of 65, rich and poor, received Ins 8s.
per week, it would be different; but if' only
the poor get it, and some sort of a court has to
satisfy itself that they are poor, and the police
have to make inquiries, and the pensioner mustn’t
be seen in a pub., the wage-tax will not knock out
the charity idea, which now, it is alleged, keeps
many who deserve the dole from applying for it.
So the only argument in its favor that even looks
any good is at bottom.specious and delusive.
The money must be raised, and there is but one
guiding principle in ascertaining the proper
sources from which to raise it. What, generally
.speaking, makes the old-age pension needful?
Why can’t the man make provision for his own
old age; or why doesn’t he? Answer those
questions, and we know where to find the money,
for then we know where the money is that the old
man should have been able to put by. First, there
is the landlord. All the years of his life (speaking
of him in the bulk) the laboring man lias been
paying rent—rent for land whose value the
owner did nothing to create. The more men
rushed to a town to compete with him for his
living, the higher went land values, and the more
the landlord charged, though he poasibly never
saw the town, and never did one single stroke to
advance it. At a low estimate the average man
pays £2 12s. per year for rent of land—Is. a week
to the landlord as interest on a value created by
the community ; and Is. a week paid by a man
from his 21st year is enough, not only to buy him
ap annuity of 12s. a week at 65, but to insure to
his family an amount equal to the total paid
in, m the event of his dying before reaching 65.
And then there is the Bung trade. It isn’t, un-
fortunately, necessary to argue that many an
old-age pension would not be needed if the'beer-
pump had less work to do, or that the money
which ought to keep many an old couple in com-
fort in a cottage is keeping some section or ex-
section of the Bung business in gaudy affluence
elsewhere. At one sweep, State control of this
business would probably reduce by one-third the
number of the old-age pensions called for, and at
the same time provide most of the necessary funds
for the other two-thirds. Further, there is the
three-ball business. In the city of Sydney there
are six pawnshops in the average street, and
the average street is about 200 yards long,
and there are some streets where every fourth
office is the den of some sort or other of
inte rest-sweating harpy. Admittedly a big pro-
port on of this vast crowd of parasites live on
smoked fish and stale bread; but there are
others who wear diamonds the size of saucers,
and help on the business by taking down
the fool, through a pony or two bought with
the money made by taking down the un-
fortunate. If this unholy retail money-lending
business were taken over for the State, it is certain
that both the need for pensions would be reducel
and the means to pay them partly provided.
Finally there is the tobacco business—the smallest
perhaps among those things from which the need
of old-age pensions spring, yet commending itself
as an easily practicable means of supplying great
revenue without inflicting any hardship upon any-
body who doesn’t deserve hardship.
*** r *
If old-age pensions were necessary because people
get too much wages, then a wage tax would be
the very thing to meet the case. But it is simply
because they don’t get enough wages, and because
what they do get don’t go far enough, that the
pensions become necessary; and these things
again simply arise because there are monopolists
who trade on the people’s needs, and squeeze
them, and so live fat. It is the duty of the State
to squeeze those monopolists. Individually, the
community is at their mercy; collectively, the
community has them under control—and it’s a
fool if it doesn’t now exercise that control, instead
of taxing its poor (already over-taxed by the-
monopolist and the horde of non - producers)
through their wages, their tea, and their kerosene..
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The ILS-W. Lands Department.
Mr. Justice Owen is to be appointed a Royal
Commission to inquire into the working of the
iS.S. W. Lands Department. That augurs well.
But the range of his commission has not yet been
settled. On that depends its usefulness. It is
quite possible for a just judge, inquiring only as far
a u 6 18 a^ to do, to be forced to whitewash
a bad system and some bad workers in that sys-
tem. The country wants an inquiry, broad and
deep and wide as can be —an inquiry which will
tell the whole truth and pave the way for a com-
plete remedy. It can, with advantage, be ad-
mitted frankly by the present Government, and
by present officials of the Lands Department,
that such an inquiry would cast reflections on
very many people. There has been a bad, an
utterly bad system, and doubtless many men who
wished to be honest have been, to an extent, its
victims. There will be no call for too great harsh-
ness in their cases. Let the country have the
truth about everybody, with a preliminary recog-
nition of the fact that only guilt which is clear
and unmistakeable shall be punished. If there
has been actual corruption, the rendering of ser-
vices for direct bribes, let that be known ; and, in
all clear cases, let rigorous punishment follow.
If, too, there have been transactions that are shady,..
The Lacking Ideal,

Gem gives us sight that we may see a star,
And follow it for ever ;
Else we shall be as men that journey far
And find a fireside never.
Ideals make a race, a nation moves ,
By starry lights attended ;
Behind ber lie the royal deeds she loves,,.
Before her triumphs splendid.
A star we need—a great white star of, lyuth -
Firm fixed within our heaven,
I hat gleams of nationhood may light; Qur youth?
And dreams of glory leaven.
False lights we had—red meteorites.that flamed
In lurid skies before us ;
A blood-lust filled our veins, and; unashamed
We howled like wolves in chorus..
We watched them rise and burn, and with their
gleams
Our very thoughts turned ruddy.
We drenched white hands in blood, and in our
dreams
Our nation’s way grew bloody.
We opened our account with Fame, and wrote,
A shameful murder story—
Blood-lust was in our hearts, and in each throat .
A cry for alien glory.
We left our ploughs to plough a bloody field,
And trampled dead men under—
Dead men whose fight for right to God appealed,'.
And filled the world with wonder.
The false lights paled, the meteors passed, and lo !
The evil spell has riven ;
We sighed, as when dreams fade and morning
glow
To troubled eyes is given.

At Freedom’s breast was vital;
When, like the slain, the slayer lies in dust,.

Our birthright desolated.

Within whose heavens of over-arching blue
bhall flame a fair ideal.

Woollens.
These weeks the Tariff Commission has been
listening to evidence about Australian woollens.
Even Senator PuLSFORD would not call woollen
mills in a great wool-growing country “Polar
bear industries.” There ought to be many woollen
mills in Australia because Australia grows wool
enough to attract buyers from all over the world.
But there are not, and the expert witnesses have
given the reasons ; a 15 per cent, duty does not
give the mills a chance against the dumping of
low class goods by low-wage countries; and the
mills cannot get their high-class goods on the
market because people who buy high-class goods
are too prejudiced to deal with the country which
supplies tiieir wealth. The low incomes buy
shoddy, because it is cheap. The high incomes
buy West-of-England or Bannockburn tweed
because of its style. Sometimes they are dressed
in Australian Bannockburn tweeds, but as they
do not know it they do not feel “colonial,” and
they are happy. These two influences cause Aus-
tralians to import £8,838,678 worth of goods to
.clothe themselves. Think of it. A country grow-
ling a big surplus of raw material pays more than
£2 per head of the population to outsiders for
.clothing to cover its nakedness ! _ Freetraders
; say this is a mark of prosperity. It is good, they
.say, for a country to have bills for clothing
...coming from over the sea. Then in the,,
same way it would be good for all families
•rto have big drapers’ bills, and the house-
hold with such bills should be more pros-
perous than the one which makes its own clothing ;
; but hhe first has less money for the butcher and
' baker ; ; the second has more. Protectionists
• want to see Australia in the position of the
■ second lot. Cannot those £2’s per head, or part
. of them, be kept in the country ? And cannot
ithe Tariff Commission accept the fact of
i the amount of money going out of the
, country as reason enough for an increased duty ?
i Of course, the greater part of the eight millions
i is for cotton goods, and so forth. Until we grow
. our own cotton, we must send money away for
. such goods ; but, in the meantime, we can make
; a start with the woollens. Australia sends away
£1,377.780 for woollen goods, and £70,946 for
I blankets. It is quite within the power of the
. Australian Parliament to turn that trade into the
.Australian mills, and, by the time it is absorbed,
jperhaps the matter of cotton will be solved,
(or a light woollen material will be pro-
duced that will be better than cotton. Under
a 15 per cent, duty we use up only 3,702,1021b5.
of our own wool. Maoriland uses up 3,257,3191b5.
of wool. Surely Australia can be brought up to
a relative output with Maoriland. That would
mean a fivefold increase in the output of our
woollen factories, a fivefold increase in employ-
ment, a fivefold increase in profits, and no more
to be paid for clothing our nakedness than we have
to pay now. There is no reason at all why chair-
man Quick of the Tariff Commission should not
report to this effect. The three Freetraders in
the Commission will object, and the Freetrade
|-Prime Minister will be cross, but the com-
munity will benefit. If the Australian Parliament
has the facts as to woollens put before it plainly,
it can hardly fail to act promptly.
The Distributing Thief.
The Federal Tariff Commission, now sitting in
Melbourne, has given to the public some startling
facts as to that very frequent requirement of hu-
manity, a 411 ready-made suit. ’ Various manu-
facturers have been compelled to admit that they
turned out the ordinary suit of colonial tweed or
serge at prices ranging between 17s. 6d. and 20s.
Pressed with questions as to what price the re-
tailers obtained for these goods, they answered
with one consent, “ That is none of our business.
So far the Commission has not placed any retailer
on the stand to com ole te the information officially,
but it is already in most folks’ possession. The
17s. 6d. or 20s. suit is sold usually at prices ranging
‘from 30s. to 455. Evidence, directly corroborative
.of this elicited by the Federal Commission, has
been available in Sydney for years past. Various
woollen-mill proprietors and manufacturers there
have been supplying the serge garments worn
by the StaTe railway employees at a trifle
under 20s. a suit, and making them to order.
But every man who has bought for himself knows
well, and every woman who has bought for a man
knows better, that a suit of clothes equal to that
worn by the railway hand could not be obtained
in any usual way of purchase under 40s. How,
then, do things stand ? The cost of distribution
under absolutely Freetrade conditions, and with
all the stimulus of unrestricted competition, is
equal to the whole cost of production and manu-
facture. To display, sell, pack and deliver these
goods costs just as much as to grow the wool from
which they are made, scour, dry, card, spin,
weave it into cloth, make that cloth into gar-
ments and deliver them at the retail store. It s
a monstrous fact when you get hold of it,
and it is by no means an exceptional case.
Turn from cloth to tea, and exactly similar con-
ditions appear. Tea by auction is sold in every
Australian capital at from sd. to 12d. per lb. lea
retail costs from Is. to 2s. 6d. per lb. The retailers
get, or rather the business of retailing costs under
the “free competition” system, just as much as
planting, picking, drying, packing, shipping, and
delivering. Bread is in the same list. The price
of flour delivered as bread is far more than double
the price of flour at the mill, though the flour sold
as bread has with it a lot of cheap water. Milk
costs the consumer fully three times as much as the
dairyman get; cream four times as much. The same
startling figures might be gathered in connection
with many other commodities of general and daily
use. This matter of the cost of distribution concerns
nine-tenths, probably, of the population. Cheap
goods are worthless to a people if cheapness comes
through the cutting down of the wages rate ; but
the cheapness which would result from economical
"and scientific distribution would be as beneficent
•as that which follows, or ought to follow, an all-
round bountiful season. How long will it take to
get this truth into the general apprehension of man-
kind ? In rational and earnest co-operation is the
only hope of killing out this tax, which is like a
diabetes in our general being; and co-operation
is a word which does not smell sweet to the
average democrat. So many rogues have worn it
as a garment and used it as a trade-mark. But
if the producer and the wage-earner would but
look out understand] ngly, they could not fail to
see the world’s capitalists working out the co-
operative idea practically enough day by day.
Every trust and every combine is a co-operative
scheme, but founded and run for the spoliation of
mankind. And these, within the past quarter of
a century, have scooped up millions. _ The enemy
has got the idea, and makes for his own ends
magnificent use of it. But the great, toiling,
desponding human host regard it as. a mirage
or a closed paradise, or dabble with it. as
society folk dabble with religion. Actually, it is
the other hand and the other arm with which they
must, when they really mean business and con-
template victory, fight the Other Thief. To fight
successfully, they must organise steadfastly. The
way is clear and the end is plain. Realise how all
along the line you are used and “had ” —robbed of
your earnings. And then look at your Carnegie
chief robber, and your Pierpont Morgan, Armour
or Itpman bulging in splendor, overstriding the
world colossus-like, mainly through that co-opera-
tion which is as ready to your hands as theirs.
The Bear and The Monkey.
Russia marshalls its forces to withstand Japan
north of Mukden, and south of Harbin. The
threat of the Baltic fleet remains as the shadow of
a sword on the content which reigns at Tokio.
For the war is to continue. It is good news for
the white race—though it may seem bad news to
Russia, bleeding at home and abroad, in sore need
of time for rest and recuperation. Yet it is hard
to see that statesmen could resolve otherwise
than the Russian rulers. To secure peace from
Japan, Russia would have to make huge conces-
sions of territory. Certainly some of the terri-
tory would be that to which she has no
shadow of a just claim, and which Japan could
hold only by the right of the stronger robber.
But that would make no difference to the prestige
involved. The boundaries of the Empire would
be pushed back, and that by an Asiatic power.
Further, Russia would have to pay an indemnity,
and the Japanese demand would probably equal
an amount sufficient to set the naval power of the
Mikado in Asiatic waters at such a pitch of
strength as to secure the undisputed supremacy of
those seas. And there is no delusion in the
Russian mind as to the ultimate aim of Japan.
Russia is aware that the peace made to-day w’ould
last only so long as was needed to organise Asia for
the next blow against Europe. “Relief ” would
be bought as the Romans bought relief from the
first Gallic invaders, by handing over the means
to prepare fresh attacks. So the war is to go on.
“ Counsel of despair” many will say, but if future
operations are for a while kept to Fabian lines,
there seems no reason why Russia. should not,
even now, retrieve its position. r lhe financial
exhaustion of Japan has proceeded to the point
at which the definite assets and not merely the
general credit of the country have to be pledged
to raise money. Campaigning around Harbin
will be to Japan almost twice as difficult and
dangerous as campaigning around Mukden,
and there will be an appreciable lightening
of Russia's task in keeping its forces sup-
plied. With the coming spring, too, the
resources of Siberia will be available to the Russian
army. The whole position, viewed impartially,
and comparing a Titanic to a pigmy struggle, does
not look worse for Russia than it did for England
in S’Africa after the defeat at Tugela. Were it
possible for Russia to cure its internal disorders,
the war game ivould still be quite an “open” one.
Even as it is, to continue the war seems wiser than
to pay the price Japan would demand for peace.
The odds are heavily against Russia ; but it is
better to face even heavy odds than to endow
Japan with the means to organise an even more
formidable war.

The Mail Subsidy.
Mr. Reid has at last consented.to the Common-
wealth being bled an extra £20,000 a year to
secure a regular weekly mail service to England.
The Bulletin frankly doesn’t think the game
worth the candle, but, as a very large section of
the community does, and that section is made up
chiefly of violent opponents of Socialism, on whom
this bit of Socialism may have a soothing effect,
this paper does not altogether regret that the new
contract has been agreed upon. But let the
Commonwealth bear in mind that there is
no proof at all that the sum exacted for the mail
service is a fair frei-cotnpetition rate that,
rather, the presumption is the other way,
pointing to the fact* that we have the
victims of a general agreement among the Ship-
ping Trust in Britain. If, keeping that in mind,
Australia decides to take steps to prevent such
another “ squeeze “ in future, the lesson will be
worth its price. Australia, by practising Free-
trade in shipping, has left itself almost entirely
without ocean-going vessels. By practising Pro-
tection, on the same lines as America, it can,
within a very few years, change all that, and be
in a different position to bargain when next a
European mail subsidy comes to be talked about.

“Australian Art.”
“San Remo” writes
“ In a Matter of Art ” (B. 30/3/’O5) strikes a keynote ;
and it rings true. The great weakness of Australian Art
is its dead level of sameness. It is bush, bush, bush,
wherever a canvas is seen ; the shade of the eternal gum
tree is over it all. We’re proud of our gum,
but ’tis possible to get too much of a .good
thin". We want more life —living, breathing,
heart-appealing life. Away with your everlasting
landscapes and seascapes. Have we no life in Australia
worthy of glorifying a canvas? Our painters works
would argue that ours is a Deadman’s Land. But what
sav our poets— Kendall, Gordon, Lawson, Oqilvie ? Have
we no truly national life in Australia? What a wealth of
honest, burning, typical Australian life have our artists
to draw upon—from the golden days, the roaring
days” of the early goldfields, “the days when the world
was wide ” to the present 1 Why such a chasm in
spirit between our poets and our painters? Or have
we any painters at all ? To make breathing pic-
tures of our poets’ songs is the art we want. The
writings of Kendall, Marcus Clarke, the deeds of
Peter LALOR-the wild, free, coaching, bushranging,
drovino- days—what subjects for pictures ! Will it pay I
Realise on canvas, say, that pathetic figure, emblematical
of the last flicker of a dying race-and Australia will
answer you. What Art Gallery in Australia but would
be adorned with such a picture-- The Last of h.s Tribe. •

A Backblocks Bogey.
The agent who handles Crown lands business
would have his wing-feathers clipped if the back-
blocks person could be got to believe that the Band
Boards won’t bite. The average backblocker
would rather yoke a wild bull any day than sit at
a table strewn with pens, blue papers, and red-
tape. It is all very absurd, of course, but there is
the fact. And it is not as though the average
Land Board wore any large quantity of frill and
feathers. Most of the chairmen have been dis-
trict surveyors, and the surveyor is nearly always
a common-sensible, practical man whose training
and hard roughing-it have not predisposed him to
millinery and other forms of ostentation. And
still, afraid almost to open his mouth when the
“land board court” sits, the selector sheepishly
falls a victim to the agent who lies in wait for
him. In the interests of that agent, too, weird
tales are told of the pitfalls in the land laws, until
almost the whole backblocks population has been
got to believe that you have to be up to all sorts of
tricks to get through safely ; with the result that,
when a back blocker with more confidence in himself
than the rest —a rnulga lawyer they call him in one
part of the country —appears in person, he invari-
ably gives the current impression which the others
withhold, that he knows what they’re all after —
they want to trap him, and turn him out, or make
him pay something extra, and he isn’t going to
fall in. The reform will have to begin with the
Crown Lands Agents, who are public officials.
They are the people with whom the Crown tenant
oftenest comes in touch —they are, in fact, in
touch with the tenant all the time. It would be
a very good thing, perhaps, if the Crown Land
Agent were permitted to act as a sort of amicus
curiae when the selector’s business is before the
board. And the boards themselves ought to
break down the absurd notion that it isn’t
safe for settlers to approach them except under
the wing of solicitor or agent. That will mean an
entire reversal of the attitude of some boards,
which, so far, either because it shortens proceed-
ings sometimes, or because it adds to the import-
ance of their “court” in the township’s eyes,
like to have a lawyer or an agent in a case.
But, reversal of policy or not, the agent or lawyer
should be frozen out, since every agent or lawyer
around these board meetings (they shouldn’t be
called “courts ” at all, as though the settler were
a suspected criminal on trial) adds to the burden
of the struggler on the land.
Another Trade Scandal.
The inquiry by the Victorian Health authorities,
prompted by Premier Morgan, of Queensland,
into the “ weighting ” of Victorian leather, shows
up the exporters of that staple in just about as bad
a light as the butter-men have been seen in lately.
Briefly, the report of Govt Analyst Wilkinson
sets forth that out of 51 samples of leather from
10 Victorian tanneries 11 were impregnated with
barium chloride and glucose to the extent of from
11 to 16 per cent. Leather is at present valued at
lOd. per lb., the two adulterants named cost Id.
per lb. each, so the tanner made a dishonest
“ profit ” of £l3 Bs. 9d. on every ton of
leather he sold. The object, of course, is
to “weight” the leather, but one of its
effects is also to deteriorate it ; scientists
also say that barium chloride tends to induce
pneumonia—certainly, it is poisonous. Premier
Bent is understood to be skirmishing round for
some statute that will reach these fraudulent
adulterators, but so far without success.
“Customs” McLean has perfunctorily remarked
that it seems to be more a State than a Federal
matter, and he doesn’t see that he can do any-
thing. This is regrettable enough, but the worst
feature of the case is that pretty well every tanner
who has opened his mouth on the subject has been
eager to back-up this disgraceful fraud. They
have said : (1) It is done elsewhere ; (2) it is quite
harmless; (3) nobody is swindled, because the
buyer knows the leather is “loaded,” and gets it at
a commensurately lower rate. All which is flam.
If the buyer knows the leather is “weighted,”
and so gets it cheaper, and if there is no pecuniary
advantage to the tanner, why put the loading in
at all? A drastic, far-reaching Adulteration Act
is badly needed. It should embrace all exports
and imports, and all articles manufactured for
home consumption, and it should provide gaol,
“without the option,” for every deliberate adul-
terator.

The Tender Mercies of the Octopus.
This cryptic cable was stowed away the other day
in the newspapers :
The House of Lords, deciding on an appeal in favor of
the retail tobacco dealers, held that Ogdens, Limited,
were liable for the distribution of the promised £200,000
per annum for four years.
To the Australian retail tobacco trade the message
had, or should have, a lot of meaning; for it
powerfully illustrates the treatment which the
retailers get from a trust when it secures com-
mand. Before the British and American oetopi
had decided to go hunting together, the Imperial
(British) Tobacco Co. was formed to fight the
Americans out of the market; and, as part of
the scheme, it offered the retailers £50,000 a
year to boycott the trans-Atlantic trust. But,
large as this amount was, a branch of
the trust—Ogdens, Limited—capped it with
an offer of no less than £200,000 a year for four
years—Boo,ooo golden proofs of the profit which
the wholesale tobacco trade squeezes out of re-
tailers and public. With this bait the co. ob-
tained the signatures of 10,000 retailers, all bind-
ing themselves to the Ogden Co. But about this
time the two oetopi decided that it would be
much better business to feed in brotherly amity
upon the retailer and the public ; so they fixed up
an international trust —and the contract bearing
these 10,000 signatures was handed over as part of
its assets, and it is alleged that the Trust paid no
less than £1,500,000 for it. Ogdens then went
into liquidation ; the Trust denied all liability for
the £800,000; and the liquidator offered the re-
tailers, not their £BOO,OOO, but £lOO,OOO in full
satisfaction of all claims, Instead of accepting
it, however, they formed an association, fought
the matter right up to the Privy Council, and, as
the cable shows, won. . The president of the Re-
tail Tobacconists’ Alliance, with a knowledge of
the sort of thing that happens to the trade when a
trust gets really to work, said lately
He had met Mr. Duke (the representative of the
American octopus, who fixed up the business in England),
and admired him as a man ; but, as the head of a gigantic
trust, such men were better in heaven. T< usts were bad,
and the man who come into their midst to create a trust
was adding to the sum of their misery.
Yet, using the State-monopoly bogey, the Aus-
tralian octopus, which is first cousin to the Anglo-
American octopus, is relying mainly upon the
Australian retail trade to fight the one thing
which can deliver this country from the trust.
It is practically impossible to conceive of the
State being a party to the repudiation of
contracts such as the British tobacco people
tried; yet the octopus is being pictured to the
retailers as a benevolent institution, formed
in the interests of the retailers and the
public, while the State is represented as a
destroying Gorgon. The Privy Council repre-
sents the State; and in this very first great
contest between monopolist and retailer, the
retailer can get a good idea as to which of the
two—State or private monopoly—he will get the
fairer deal from
In re the Sims v. Browne case—which disclosed
the fact that Sims paid to land agent and ex-
M.P. Willis £6OO as a consideration to obtain the
conversion of his family’s several settlement leases
into one improvement lease—the magnitude of the
contract which Willis took on, and his faith in
his power with the Lands Department, may
be realised from the following facts : —Settlement
leases under the Act are granted only for such
lands as have been proved suitable for immediate
use as farms. And they are very properly bur-
dened with onerous conditions, such as that the
lesse shall pay an annual rent of 1-J per cent, on
the capital value of the farm ; shall pay the value
of improvements as appraised ; make the farm his
bona, fide residence during the ivhole of the lease;
fence the farm within five years ; keep it free from
rabbits and other noxious animals and noxious
weeds; and, finally, the lessee may not assign or
sub-let without the Minister s express consent.
Improvement leases, on the other hand, are only
granted, to quote the Act—
of Crown lands which, by reason of inferior quality,
heavy timber, scrub, noxious a. als, undergrowth,
marshes, swamps, &c., are not suitable for settlement,
and can only he rendered suitable by the expenditure of
large sums of money.
And practically the only onerous, condition
attached to such leases, for the rent is a neglig-
able factor, is such improvement of the land
dering the term of 28 years as will make it ulti-
mately fit for settlement —a condition which, - in
Sims’ case (since Sims’ land was already proved
to be quite suitable for settlement) would have
amounted to a dead letter. The conversion pro-
posed should be impossible under the Act, and
was only feasible by the Lands Department over-
riding the law.
“Chasselas”: Bent’s Closer Settlement Land
Purchase Board is travelling over Victoria,
drawing fees, piling up expenses, and making
recommendations that set in motion yards of
red tape, which will strangle the agriculturists
who do any settling under the conditions they
offer. The Wyunna estate was purchased at 25
per cent, above its value, ostensibly because it
was irrigable land. Yet this so-called irrigable
land is to be let out for wheat-farming under
ordinary conditions, though the climate is too dry
to allow of .anything but failure without irriga-
tion. As the Government has purchased the land
on the basis of its irrigability, why not do the
necessary grading and channeling, and cut it
into blocks for irrigation ? Becently the board
appeared at Wickliffe-road, the centre of S.W.
Vic.’s rotten tenant system. Apparently, the only
persons who knew of the visit were the local squat-
ters who looked after the board throughout its visit.
No evidence was taken from the agriculturists who
are to take over the land, though the board drove
within speaking distance of them. It is safe to
assume the land-owning crowd were not slow in
producing the outrageous renting agreements,
wrung from unfortunate, drought-stricken mallee
cockies, showing 10s. to 12s. 6d. an acre paid for
land which they themselves have valued to come
under the 3d.-an-acre land-tax. Early in the history
of the tenant system present writer pointed out
that squatters were making dupes of the tenants
to load their land before the Closer Settlement
Act became law, and the forecast has turned out
correct. The only course open to Vic. now is to
tax the squatters on the rental value they are
charging their tenants.
