Dr. J. Beattie Crozier, in the Fortnightly
Review :
The mixing of antagonistic races on the same areas and
soils is the supreme crime, the supreme treachery to a
country ; more infamous than treason in war ; and for
which the names of all engaged in it must be execrated
in after ages—as, indeed, the history of the negroes in
America, of the mixture of Spaniards and Indians in,South
America, of Greeks, Bulgarians and Turks in Hungary, and
of Jews on the Continent everywhere, has demonstrated
and painted in colossal characters black on the walls of
the world. And for the following reasons: —The first, and
the one on which the others depend, will come to most
people as a novelty, and yet to evolutionists it need only
be stated for its truth and significance to be at once seen.
It is this : that civilisation, with its fine flower of all that
is best in character, intelligence, morality, and love of truth
and justice, and which it is as much a part of national
honor with each generation to transmit untarnished and
undimmed to its children as their heritage and birthright
as the national soil itself, is not a thing which is built
into the very texture and organisation of a people,
like their appetites, their sensual loves, their love of
children, and the like, as a thing of course, whose
indefinite continuance may be as much taken for granted
(as, indeed, it is by those who look merely through the
keyhole of to-day) as the continuance of time itself. On
the contrary, it is as much an artificial product and
hothouse growth as the flowers in a conservatory, and
like them will, without the most assiduous care and
culture, speed ly relapse into the wild state again.
Unfavorable material and social conditions, like unfav-
able atmosphere and soil, will, indeed, gradually destroy
the finest qualities of fruit and flower of civilisation, but
if vou would ruin them all at a stroke, all you have to do
is,"as with the finer breeds of dogs or pigeons, to mix
antagonistic races on the same areas, and let them have a
free run of promiscuity. If this were consistently carried
out, and on a sufficiently large scale, you could set back
civilisation as much in a generation as in a hundred
years by the ordinary process of decax - . Not even inter-
marriage or promiscuitv is necessary. Mere proximity is
enough. Transplant enough Kaffirs to England to do its
menial or unskilled work, and enough Chinamen, or
Hindus to do the more refined and skilled forms
of labor, and in a generation of two not an
Englishman could be found to do a stroke of manual
work at all for love or money. England as a factor
in civilisation would be wiped out of the high
circle of nations, and its inhabitants would differ as much
from the men we know, as the “ mean w'hites ” of the
South before the War did from their go-ahead brothers of
the North, who have made the America of to-day. Now,
the reasons why a civilisation is so quickly destroyed are
mainly three. The first is psychological, where the effect
of such admixtures, especially when the races are so
radically different as the Aryan and Semitic, the Negro
and the Mongolian, is, as is well known, to eliminate
from the offspring the best points in the character of both
parents, and to bring them down to the instincts of the
barbarians from whom all civilisations alike have started ;
in the same way as crosses between the finest opposite
breeds of pigeons are sure, sooner or later, to bring a
reversion to the wild “blue rock” pigeon from which
they originally sprang. The second effect is a result of
the first, and is sociological, namely, that all the finer
products of civilisation and morality which come from the
breed or stock of the men on whom these products have
been engrafted, are, by this admixture, quite shorn away,
and the work of civilisation has all to be built up
again from the beginning, and this time from an in-
ferior stock; so that the very end for which the
idealist reformers are so zealously striving, namely, a
higher morality, is, like the song of an over-fed canary,
blasted by the means used to compass it. The third effect
of mixing antagonistic races on the same soil would be to
degrade the higher code of morality of civilised peoples
by reason of the political and social antagonisms it would
engender, by the passions it would let loose, and which, if
continued long enough, would, as seen in the lynchings of
negroes in America, gradually sink society to the ethics of
the ages of barbarism and civil war; and so again the
work of civilisation would all have to be built up afresh
from the beginning.

what barmaid does not appear p. and i. to the
man who has just squeezed her hand ?—always
serves a seductive* smile to the D.O.L. whisky
customer. She knows that the citizen who goes
nap on D.O.L. is a brainy, courteous person of
good taste—one likely to advance in the world.
Man—as The Bulletin has many times remarked
—is, in a great measure, what he drinks. If he
drinks D.C.L., he thinks and acts D.O.L., and
the girl behind the bar knows him for a wise, self-
respecting chooser of the cup that cheers.*
improved but cheaper footboard was put on the
later lot. Of the total saving of £2400 (after
allowing for the footboards) £1924 was due to
“the use of new and improved machinery, better
working methods, and a general increase of expert-
ness on the part of the men” ; and £336 was due
to the fact that they knew better how to buy
materials. In other words, in return for the com-
paratively small amount that it cost the State to
establish this business, it trained men to the work
and acquired experience so valuable that the cost
of the second lot was reduced nearly 15 per
cent. The foreign-trader doesn’t see anything
absurd in paying a premium to have his son made
expert in drawing plans, or pulling teeth, or
charging six-and-eiglitpence ; yet when the State
Eroposes to pay a premium so that its people may
econie expert in building engines, the same
foreign-trader calls it “ robbery.”

The Tivoli’s latest newcomer of note is
Miss Alice Pierce, and when you find her sand-
wiched between “Moorish Revels” and the
Whistling Coon, you begin to realise, if you
didn’t before, that the Rickards bill is a variety
bill. She gives “impressions” of several people
who are not known to this country and
one or two who are, and strikes one
as being too good an artist to waste
her time in being a mere imitator. Her
hair is hanging down her back (and past it),
and she seems young and fresh and natural enough
never to have worn it any other fashion; and
the way in which she merely introduces her
business, when she isn’t mimicing anybody,
is as charming as anything in her “turn.”
Kara, _ the juggler, would now hardly be
recognised as the same Kara whom The
Bulletin last saw just after he had finished
his long sea-trip. The warbler can warble all the
way out, and Paderewski can still keep up his 27£
hours’ practice (or thereabouts) every day ; but
during a life on the rolling wave the man who
juggles with knives and turnips has to take a spell
—and then the early performances suffer. Come-
dian George Dean is a much-applauded item in
the bill; and a mysterious kettle is billed to take
away the breath of the Man from the Land, who
will presently take a week off from ploughing and
look Sydney up at show-time.
** * #
George F. Boyle made a palpable hit
with his pianoforte recitals at the Sydney
Y.M.C.A. Hall last Saturday evening. The
large audience included many musical stu-
dents of the fair sex, who are too
apt to go into raptures over every male pianist
who comes along, especially if he be a verdant
bachelor; but their wild applause of all the young
Sydney performer’s efforts was sufficiently
leavened by that of hard-headed critics to make
the commendation of consequence. Of the origi-
nal compositions, presented, the most interesting
was the “Fantasie” trio, in which the pianist
had the assistance of Messrs. F. Mowat Carter and
Bryce Carter as violinist and ’cellist respectively.
This is a bright, melodious, and, for the most part,
well-written work, in the free form allowed by its
designation. Other solo compositions of young
Boyle for the piano show that he has the gift of
inventiveness, which he will do well to cultivate
as Well as his playing. He has already worked up
a good technique, and what he now mostly
requires is a steadfast avoidance of anything ap-
proaching “ swelled-head,” and a mellowing down
of manual force and dexterity, so that he may at-
tain intellectual expression, delicate nuances, and
tenderness of touch. At present he is at that
awkward period of development which Mark
Hambourg has almost passed through, and during
which the young performer, dazed and stupefied by
the ready acclamations of the undiscerning, is apt
to dash into mere bravura efforts on the slightest
provocation. In his interpretation of Grieg’s
Ballade (Op. 24) spurious sporzandos, betokening
false emotion, were especially noticeable. With
the perfect graduated phrasing of the famous tone
poet, Paderewski, still so vividly within recollec-
tion, there is no excuse for that sort of thing. The
lesson which the great exponent of Chopin taught
above all others was that it is never necessary to
thump the piano, and that the finest effects are
those of pianissimo.
*# * *
Life of “The Orchid,” at Her Majesty’s,
is limited to less than another fortnight. It has
been wonderfully freshened up this week, with
some pruning and several graftings in the shape of
new topical songs given by Miss Clinton,
and the Pre historic Man of Mr. Lauri. Dressed
in a wild-beastskin, with matted locks and
carrying a club, the cave-dweller announces that he
is married to Her of the Terrible Tongue, and
after his references to his home under the rocks
and his rival, Him of the Knotted Knee, and his
mate. Her of the Frightful Face, the audience
feels itself quite well acquainted with the Paleo-
lithic age. After the Orchid, the Cingalee.

Fitzgeralds’ circus will be on the old spot
in Belmore Park at Show-time, with the Jap
wrestlers, and many horses, and a newly-imported
roupe of aerial gymnasts, and a flock of elephants,
and other strange animals, who will all strive to
live up to the Fitzgerald reputation. Saturday
of this week will witness the first rush of patrons.
* * * *
Sydney Lyceum Theatre, which it was
said George Musgrove, its recent lesseee, had
been nibbling at, has been bought by Mr.
Ebenezer Vickery, M.L.C., who is reported to
have paid £24,000 for it, and proposes to use it
“ for reform purposes.” The buyer is a trustee of
Sydney Y.M.C.A., and when he got on
the platform at Yankee F. B. Smith’s
Town Hall meeting on Sunday, just after
the purchase had been announced, received as
much attention as the advertised star. Sydney
Y.M.C.A., these times, is run by Mr. J. J. Virgo,
a muscular young Australian with a big singing
voice and a love of elbow-room, who shocked the
barnacles of the association shortly after his
arrival by proposing to put The Bulletin along-
side the S.M. Herald in the reading-room.
Understood his influence has been at work in this
newest business.
* # * *
Elsewhere an artist sketches a number of
the things that will happen when the Wirths un-
fold their green tents in Sydney Exhibition
Grounds, near Redfern Station, on Saturday.
When the circus was here three months ago there
was a thrill every three minutes, and as more
thrills have since been added, the intervals have
been reduced to 2min. 45sec. Bar the price of
admission, everything else has been increased—
including Tom Colquett’s jump, Chefalo’s leap,
the menagerie, and the alligator’s appetite.
* # #
The seven ages of man looking down
from above the stage of Sydney Criterion won’t
know the old place when the lights are turned up
on Saturday night, and “The Skirt Dancer”
begins Stephenson’s season of frivolity. For the
last three months a contractor has had an unin-
terrupted run, with the stonemason, and the
upholsterer, and the decorator playing the leading
parts. Between them they have made a solid
£6OOO worth of difference to the house. The stalls
have been stretched out towards Pitt-street, so
that the bald-headed gentleman needn’t sit so close
up to the stage if he doesn’t want to; the
approaches have been improved, and the City
Council committee’s reproaches have been re-
moved. George Stephenson’s co., which has first
use of the reformed house, was seen in Sydney a
year ago; but “The Skirt Dancer” is new to
these parts.
* * * *
Sydney Palace Theatre, which hasn’t been
able to get a license lately, for one thing because
it had a couple of big electric-light boilers stowed
away underneath, just where they would boil
most people if anything happened to them, has
been struck out of the theatrical index expurga-
torius. Trustees of George Adams’ estate are
shifting the boilers, and other changes have been
made, so that Easter will probably see the decora-
tive little show-house open again.

Au revoir, but not good-bye, to Bland
Holt and Mrs. 8.H., likewise the B.H. co., who
finish their moral-show and their long Sydney
season simultaneously on Friday evening. This
week and last Albert Norman’s betting book has
been his nightly ruin,'-but Baker has been rescuing
him amidst great applause. B. Holt now goes
back to Melbourne, taking with him (judging by
the size of the houses all through the 12| months)
much golden treasure, and leaving in return plea-
sant but confused memories of vice riding trium-
phant on galloping horses through three acts, with
virtue in the saddle at the finish.

* * * *
“ J. 0.8.” takes another view :
There is no doubt that the familiar stage Irishman is
passing, and enter the new type, like the coming Mr.
Andrew Mack. The new type of Irishman on stage and
platform must, it appears, be genial and, it would seem,
genteel. But who are they who’ve brought about this
cry for the death of the Irish cock-robin ? Most certainly
not the genial Irishman, most certainly not the genuine
Irishman at all. Granted that he was largely a burlesque,
that fellow with his coat buttoned behind him, and
dinged caubeen and unfailing blackthorn. But he was
the caricature of common traits. It wasn’t Ireland that
first kicked at him ; it was the Irish section of America
and Australia. Ireland has enjoyed him for a century,
and in folk-songs and merry-makings since the days of
Sarsfield, ana the Irish in Ireland would, if let alone,
enjoy him for centuries to come, but the Yank and
’Possum Irishmen, especially such of them as have taken
to tall hats and a smirk, call him “vulgar.” Stage man-
agers will accordingly supply a genteel article in his
place, and the refining will go on till the Irishman of
comedy will have altogether ceased to be an Irishman
even in Ireland. Those who say that the stage Irishman
is not a legitimate caricature, but a libellous misrepresen-
tation, know little of the matter. He exists to-day in
Donegal, Connemara, and even in Dublin, and in Aus-
tralia wherever he has been able to maintain an Irish
quarter. His loss will be irreparable to the broad farce of
the world.
* * # #
The curtain-raising comediettas that grow
in England are mostly sickbed o’er with mawkish
sentiment; probability is sacrified to “prettiness”
and the climax defies belief. A curtain-raiser, en-
titled “ The Borrowed Uncle,” appears to be the
masterpiece of saccharine foolishness thusfar. The
name character is a hungry burglar (the same old
burglar with a sorft ’eart who has figured in fifty
previous comediettas) in search of compassion and
portable property. He has escaped from prison,
and whilst hiding in a house he hopes to burgle,
this incredible Bill Sikes learns that a spoony
pair of lovers would get married im-
mediately if a tough old uncle would
put up the cash. They discover the hidden
Sikes, and give him food instead of giving
him into custody, whereupon he swears to show
his gratitude in a substantial manner. On hearing
the name of the tough uncle, Burglar Bill remem-
bers that he is identical with an unconvicted
forger. So he blackmails uncle to the tune of
£5OO, hands the money over to the lovers, and
then walks back to prison with the warders who
have come to fetch him, like the escaped convict
in “Ihe Village Priest.” He hasn’t exactly
earned the martyr’s crown, but he has made two
young people ’appy. Therefore he is ’appy, and
the tears of the congregation are earnestly re-
quested. This sort of thing passes for pathos
with a London audience.
* *- *
The revival of Offenbach’s “ Tambour
Major,” at Melbourne Royal, strikes a note of
interrogation. Why should this antique opera-
bouffe, in which there are not more than two
catchy airs, nor any hero or heroine to speak of,
be so affectionately remembered by people whose
impressions are 20 years old ? Kindred French
operas of the comic-grand order, equally attractive
in their time, contained music more popular
than anything in “Tambour Major,” but they have
dropped out of mention whilst this heavier work
has remained in an odor of celebrity in Melbourne
and Sydney. The answer to the inevitable question
is mainly Henry Harwood and partly Johnnie
Wallace. The late Harwood played Le Due
Della Volta in the early ’Bo’s. His matter-of-fact
treatment of the part (including certain bits of
“business” which Fred Leslie had created) was
too funny for words. John Wallace appeared as
the Marquis Bambini, a frivolous, smileless idiot
ambling and sidling about the stage in attendance
on the Due, but never in the place where the half-
blind potentate expected to find him. A
droll planet revolving around an incomparably
humorous sun. What time the Due and Bambini
held the stage, the public wept tears of
laughter. They didn’t laugh at much else
in the opera. Robert Brough as the Tambour-
Major was a comparative failure ; Nellie Stewart,
as his daughter, was a minor consideration. The
chorus music came next in importance to the pair
of companion comedians, and the spectacular
effects went for something, but the success of the
opera in Australia 20 years ago radiated from the
Due-Bambini combination. The fact is recorded
in the survival of the old catch-line, “lam here,
dear Due.”
* * * *
The “Tambour Major” of last Saturday
night was a feeble show in the principal parts:
amateurish, not thoroughly rehearsed, and
strangely deficient in singing power. Both
Hanstein, the musical conductor, and M‘Nicol
Turner, who got the co. together, have been
associated in the past with brighter amateur
performances than this, although it is well staged
and thickly populated. The crowded spectacle and
the volume of chorus vocalism give some dignity
to the performance. The principals carry no
weight. In the tall, thin person of George
Majeroni the Due is a mere suggestion of a joke;
he hasn’t even the traditional Della Volta method
of taking snuff. Majeroni is at least an intelligent
actor, but in this case he under-acts an unsuitable
character, whilst the Bambini (M’Nicol Turner)
labors in his gambols and obtrudes their crude-
ness. Miss Ramsay is a natty little Stella,
who sings out of tune; Miss Ettie Carlisle
shows no marked claims to distinction; and the
voice of shapely Maud Thornton has not grown
richer since she was previously heard in public.
All the same, Maud Thornton is a vivacious
drummer-boy with sweetly harmonious limbs.
Signor Achille Rebottaro makes the most of his
limited baritone, and puts tremendous expression
into his rolling orbs, and comports himself in the
finest Achille manner; also Miss Opie and the
gigantic Con Burrow promise to improve as they
proceed. The show altogether will improve con-
siderably during a run of 11 nights, but it will not
cease to be disappointing.
** * *
At Her Majesty’s there is a silent interval
of two days between the departure of Miss
Jeffries and Julius Superbus and the forthcoming
of Andrew Mack and Co. Andrew Mack is an-
nounced as a modernised Boucicault among Irish
character actors, who has brought his own co. to
this country to illustrate the points of difference
between new Irish drama and ould Irish drama,
and do honor to the shamrock in both sorts of
narrative. He starts the tour on Saturday with
an up-to-date play of the newest green tint, en-
titled, “ Tom Moore.”
** * *
Little Tich is accounting for a persistent
rush of patronage at Melbourne Opera House,
where the programme, apart from Tich, is of a
hai-dy annual character. The little man’s merits
have been lately stated in The Bulletin. His
diminutiveness is the least remarkable character-
istic of his Tichness. The term “droll” would
fit him just as well if he were a tall comedian and
dancer doing many of the same things in the same
way. Presumably he couldn’t get boots to sup-
Eort him at an angle if he were a full-sized Tich,
ut the boots are only one novelty in his assort-
ment of tricks. The man is an artist who has
polished his little bits of “business” until they
look like brilliant inspirations.

Melbourne Princess’ is still waiting for the
return of Miss Tittell Brune in Easter week. At
the Town Hall an orchestral concert—the first of
the Peterson series—happens to-night (Thursday);
and at the Bijou a popular-priced melodrama
never fails to occur, except on Sunday. Prince’s
Court is likewise open to receive visitors every
week. Flower shows and bazaars are thrown into
the Court’s bill of fare from time to time without
prejudice to the usual fixtures. Miss Kellermann
has been a picturesque fixture for many weeks,
but her engagement is about to expire. Bonnie
Annette talks of leaving for London on the 25th —
by the Ophir, in preference to swimming across
the waste of waters.
The Wild Cat Column.
Treasurer Carruthers is flitting from town to
town, and every time he mounts a perch crows vigorously
that this year he is paying £350,000 out of revenue for a
sinking fund. Generally the report mentions “ Loud ap.
plause.’ But the business doesn’t justify either the crow
or the applause. Engines bought with borrowed money
have been going on the scrap-heap for years, bridges
have been rotting*, dredges have been turning to rust.
A sinking fund should be a set-off against that rust
and rot. But Carruthers’ sinking fund is mostly nothing
of the sort. At the end of last financial vear the N.S W
accumulated deficiency was £2,501,(190. That represents
policemen’s wages that were paid out of loans, and the
politicians’ picnics that were paid for bv a small advance
from Cohen. Parliament years ago decided that that
deficiency should be made good at the rate of £250,000 a
year, so that £250,000 of Carruthers’ sinking fund is a
statutory obligation to pay up policemen’s hack wages,
&c., which were temporarily squared floating Treasury
bills. Moreover, Treasurer Carruthers has had, for the
past nine months, £1,298,315 of land revenue, a great part
of it derived from the sale of the public estate (last year
the total amount derived from land sales, and used as
revenue, was £1,030,138); in other words, he has sold his
securities, and collared the proceeds as revenue, except
for this small amount of £350,000. If a footpad stole £lO,
and then made a virtue of handing back £3, the public
would snort at him. But in very similar circumstances
the same public rewards its politicians with “loud
applause.” Which only shows how much more virtuous
the public expects its footpads to be than its politicians.
* * * *
Next time Bully Bent charges the Federal
Government with extravagant outlay at the expense of
the States in general, and Victoria in particular, it would
be well for Treasurer Turner, or somebody, to draw atten-
tion to the manner in which the Commonwealth printing
office has been exploited by the Victorian Government.
Here is an example of money wasted on the one hand,
with official “besting” on the other, arising from the
complex conditions of Federal and local government busi-
ness in the temporary capital of Australia. In 1903 the
Commonwealth printing office set up three monotype
machines on the second floor of the main building devoted
to the Vic. Government Frinter. At the same time, or a
while later, 16 linotype machines (worth about £14,000)
were imported by the Commonwealth office, and
placed hastily in the old power-house near the Vic.
Govt, printing office aforesaid. The Commonwealth
printer started to work on the Federal rolls, but
owing to the lack of proper foundations for the heavy
machines and the unfitness of the type-metal ordered by
the State Government, a lot of extra time and money
were lost in the production of those rolls. Subsequently,
the linotypes were pulled down and re-erected on new
foundations at the cost of the Commonwealth Govern-
ment, but to the benefit of the State Government’s
property. But it is now proposed to clear all the printing
plant out of this newly foundationed building (the former
power-house), Bent having claimed it on behalf of the
Government analysts, with a view to amalgamating their
departments under one roof. The building which the
Commonwealth authorities strengthened at a cost of two
or three hundred pounds is to be handed back to the
State landlord without question of compensati on. Where,
then, are the 16 linotype machines to go ? They are to be
Installed on the third floor of Bent’s Government printing
office—a building not large enough to accommodate the two
printing departments when their work will have increased
beyond the present output. It is inevitable that wi thin a very
few years the Commonwealth plant will need to be shifted
to another pitch. Meanwhile, the State printing office is
to be strengthened, at the Commonwealth’s expense, to
bear the new burden of those 16 linotypes, and what with
alterations, cost of moving, etc., the expense will be fully
£2OOO, if not more—and all because Bent didn’t provide a
suitable fixed habitation for the Federal Government
printing office in the first instance.
So much for the matter of needless expenditure
in the fixing and shifting of printing plant. Now, as re-
gards the financial advantages that Bent gains through
having the Commonwealth plant at the service, more cr
less, of the Vic. State. Bent’s Government .is prac-
tically boss of the situation, and the. Commonwealth
machines have been used by the local office, and in the
confusion of accounts the State gets the benefit of all
doubts, and so it came about that a saving of £5OO was
effected in the State Hansard expenses for last session.
The Commonwealth pays for Bent’s “economies,” and
the Commonwealth printing machines, being worked two
shifts per day, are having the term of their natural lives
shortened by one-half. These are instances of how Bent
is able to work points on the Federal Government. One
other point is said to be worked at the cost Of his own
Government servants. From the outset it has been the
rule for the Commonwealth printing department to
allow overtime payment for the State servants employed
on the job. For a while the money was duly received by
the people for whom it was intended, but when Bent
came to the throne he seemingly decided to confiscate
the little monthly cheque for overtime. At any rate, the
State officials don’t get it. If the cheque is paid over to
the Vic. Government every month, as of yore—well, it
goes into the Treasury and stops there.
* * * *
The M.L. State Fire Insurance office has had
one laugh. The private co.’s “protested” when the
Christchurch Hospital gave a small “ line ” to the State
office ; promptly the committee told the co.’s to put their
protest in their pocket, and take notice that it would give
all its business to the State office—just to show what it
thought of these starve-’em-out tactics. So about £BOOO
worth of a first-class risk leaves the co.’s.

March was a bad month for State Treasurers.
The results of the boom harvest are nearly worn out, and
things are getting down to normal again. The Customs
revenue has slumped badly—which, however, apart from
the revenue aspect of the matter, isn’t anything to feel
sorry for. Nine months receipts for the Commonwealth
total £6,782,139 ; last year, for the same period, they
were £7,067,269 ; or, in detail—

The experience of the galvanised-iron works at
Lithgow (N.S.W.) is a whole volume on the science of
killing industries. There was for 25 years a duty of £2
per ton on this iron in N.S.W., and, as Government after
Government left it at that, Wm. Sandford thought it was
reasonably safe to assume that a £2 duty was a
settled thing; so he erected a mill. There wasn’t
much profit in it, but he was training men, and
getting to know what machinery was wanted,
and making a market; hence, as there was no mention
of altering the duty, he put up a second mill. Then
George Reid knocked out this duty under his scheme to
make everybody jolly and fat; whereupon the works at
Lithgow staggered, and the workmen could only find
occasional employment. Still Sandford struggled on,
expecting that the Federal Parliament would have ideas
for making a country prosperous, other than sending its
work away for foreigners to do; but all that the
shandygaff tariff did was to put 15s. per ton on
galvanised goods while admitting black-iron sheets
free. That knocked out the industry of making
iron sheets; instead of employing Australian labor
to make the sheets, the Lithgow works were compelled
by foreign competition to buy the product of foreign
labor and materials. Under these conditions the gal-
vanising business struggled along for a few months till
some changes were made in the railway rates ; and then
even that had to stop. The employees, however, asked
Mr. Sandford to give the business another trial, offered to
accept lower wages (these shocking working men, who
are always trying to crush industry!), and under these
conditions a little wprk is still being done. But this fact
sticks out: that no work at all would be in progress but
for that wage-reduction. In other words, workmen can’t
have high wages and a low tariff.
* * * •
N.S. Wales supplies more than one-seventh of
the world’s lead. Latest complete figures are for 1903.
World’s production for that year was 910,000 tons ; but of
that, in European and American statistics, Australia is
credited with only 93,500 tons. Belgium is down for
20,015 tons, England 30,958 tons, and Germany 141,558
tons ; and there was much Australian lead in all three.
N.S.W. alone in 1903 sent out, according to official esti-
mate, lead (metal) 92,293 tons ; ditto in concentrates
29,706 tons. Latter figures were, however, under the
mark. For the lead in concentrates the foreigner gets
all the credit, simply because Australia doesn’t get the
dirt out of its metal before exporting it, and gives that
job to the cuter foreigner. Last year’s N.S.W. output of
lead was 106,038 tons in metal and 59,507 tons in concen-
trates ; and the world’s total for the year was awhile
back estimated at 945,000 tons.

The expenses shown do not include debenture interest; and
the depreciation shown is the amount charged to profit and
loss, irrespective of the special writings-off. Most of the
assets are lumped, including a goodwill item of unknown
size. The position, as compared with that at the end of
the previous year, no doubt shows some improvement.
Gross profits (net sales) are better, though by no means
up to the old level, and expenses, which had been creep-
ing up steadily, have turned. On the other hand, with-
out any reduction of the lumped item previously referred
to, which includes freeholds and leaseholds, rents have
fallen seriously. The unusually heavy charges for depre-
ciation are probably the result, to some extent, of a
change of management; hut for this the profits would
show up better. The return on capital and reserves was
£4 11s. 9d. per cent. ; on capital alone, £5 3s. 3d. per
cent. ; but this is computed before the £2750 is written
off. Obviously, on these figures, 5 per cent, cannot be
paid with any safety.
• * • *
“C.H.” has an idea:—Australia is now almost
the only opal-producing country in the world. Most of
the gem goes away to be cut. One town in Germany has
grown from hundreds to thousands simply through
cutting and handling opal—almost all Australian opal.
Why not have that town in Australia ? Put a 100 per
cent, (or 10,000 per cent., if necessary) export duty on
uncut opal, and Australia will-must—get that work;
the German opal-cutters would come here. An entirely
new industry, which, by the way, would make a very
crood start for the Federal capital.

• * * *
Queensland Stock Exchange tipsters are still
saying that there isn’t any reason for the general drop in
N.Q. share values. It’s time this old gag was pensioned.
For the first quarter of 1904 the Charters Towers dividends
amounted to £102,992 ; for the same period of 1905 they
amounted to £52,957. That’s surely reason enough.
Figures include liquidation dividends too ; but they don’t
amount to much.
• • • •
Queensland gold returns for first quarter show
a continued set-back. Compared with 1904 the yield
was 133,9220 z. as against 145,0270 z., adecrease of 11,1050 z.
Two or three years back the northern State talked of a
million ounces per annum; but Mines Department’s
decision to record on a fine-gold basis soon shattered the
hope, for much of the Northern gold is very poor. Queens-
land will have to keep going in order to put up half-a-
million ounces during 1905. March figures show Charters
Towers and Gympie, the largest producers, both on the
down grade, the decreases being 54720 z. and 96Soz. respec-
tively. The Hodgkinson, the Jordan, and the Coen, all
minor fields, but capable of serviceable yields, have
dropped back to the tune of 12330 z. On the other hand,
Croydon has gone ahead 5210 z., Mt. Morgan 17010 z., and
Ravenswood 8170 z., while Clermont, Gladstone, Herber-
ton, and the Etheridge show small increases, aggregating
500 oz. Good to notice this improvement at Mt. Morgan.
Some of it can be attributed to the Dee River rush, which
good authority calculated had been responsible for well
over 6000 oz. up to end of 1904. Wardens’ returns in
Queensland are carefully compiled as a rule, but they
rarely fail to miss something, and there is good reason to
believe that the Dee River figures were under-estimated.
m * * *
Stated in some quarters that real reason North
Queensland miners will not sell wolfram at present is that
they cannot mine it to sell. Quite true that in one or two
isolated cases the ore is not being obtained with the old
regularity, and the ring cannot of course be blamed for
this, but certain it is that, in a relative sense, tungstic
minerals abound in Queensland, and are at present being
mined “ all over the State,” or nearly so. In 1903 there
were sold 197 tons for £7870. Figures were small, for the
miner received approximately only £4O per ton. But
during last year 1539 tons realised £161,635, approxi-
mately £lO5 per ton. With the metal at the latter
figure quite an industry became possible.
Stannary Hills is keeping its end up better than
any other of the N.Q. concerns into which down-south
speculators have thrown their money. To the 20-head mill,
which so far has been mostly a vexation, a 10-head is now
added (it starts this week), and when the combined
concern is at work it is expected that some-
thing like a monthly 85 or 90 tons of tin-oxide,
worth at current prices about £B2 10s. per ton, will be
turned out—while ore of the present grade lasts. _ There
is enough ore of some grade to keep the mill going for
three or four years. Costs will be close up to £4OOO a
month, leaving a probable profit of £3OOO _ per
month, which would be very good business
for a co. with a decent capital and no heavy debts. The
co. at last statement, however, owed on mortgage deben-
tures and otherwise over £BO,OOO ; and the issued capital
is £441,750. Shareholders are still far off from dividends ;
expenses are very high, estimates up that way have
a bad habit of landing co.’s in the soup, and tin-veins are
mostly as erratic as the tin-market; yet prospects are
much less hopeless than they were. F. G. Gipps, who for
some years battled to make a “do” of the Euriowie tin-
field (far-west N.S.W.), is now in charge.
* * * *
Success of an Italian working-man party at
Kalgoorlie’s latest marvel, the Hidden Secret, recalls
that it was a party of miserably poor Italian charcoal-
burners who found the Australian mainland’s most per-
manent tin-mine—the Vulcan, Irvinebank (N.Q.). They
worked it in 1889-1890, got out about £2OOO worth of tin, and
when offered £2200 for the claim right out jumped at the
money. Since then it has given 67,060 tons of ore, from
which 7055 tons of black tin have been won, worth £354,210,
of which £90,163 has been distributed in di vs. ; and it
seems good for as much more, although its bottom
workings are now among the deepest for tin-mining in the
world, outside Cornwall.
* * *
Mt. Tarrangower, Maldon (Vic.), had a record
as a producer in the “good old days,” but even in its
prime reports were not more emphatic than they are now
about the show that is delving for the lost shoot in the
heart of the mount. One report tells of “splendid
gold;” another says—“ Stone showing gold ; 54ft. of reef
exposed. No hanging-wall yet. All highly payable
stone.” It is very commendable of the management to
let the world know that a little Golconda is having birth.
But a cold public may want to see a bit more of this in-
teresting stranger from Maldon before it takes it into its
lap. Fond parents often think their offspring the best in
creation, when to other eyes they are very ordinary little
cusses. With s£ft. of highly payable stone available, it
ought soon to be possible to prove that little “ Tarry ” is
of the true metal.
* * * *
South German, once a Maldon giant, on its
reports doesn’t look like being worth much more than the
value of its plant. Solid quartz, great formations, no
gold. Yet shares are nibbled at day in and day out. Can
someone tell the secret ?
* * * *
Lord Nelson, St. Arnaud, has done things the
wrong way by the fashion in which it has sought to acquire
the Lord Nelson North ground. To purchase would have
been better, or to have let alone still wiser. If it doesn’t
get the ground, it will still have shown that it is of some
value, and that is just what the holders wanted some-
body to prove. It looks like the South German and the
South German Deep over again. What sport it will be to
outsiders to watch the little game that has now to be
played 1 Lord Nelson, if it goes into the fight, is not
likelv to let its neighbor shepherd, assuming that that
neighbor wants to hold its Naboth’s vineyard at as small
a cost to itself as possible. John A. Wallace spent a
fortune over the North, but never got decent gold in the
upper levels. When his shares were sacrificed, the
Nelson might have got hold of the tsorth leases if it
had seriously tried. Somehow', though, its people never
seemed to see any risk of the shoot of stone dipping out
of their own lease.
t * * *
Berry Extended, Creswick, re-enters the list
of payable co.’s with a div. of 6d. The bottoms and the
battery helped towards this result, as did the gold won
under the tribute arrangement with the Spring Hill and
Central Leads adjoining. It is a Ballarat way to pay
divs. on the least provocation, and there is good ground
for saying that with so big and so costly a mine as the
Extended, another month's work ought to have been
done before profits were disbursed. A patch of good
wash bored into or cut up by cross-drives may seem a
“ bank,” but the gold isn’t always there. That is the
reason why Pinder’s policy at the Duke United of having
two banks—a solid cash credit and a bit on the mine—
meant good mining.
* • * •
The Bull-coveted Cassilis, Gippsland, is living
down its reputation of being a flash-in-the-pan property,
from which divs. are always wanted, but only occasionally
got. To Coote, general manager, is given the credit of
getting things ship-shape. With sft. of high-grade stone
just cut 400 ft. below the main tunnel level, it looks as if
the lode lives down in size, despite great tightness of
country. Ore occurs in short lenses, and it needs con-
stant pegging away to build up reserves. Coote saw this,
and has been"keeping picks on dead work north and south
till he could be safe to run the mine for all it was worth.
Ore is very refractory, and costs are high, but the grade
is about 17dwt. Mine is well equipped with battery,
wasters, chlorination and cyanide plants. Most important
job in hand is the testing of the lodes parallel to the
Cassilis and New reefs, and the proving of the Cassilis
reef where cut at bottom level.
• • • •
New Hopeful is a little show adjoining New
Argus, Bendigo. Its present vogue arises from the fact
that the shoot of gold from which New Argus pays its
divs. has been cut in continuing into it the drive at
1780 ft. As the property is not a big one, gambling is
going on "over the chance of the shoot extending through
it to the Specimen Hill beyond.

12,672J0z. ; average, OJdwt. Divs. totalled £6BOO, of

£lBOO ; Victoria United, £lOOO ; North Woah Hoap, £6OO ;
and Llanberris, £4OO. Figures are practically identical

# * * •
Monazite exists at Pinch Creek, near Bonang
(Vic.), and is found in a rough wash set in tight blue clay.
Run of dirt is 14ft. wide and a mile and a half in length.
As difficulty exists in separating the mineral from the
sand and titanic iron, there isn’t much chance of cash
being made out of the deposit.
# * * •
One by-product of the long labor troubles in
Gippsland is the Strzlecki colliery, which has started
work on a co-operative basis. It has got hold of a railway
contract to begin with.
* * * *
From Mt. William (Vic.), which had a boom
once, not so long ago:—“Our first few months were
lively. Preston and Starlight ‘ stoushed ’to an audience
of about 1200 one Saturday night; following Sunday
morning Church of England parson preached from the
same ring to a 200-house. But nearly five years have
passed, and now about 100 men remain—to scrape out a
few dwts. from the crevices in the rocks in summer, and
do a bit of ground-sluicing in winter when water can be
had ; and two pubs and a shanty do all the liquor trade.”
* * * *
“Baker’s Creek, sellers lOd.” What is the
matter? The mine is certainly worth much more than
that. The developments at 1500 ft. haven’t been made
the most of, and there is no apparent reason why
1600 ft. should be a blank. The office is, unfortu-
nately, as far away as Adelaide, and there seems to be a
great lock of sympathy between the directors and the big
body of Armidale and Hillgrove shareholders, who still
have much faith in the mine that has paid £275,000 in
divs. It was significant that, a while ago, when shares
had their first bad slump, practically every local miner
who could possibly manage it bought shares, if he didn’t
hold some already.
* * * *
Broken Hill is now employing more men at
mines and local works than it did during even the
£l7-a-ton lead boom of 1889-1900. Latter year there
were 7010 on the sheets ; now, 7259, an increase of just
50 per cent, on the number in 1902. Besides the 7259
there are 2200 at the smelting works, etc., in Australia,
not to mention the 5000 or 6000 at the foreign works,
Australia not having reached the stage when it gets all
the dirt out of all its own ore.
* * * »
A special-train load of sharebrokers has been
at the Broken Hill mines, as guests of Anglo-Austra-
lian Lionel Robinson. Result has been a move in some
Barrier stocks, notably Junction North and Block 14,
which probably deserved the rise. Junction North is in
difficulties now with the Australian Metal Co., which
supplied the magnetic plant; co. wants to be paid £2OOO
odd, and the co. wants to see the plant successful first.
Local confidence in the plant is still pronounced-
just as pronounced as is its want of confidence in the con-
trol of the co. Ultimate success of the machinery is still,
however, uncertain. Block 14 has made the very needful
financial arrangements. Mine is now unwatered to 500 ft. ;
but it will take a lot of money to equip it. British has
been doing excellent work, and has a very solid credit
balance—running, it is understood, to over £60,000.
* * * *
Apropos the stupid rush (possibly part of a
market trick) to condemn the new magnetic metal-saving
plant at Junction North, and the consequent knock-back
of shares from 12s. 6d. to 6s. inside a month. The
Sulphide Corporation has been successfully operating a
magnetic plant for about three years. The adjustment
of that plant covered more than nine months. Eighteen
months ago it was decided to duplicate the plant, and an
order was sent—to protected Germany, of course. It
arrived, practically a counterpart of the section already
working, late in December ; and yet, about 10 weeks
afterwards, the engineer who came out with this duplicate
was still at work upon it, adjusting the fine parts.
* * * *
Latest Wyalong (N.S.W.) crushings—Golden
Fleece, 17 tons for 9oz. gold per ton ; Union, 40 tons for
80oz. gold. Last monthly escort took away 4500 z. ; but
very little importance is attached to escort returns, as
the bulk of the best ore is sent to outside works for treat-
ment, and parcels of gold go by private escort. J. Goggin
has gone back to the Barrier mine as manager.
• • * *
Great Boulder Prop., Kalgoorlie, which has
been looking rather sick at its lowest, or 1900 ft. level, has
had a stroke of luck. In cutting a chamber at 1930 ft.
for an ore bin, a lode has been met 25ft. west of shaft,
10ft. wide, worth lldwt. At 1900 ft. lode was split and
poor, panning-out as follows : At 22ft. west, Ift., 24dwt. ;
at 26ft., Ift., 9dwt. ; at 33ft., 2ft. 6in., sdwt.; at 70ft.,
lft., 20dwt. Although this discovery was made middle of
Sept., management has been so slow that, during the inter-
vening six months, little has been done to test the ground.
Something seems to be radically wrong when it takes six
months to confirm results of boreholes which knock
market endways. Every shilling rise or fall in Boulders
means a loss or gain of' £87,500 to somebody, and, such
being the case, management should be particularly care-
ful and enterprising.
Chaffers’, Kalgoorlie, reconstruction was unsatis-
factory. Thousands of shares held by Australians have
been forfeited because insufficient time was allowed to
join reconstruction scheme. Holders, who interviewed
their brokers, were told that it would cost them £2 10s.
to get their names on register, in addition to cost of cable.
Under circumstances shares were forfeited wholesale.
Associated, Kalgoorlie, seems to have been
all along driving above continuation of rich Oroya-Brown
Hill shoot. A bore put down below No. 10 level has cut
ore lift, wide worth 44dwt. If developments should con-
firm this, prospects of co., which have hitherto been far
from rosy, will be quite altered.
Brown Hill Extended, Kalgoorlie, has put
through a series of trial crushings of ore won during
developments. Including tailings, results pan out as
follows :—126 tons, 12Mwt. ; 112 tons, lOdwt. ; 224 tons,
16dwt. As things go* now', result is quite equal to that
from most W.A. mines, but public is not allowed to know
if the tests are a fair sample of lodes, or, in fact, anything
about the show'. It seems ridiculous to publish results of
test crushings w'ithout specifying where ore comes from.
* * * «
Victory United, Cuddingwara (W.A.j, still
looks a very lame duck. According to temporary
manager Lindley, reef to north of shaft is no good,
while to south it is split up into mere stringers of Cin. to
lOjn. wide. Ladders are broken and unsafe, winding-
engine broken down, and everything in a state of chaos.'
If Lindley’s report is correct, mine useless to
the co., and concern should be wound up.
Hidden Secret, Kalgoorlie, has treated a further
44 tons for 7980 z. gold and 3808 oz. silver. (Total since
October, 342 tons for 62650 z. gold, and 31,3100 z. silver,
worth £28,200.) Fosson, who held 110 shares, has sold
out his holding for £5OOO, practically £45 10s. a-piece,
and goes back to Italy with £SOOO, including his divs.
Buyers of Fosson’s shares are chiefly his mates, Mayman
and Gianini, but Fraser, M.H.R., figures as a purchaser,
though it may be on somebody else’s account. The bulk
of the richest ore down to 150 ft. has now been rooted out,
but until more development is done at 200 ft. it is impos-
sible to say how deep the ore shoot extends. Williamstown
town site is situated right on the gold belt, the boundary
being within 70 yards of main shaft. Had the boundary
been 70 yards further east there would have been no
Hidden Secret mine. Only two years ago residents of
Williamstown clamored for a school site just north of
Hidden Secret shaft, and, when owners protested, they
were told they were only wasting their time prospecting in
such an unlikely place. One or two shows in vicinity,
notably Fair Play, which is treating 2oz. ore, are doing
well, but none on direct run of Secret lode.

Sulphides need to be efficiently concentrated to
pay, and the Card Concentrator, with its auxiliary
attachment, is the nearest thing to perfection. Air com-
pressing is usually costly, but with Rand Compressors is a
cinch. See bottom of back cover. N. Guthridge, Ltd.
Sydney and Melbourne.*
* * * *
The McDermott Sizer now on the market fa
rapidly coming into favor with mining men. Useful as
this machine is, there is no intention of claiming that by
the use of it, it becomes possible to save by concentration
all of the mineral values in ore. Some loss is bound to
occur, no matter what mechanical methods are employed,
but what is claimed is that by the use of the Sizer, the
pulp can be prepared in a manner more suitable and in
such a way as to materially increase the efficiency of the
concentrating plant. Nor must it be conjectured that
the McDermott Sizer is only for use in conjunction with
Wilfley Tables; although it acts admirably with those
machines, it is of such general application as to enable it
to be used with equal advantage with whatever type of
dressing machine happens to be employed. Those wishing
for more information in regard to this useful machine can
obtain catalogues and full particulars from the Austral
Otis Engineering Company, of South Melbourne, who
are the Agents and Sole Manufacturers of the machine.*
The Bankers’ Magazine (London) September,
1904 :—“The Colonial Mutual Life Assurance Society
Limited, was founded thirty-one years ago, with head
quarters in the capital of Victoria, and the amount of its
business and of its accumulated funds far exceed those of
any life office established in England since the passing of
the Life Assurance Companies Act of 1870. The rapidity
in the development of this Australian society attests the
energy and organising powers of its administrators.*

The new electric light installation of the Mel-
bourne Exhibition Cycle Track, formed a great attraction
of the recent “Austral” meeting. The main feature of
the 83'stem is the “Ganz” brilliant arc lamp, forty of
which are distributed round the track, suspended on iron
brackets. The light is absolutely steady, _ soft and
diffused, doing away entirely with the injurious glare
common with other lamps and overcoming the objection-
able shadows in and under the globe. Special globes are
used which, while diffusing the light, do not cut off an
undue quantity and the effect is a light known
scientifically to be the best for all illuminating purposes.
The candle power is more than four times that furnished
by other lamps at the same current consumption. Electric
lighting by the “ Ganz ” arc lamp is cheaper than any
other form of illuminant, both in first cost, in fixing and
in maintenance. The contractors for this installation
were the Austral Otis Engineering Co., Ltd., corner
Hannah and Kavanagh streets, South Melbourne, Sole
Agents for Ganz and Co., Budapest, Hungary, who will
gladly furnish estimates and expert advice upon similar
or any electrical schemes.*
• * * *
The strength, lightness, durability and low cost
of spiral rivited pipe have given it a place of superiority
above any other pipe. This pipe possesses enormous
bursting strength, and is protected, inside and out, with a
perfect coating manufactured from asphalt. Asphalt
coated steel pipes have lasted from 25 to 40 years without
any sign of decay, and may last as long again. Spiral
pipe is manufactured by Mephan Ferguson, at Wanganui,
New Zealand, and at his engineering and pipe works,
Melbourne, where the latest up-to-date machinery is also
used for making boilers, fluming, refrigerators, bolts, nuts,
and every discription of cast or wrought iron work.*

Oil Engines.—lnformation has just reached
us that the Government Award (Gold Medal) in this class,
at the present Capetown Exhibition, has been' awarded to
the “ Hornsby ” Oil Engine. The success of this engine is
perhaps the most remarkable in engineering history.
There is no engineering speciality which can claim, as
the “ Hornsby ” oil engine does, to have obtained every
highest award in the world—viz., England, America,
Australian, France and Russia, &c.—and last year it
secured the British Government award of £lOOO. It is
made in various types of fixed, portable, traction and
marine, and in the latter it is made in suitable sizes for
small pleasure craft, and in gradations of h.p. up to 500
b.h.p., as supplied to the British Navy. R. Hornsby and
Sons, Ltd., Sydney and Melbourne.*
* * * *
The Babcock Boiler seems to be favored by
mining engineers, over 50 mines in Australia using these
boilers. Mount Morgan has 20, Waihi Gold Mining Co.
24, Mount Lyell 12, Tasmanian Gold Mining Company 7,
and at Broken Hill there are 30 distributed amongst the
mines, water works, and electric supply, while in
Western Australia they are looked upon as a necessity,
there being now 68 in the principal mines and Kalgoorlie
Water Supply. It will be noted that coal is expensive at
Broken Hill, and wood fuel is expensive at W.A. These
figures speak for themselves. Evidently the ease of
transportation, the economy in fuel and maintenance and
the adaptability to any class of fuel, that are such con-
spicuous points in the Babcock Boiler, are much
appreciated in Australian mines. * *

Bellevue, Mt. Sir Samuel (W.A.), is not panning
out anything like predicted. When new 40-head mill
was ordered shareholders were told that divs. would come
immediately it got to work. Now, however, 4000 tons-of
ore only produced about £5OOO of gold, and there is prac-
tically no profit. There are over 100,000 tons of tailings,
estimated to contain sdwt. gold and 2 per cent, copper,
accumulated on mine, but difficulty is how to treat them
and make it pay. A chlorination plant was to do the trick,
but, though three months have elapsed since plant was
announced to start, nothing has been heard of it. Share-
holders were told that copper would pay cost of treat-
ment, and gold would be all profit. 8., M. and Co. evi-
dently don’t believe this, or shares, with a prospective
profit of 55., would not be selling at 6s. to 7s.

Sydney Mayor Allen Taylor intends treat-
ing 1000 Sydney poor to dinner on Easter Sunday,
but those two Christian gentlemen, Canon Boyce
and Mr. Hugh Dixson, representing the British
Empire League, waited on him recently with the
request that the feed be postponed till Empire
Day, May 24. Does Canon Boyce also then
reckon Empire Day of more importance than
Resurrection Day ?

Concerning the Melbourne Literature
Society, by one of its members :
The society is headed by Professor Tucker, of the Mel-
bourne University, and incidentally suffers from the
patronage of Chief Justice John Madden. (What does a
L.S. want with a patron ?) Mr. Henry Gyles Turner,
“ Rolf Boldrewood,” and Mr. E. Wilson Dodds are mem-
bers. It has nice, laudable objects, but doesn’t promote
them worth a cent. The trouble is too much mutual
admiration. F’rinstance, Mr. E. Wilson Dodds read a
paper on “ English Ballads of Battle ” (not Bottle) at a
recent meeting, and finished up by reciting “ Temeraire ”
in sixteen canary-quavers and a dress suit. Mr. Henry
Gyles Turner—our “ worthy president ” of last year—then
proceeded to butter E.W.D. in an outrageous fashion.
Mr. A. W. Brazier, M.A., followed in much the same
fashion ; and the only breeze came from a lady member,
who declared that war was rot, and poems’ of Blood
blasphemy. Now at some future meeting Mr. Henry
Gyles Turner will read a paper on the “ Vedic Hymns,”
or thereabouts, and Mr. E. Wilson Dodds will pass the
butter. At yet another meeting Mr. A. W. Brazier, M.A.,
will read a paper, and Mr. H. G. Turner will wield the
churn. Messrs. Turner and Amos Brazier and Wilson
Dodds are all good chaps in their way [I hope it is not
sedition to speak of Mr. Henry Gyles T. as a “ chap ”];
but, if you please, a literary society should not be abutter
factory. If we had less august patronage, and less dress
suit, and less M.A., and more plain thinking and more
plain speaking, our society would cut more ice.

Adelaide Advertiser of l/4/’O5 contained
this advt. :
TAT ANTED, clerk for warehouse; salary, £l3O per

office.
Result—a tremendous budget of applications.
Close ifispeetion of “address” revealed the stupid

The Adelaide Hospital Board has sensibly
decided that its nurses shall be instructed in
scientific cookery. Cookery should be made a com-
pulsory part of every nurse’s curriculum. Some
nurses are sent out who cannot even make beef-tea
and barley-water, much less cook a bit of fish, a
basin of pap, or any of the dishes needful to ailing or
convalescent people. And nursing, to be success-
ful, depends more on good cooking than good
dosing.

A bit of “Dooley,” which is real truth •
If Uncle Sam begins to lick wife-beaters, before he’s
been at it long he won’t have anny time f’r annvthingbut
th’ whipping-post. He’ll be in his shirt sleeves all day
long, s ashing away at counterfeiters, illicit distillers,
postal thieves an’ dimmycrats. No, Hinnissy, there ain’t
a hair’s diff’rence between a blackguard who beats his
wife an’ a Governmint that beats his childer. Ye can’t
cure corp’ral punishment be makin’ the Governmint th’
biggest kind of corp’ral punisher. Ye can’t inflict corp’ral
punishment onless ye’re stronger thin th’ fellow ye
punish, an’ if ye ar-re stronger, ye ought to be ashamed
iv ye’self !
“ Spare th’ rod an’ spile th’ child,” said Mr. Hennessv.
“ Yes,” said Mr. Dooley, “ but don’t spare the rod, an’
ye spile th’ rod, th’ child, an’ th’ child’s father.”

There’s always one woman waiting round
the corner to give another woman away. Bulle-
tin has received this, signed in a female hand :
“Woman’s Letter” correspondent of 30th March
must surely be a masculine person. The number of
feminines afflicted with bow-legs, knock-knees, and
pigeon-toes explains the vogue of skirts. One of
Melbourne’s smart dressers appeared at a physical
culture class in knickers and low-heeled shoes. A
champion fat hog could have escaped easily between
her knees and ankles. This lady has always advo-
cated very long skirts as being “most graceful and
modest.” The average feminine simply can’t wear
“knickerbockers resembling a boy’s.” Horses would
bolt at the resulting weird sights, and dogs howl their
hearts out at the dreadful spectacle.

There is some talk of a Government in-
quiry into the management of Melbourne Benevo-
lent Asylum. The pre-
paration of meals at the
asylum is alleged to be
so shockingly unbenevo-
lent that sometimes a
mouse gets boiled along
with a cabbage, and the
presence of snails among
the vegetables is said to
be common; the beef
served to sick boarders
in the hospital wards is
occasionally “high,” and
if perchance the medical
officers prescribes stimu-
lants for some of the
poor old souls, the pre-
scription is not put up.
Needy cases are turned
away from the asylum
when there is room to
spare for them. One
part of the building,
where a kind-hearted
nurse has charge, is
known as “ Happy Val-
ley,” to distinguish it
from other parts where
unhappiness prevails.
All these things were
stated at a recent meet-
ing of the Political Labor
Council, and they didn’t
cause any astonishment. Melbourne Benevolent
Asylum has been ill-spoken of many a time, but thisis
the first time the truthfulness of the allegations has
seemed likely to be tested. It is only fair to say
that the management denies all the allegation,
especially the possibility of vermin delicacies
being served with the vegetables.

* * • *
A woman doctor, who learned her business
in Adelaide, but is now dispensing pills at Delhi,
India, writes to her S. Australian friends about
some effects of the Jappo-Bear war. Every time
the Monkey scores, the native anti-British propa-
gandist bangs his drum with vigor, and tells the
crowd that that's what the Asiatic can do.
Already, she says, there is a strong undercurrent
running towards Hindu self-government—any-
way, against British bureaucracy. Most of the
natives don’t know yet precisely what they want;
they are only beginning to know that they don’t
want the British boss.

HE: “ Out of sorts'!" SHE: “No; why?”
HE : “ You look ‘ ruffled .’”
“ One is always in Sydney when they are
tearing up the pavements,” says Mr. Justice
Barton, giving a humorist twist to the statement
of fact. True, and the worst of it is that no
attempt is made to replace the flags in their
original condition. Private companies have been
permitted to make Sydney’s sidewalks the most
disgraceful on this continent by their cheap and
inefficient methods of relaying pavements torn up
for their purposes.

* ♦ • *
The governors of S.A. Art Gallery, Mu-
seum, and Library have now definitely decided
that the Morgan Thomas bequest (about £65,000)
cannot, by the wording of the will, be used to
benefit the country institutes; also, that it shall
not be spent on buildings. Half of the whole sum
is to be devoted to the Public Library, which
ought to be altered out of all knowledge, as £30,000
would doubtless buy the present collection of 50,000
books twice over.

A Kangaroo Island (S.A.) “ Zion convert ”
of last year, writing from Zion City (Adelaide
dailies of April sth):
Out of our shipment seven families mean to leave.
Almost every day we hear of fresh ones who intend to
clear out. We have written to Islanders to warn them
not to come here —if there are any determined to come,
you just put in your word and tell them anything I have
told you .... Mr. Dowie has called for contracts for
building a wharf near his wife’s summer residence. He
recently bought more land there, and gave it to his son
Gladstone. Now he will build a wharf there to enhance
the value. They say 500,000 dollars was spent there in
improvements last year—and our would-be leader of
God’s Israel bangs and cuts away at Zion people who have
property outside Zion City !
Youngsters at Waircyapa, M.L., have
introduced a new game called “Ellis.” Two
scrub-cutters go into the ti-tree, a third shoots one
of them, and runs ; then the whole school hunts
the criminal down, and hangs him on the hori-
zontal bar in the playground. One “Ellis” was
so successful in eluding pursuit that he couldn’t
find his way back, and was ultimately discovered
by a search party.
The Vic. police and railway
authorities seem to have at last got
on the track of an organisation
that has for long been plundering
the public. Some of the gang
would hide in goods-trucks run-
ning straight from Spencer-st.
to stations 100 miles or so out.
On the run, they would heave
overboard whatever they
could conveniently lift, and
mates at arranged points
on the line would annex
the spoil. When the
N.E. goods reiehel
Seymour, the other
day, one truck was
noticed to have a
large slit in its tar-
paulin through
which the thieves
had escaped—and its
cargo had been thor-
oughly overhauled by
means of lanterns
and pinch-bars. Ap-
parently the re-
ceivers had mistaken
the “dumping-
point,” for, near
Wallan, next morn-
ing, were found
caddies of tobacco,
boxes of drapery,
boots, &c. Simul-
taneously with this
‘‘ give-away, ’ ’ detec-
tives raided the
house of a train-
examiner, and dis-
covered enough miss-
ing property to till a
v a n—jewellery, jam,
clothing, guns, carpets,
silver-ware, hams, brushes,
champagne, whisky, &c. A
day or two later, a second-hand
dealer’s place was raided, with
similar results, and the whole gang
was exposed.
THE TWILIGHT’S PENALTY.
[Fob Tub Bulletin. 1
Creeping outward from the twilight
Come the ghosts of days that died,
Dragging little blackened coffins
With the skeletons inside
Of the Things we left unfinished,
Starved and stricken Things unfinished—
In procession undiminished
Come the ghosts of days that died.
M.ii. J- Barr.
* * * *
A recent case from Lismore (N.S.W.),
where an auctioneer, holding a sale of furniture
in a private house on a Wednesday afternoon, was
prosecuted as a “ shopkeeper open after hours ” is
one of those instances wherein bad administration
of the law serves to throw' ridicule on democratic
legislation. The Early Closing Act w’as passed
primarily to protect overworked employees, and
the department administering the Act can find
much better scope for its energies than plunging
into weary litigation to argue whether an auction
sale is a shop or not.

woman to have kept the gold.

key of the front door. One of us will have
to climb through the window.”
At Ohinemutu, t’other day, Major Mair,
who had been in charge of a Maori feast patron-
ised by Governor Plunket, was fined £5 by the
Maori Council for bringing a bottle of champagne
for his lordly guest into the Maori whare. An
indictment laid against Gov. Plunket himself,
charging him with consuming “waipiro” on the
premises, was only withdrawn after the interchange
of several perturbed telegrams vtith the Maoriland
Cabinet.
MEMENTO MORI.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
What need hath sage Philosopher, in this advanced age,
To set an eyeless graveyard skull amid his tomes of lore,
That lie may ever keep in mind his mortal heritage?
Since oft his spouse will leave her store-teeth on the
floor 1

A REFLECTION.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
No hint I gave for you to guess at my disloyalty
In wishing you that maiden who was Queen of Dreams to
me.
I kissed you, sweet, to forfeit thus my right to hold her
true,
And prove myself, by thought and kiss, unworthy her
and you.

Why should the witness in the law courts
1) : compelled to stand, sometimes hour after hour,
i.i a three-cornered box? Originally, it was no
doubt part of the same scheme which put horse-
hair on the Judge’s head, the idea being to make
the public believe that it was in the presence of
s unetliing more than ordinary man. But this is
the day of popular education, and the public
knows the courts for just what they are—places
where the public does part of its business.
Nowhere else, when a man goes to do
business, is he compelled to stand hour after
hour. Even when a deputation of unemployed
goes to ask Carruthers the whereabouts of all the
prosperity that was promised, Joseph tells the
men to bring their chairs round, and sit down and
gat to business. And when a Judge is appointed
a Commissioner, nine times out of 10 he sees that
the witness is provided with a scat; for he has
found out by experience that he can get more
solid information out of the average man when he
has settled and composed himself in a chair, than
when he is giving half his mind to the question in
hand and the other half to his favorite bunion.
IN TOPHET.—THE BUTTERFLIES DO A
LITTLE MAN-COLLECTING.
Nine Sydney hotel-keepers were up at the
Water Police Court lately for selling adulterated
brandy, whisky, and rum. The highest fine im-
posed was £2; in most cases it was only £l.
frame day, in another Sydney court, a man
was up for a very clumsy attempt to pass off an
adulterated coin in return for a meal. It was the
only bogus coin he had (the publicans mostly had
jug-fulls of their dishonest stuff), yet the Judge
said he was letting him off very lightly with a
year’s hard labor. The moral is that when you
want to do a swindle always put on the apron of
trade, do it behind a signboard, and call yourself
“one of the mercantile community.”

The best satire on bad amatory poetry is
the gush of a doggy woman. All the potted terms
of endearment which the poetaster lavishes on his
Laura she bestows on her cold-nosed quadruped.

The Argus, angry with Yic. Executive
for not hanging one Hope, a semi-imbecile con-
victed of criminal assault :
It is scarcely possible that the Attorney General puts
forward the fact that the offence is not capital in Great
Britain as an excuse. The Cabinet has to carry out the
law of Victoria.
Bulletin trusts that the Argus will not forget to
argue on these lines next time we have a Six
Hatters difficulty or a Petriana myth. The
Executive, however, did make a most vicious
blunder in Hope’s case, saving him from the rope
because of his mental weakness, to hand him over
to the cat. The wretch, who is not held fully respon-
sible for his actions, is to have two floggings and
some solitary confinement in the hope, no doubt,
that this course of treatment will brace up his
intellect and restore his moral tone.

“ Revert ” writes :
Senator F. Dumont Smith (U.S.A.) has prepared a bill
limiting term of marriage to 10 years. This is the first
step towards the salvation of millions, born and unborn.
About eight years ago I had oome to the conclusion that
some such measure must be made law, and have strenu-
ously upheld the view against all opposition. George
Meredith lately voiced similar views. Marriage should be
permanent unless appealed against by either of the parties
during (say) four weeks allowed for the purpose, at any
recurring fifth anniversary of the ceremony ; if necessary,
legal separation to be granted until the time for
appeal arrives. To bring the measure within reach
of the poor, it should be backed up by State
homes for children, and to encourage an increased birth-
rate (which is neces ary for the country —not the
ind.vidual) these homes should take children when the
parents are e.ther unable or unwilling to provide for
them. Either partner having means should provide (.f
only temporarily) on dissolution taking place for the
other having no means. No law of God can be shown
commanding permanent monogamy ; and, even if the
Bible distinctly ordered so, man must use the reason he
has been given in order to right wrongs. Morality needs
a fixed term of union, but there must be an honorable
means of escape from the hell of mis-mating. We need
less strict moral law s more strictly observed.
“Burglar Bill”: Re the unconquerable
attraction high cliffs and deep water have for
lunatics (B. 30/3/’QS). All the “lifers” in Goul-
burn gaol were housed on the top tier of cells, a
small handrail running along the landing being
the only thing to prevent them taking a 3uft. leap
to the asphalt floor of the corridor. One man,
serving a life-sentence for an abominable offence,
told me that he shut his eyes when walking along
the landing, as he always wanted to jump over.
“ War E.” : The unfortunate country
policeman has to act as truant-officer, inspector
of weights and measures, of slaughtering-houses
and dairies, compiler of Federal and State rolls
and of statistics, dispenser of electoral-rights, and
doles to persons receiving aid from the State,
scrutiniser of old-age pensioners, supervisor of
the Dog Act and travelling stock, bill-poster of
Govt, notices, &c., <fcc. Yet it is now seriously
proposed that he shall act as rabbit inspector and
administer the Kabbit Act! And when some
pannikin-squatter or crawling cocky loses a flukey
ewe or a bag of chick-feed, the Inspector-General
of Police is bombarded with complaints that Con-
stable Double-X “ain’t a-doin’ of his dooty.”

A syndicate of enterprising Frenchmen is
negotiating for the lease of the new island block
which the improvements of the London County
Council have created in the Strand. London.
Their idea is, ostensibly, to signalise the entente
cordiale by making a “ Paris in London ” —an
area covered with buildings after the manner of
the great city and occupied by business places and
amusements peculiar to it. But the pious London
C. C. is reported to be scared, lest the amusements
should be too peculiar to Paris ; and, according to
latest accounts, the promoters were undertaking
that Paris in London should contain nothing that
would bring a blush to the cheek of a Piccadilly
clubman, and no grisette should be employed
without a guarantee of character' from a clergy-
man, a J.P., or other more or less reputable person.
If that undertaking is adhered to, Paris in London
will probably be a failure, and the Londoner will
continue to take his old unexpurgated Paris by
one of Cook’s cheap trips.
* * * *
“ P.” : N.S.W. Public School teachers
are supposed to encourage meteorological study
by the pupils, and the “beneficent rainfall” is a
frequent subject of expatiation. In a few schools,
however, a wet day lowers the quarterly average
sufficiently to bring thesehool below the minimum
attendance required fora certain salary ; so while
an assistant is enlarging upon the glorious results
that will follow the rain, the headmaster prowls
round the half-empty class-rooms mentally cursing
the weather-clerk and all his works. The matte r
is so serious that action was taken by the teachers,
with the result that a particularly wet day’s
attendance was officially ordered not to be
counted. But why not make a fixed rule ? Seems
absurd that a single wet day should cut a man’s
salary down for a whole quarter.
* * * *
“ Gip ” : Out after sheep, recently, called,
at the hut of a selector who was supposed to
Eossess some information I wanted. Found the
ut closed, but uncanny sounds issued from
within. In answer to knocks, a sad-eyed little
woman came to the door, closing it carefully after
her. The boss was away ; would we wait out-
side ? Dinner-time came ; no boss ; so the woman
nervously asked us inside. In the room we
entered was a well-grown boy, roped in a chair,
grimacing and making horrible sounds. He was
dumb and mad. Peeping from the other room
were two smaller children, with the thick
features, hanging underlip, and vacant eyes of
idiots; and against the slab walls —trying to leap
up it, like a baged rat—was yet another demented
boy. The parents, I afterwards learnt, were first-
cousins.

“An imbecile may be converted into a
useful citizen ; but for idiots, past the betterment
of the surgeon’s knife, there is no hope—they
should be drowned. We should then have more
time and energy to spend on children in whom
there are the possibilities of success.” — Dr. Brown,
New York Board of Health.

“Sardonyx” : Be Professor Metchniteoff’s
(23/3’05) idea of marrying big men and women to
each other, so as to produce a larger race.
Frederick the Great tried the experiment with
his Guards, men of immense size. But there is
no record of a race of giants being perpetuated.

THE PRINCELING AND THE BOIL.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
There was grief and lamentation in the stummick of the
nation
When the cable column told us that His Royal Nibs was
il!;
When the wires insinuated that M.D.’s had operated,
Strong men tottered, women fainted, and our hearts grew
damp and chill!
In an agony we weltered when the awful news had filtered
Through the oceans and the deserts from the Princeling s
bed of pain ;
But to-day we cut glad capers, for we learn from sundry
papers
That the stalwart Prince is better, and is smiling once
again 1
Yea, we prance in jubilation—we’re a joyous, jigging
nation—
We are singing grateful anthems, we’ve abandoned all our
toil.
We are shouting and hooraying—there are scores of trum-
pets braying—
For our Princeling has recovered from a fierce and
deadly Boil 1
When the heralds caught the tidings and through Britain’s
shires and ridings
Went a thrill that men feel only when some awful danger’s
past;
Aye, they set St. Paul’s a-ringing—there was cheering,
there was singing—
II >pe returned unto the Hodges who had erstwhile stood
aghast 1
In Pall Mall and Piccadilly dukes and viscounts shouted
shrilly—
There were bonfires lit at Clapham, there was joy at
l’eckham Rye ;
Aye, they jerked the cable frantic—underneath the moist
Atlantic—
When the Prince was seen in public with a nurse-girl
standing by I
Guns were firing in the Channel—steam escaped from
every scrannel—
Eactory-whistles bade the workers cease to sweat and
cease to moil;
Even London’s starving legions piped “ Gor’-Save ” in
East End regions—■
They forgot their grief and hunger when the Princeling
beat the Boil 1
G. H.
LOST.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
(The dearest things are the dear departed.)
The dearest girl of all the girls
I’ve loved in twenty years and two,
With locks Egyptian, gilded curls,
Or hair of any other hue,
Was not the one I loved for long,
Nor she I loved at greatest cost —
The sweetest far of all the throng
Was that dear maid I wooed and lost 1
The finest fish I’ve taken slipt
Agilely from my landing net;
The greatest drink I ever sipt
Was that a clumsy fool upset;
The noblest thought that ever came
In summer fine or winter frost,
To give a lustre to my name,
Was that divine idea I lost!
The grandest story that I wrote,
Illum’ed w'ith many a subtle phrase,
The critics yet unborn to quote
In fine, appreciative days,
Was that artistic tale and wise— .
Devised at what a psychic cost !
Which some cursed editor implies
He has unfortunately lost.
EDYSON.
