A WOMAN’S LETTER.
Sydney, April 11, 1905.
Mr Dear Arini.—
Lately a comely young housemaid left her situ-
ation in a Pointer household, and the next time
her recent employer saw the damsel she was
dressed in a nurse’s uniform—becoming shades of
pink and grey—and was selling Easter cards from
house to house. It ought to be illegal for any
woman not a registered nurse to masquerade in
the uniform of one. No man could go a-canvass-
ing got up as a policeman and escape a law-
breaker’s fate. A number of women dressed as
nurses pursue an objectionable form of canvassing.
Soap-selling “nurses” are frequently met with,
and once a “nurse” called at my lair with an
undertaker’s circular.
Mrs. Scanlon, who played female lead in the
Slattery tragedy, was an ideal witness, calm, self-
contained, and, by reason of her cautious brevity
in reply, a match for Mr. Jack Want's cross-
examination. At no period of the trial did she
display any evidence of “feeling.” She is a fairly
tall woman of about 45, of good carriage, and with
hair turning grey. Her garb is usually black, but
beautiful. Since millionaire Tyson died, relations
of his by blood or marriage have been in the pub-
lic eye one way or another, and we are always re-
hearing, and forgetting, that the great economiser
left over three millions. And his property was
valued in drought time, too. Mrs. Scanlon is a
cousin of Mrs. Ross-Soden, who occasionally makes
such loud, resounding splashes in Melbourne
society. Randwick seems to have been the
favorite locality of the Tyson legatees for acquisi-
tion of property. Mrs. Scanlon and Mrs. Forbes,
another cousin, are located in the racing centre.
The Slattery family had not the least idea that
he would be sentenced to any term of im-
prisonment. He left home on the Monday of the
trial with instructions to the household to make
arrangements for a Mountain trip at the end of
the week. The house at present inhabited by the
Slattery family is the property of Mrs. Scanlon,
so she is in the cheerful position of playing land-
lady to the ruined family.
Royal Alexandra Hospital for Sick Children
is making a fresh appeal for funds, to clear off
the building debt prior to the opening of the new
hospital next year. Fifteen thousand pounds is
desired. In bad times, such as this State is now
undergoing, the sum appears huge. Still, the
kiddies shouldn’t appeal in vain. There is
nothing more pathetic than a sick child claiming
shelter. Mayoress Allen Taylor has taken the
matter up generously, and has already enlisted a
large committee to work out the two schemes sug-
gested at the first meeting. Scheme 1: That so many
thousand people should be found who would each
give £l, to be called “ the pound plea.” Scheme
2 : That 100 women should find 40 men, women
or children to give Is. per week each for 12 months;
or the same number to give 6d. a week for two
years.
Miss Birdie Leslie, the pathetic-faced little
N.S.W. composer, is faring forth to London this
week. Not to teach, but to push her compositions
and to compose some more. Her “ Polish Dance ”
and “Ave Maria” are well-known in Australia,
and divers theatre orchestr as play her waltzes and
marches. Miss Ada Crossley is to have the “ Awe
Maria” sung to her by a Sydney singer with
violin obligato, and may introduce it herself to
English audiences.
Talking of musicians: Wybert Reeve has an
illuminating article in S.M.H. on what awaits the
unknown, aspiring singer seeking name and money
in England. He relates :
I also know of a case in which a gentleman has spent
over two thousand pounds in trying to force the interest
of a colonial girl with a fine voice and good appearance,
but he has failed in getting her into what is called “ the
swim.”
I can make a pretty good guess at the identity
of the two-thousand-pounder and her banker. For
the rest, a local critic, speaking conversationally
and mentioning instances, asserts that a crowd of
third-rate (to say nothing of second-rate) singers
and players (Australians) make a very fair living
in London. Here they would starve.
Science is surely lessening the tasks of the house-
wife and the domestic servant. The seedless
apple is a fact, and all over Great Britain and the
IJ.S. it is being propagated. Apple-pie making
will be easier than in the old core-y days. Famous
horticulturist Luther Burbank, who has invented
the “plumcot,” a delightful cross between the
plum and the apricot, and a walnut Avhose shell
may be broken without machinery other than
your finger and thumb, has been experimenting
wth our flannel flower, and—disgusting fact! —
has evolved a colored bloom that reminds you of
pink flannelette. His experiments may revolu-
tionise millinery, for he has succeeded in propa-
gating flowers that will keep fresh, cut and out of
water, for months.
Mr. Alfred Deakin, whose oratory is a joy to
listen to, will arrive in Sydney next week, and
will lecture on the evening of the 19th before th«
N.S.W. Alliance.
You recollect our visitors of a year ago, Herr
Friedlander and violinist Max Schliiter. The
former has settled down in New York, and
married a musical, much-travelled girl; and the
gifted Max, who returned to Denmark to con-
tinue his studies, has become, through the death
of his father, very rich and, as a consequence,
rather lazy.
Town Clerk Nesbitt, of Sydney, has a collection
of over 11,000 picture post-cards. His English
and foreign mail of a week or so ago brought him
362, and that wasn’t a record. Mr. Nesbitt’s
daughters arrange the cards in books according to
their kind : bridges, scenery, Town Halls, Cathe-
drals, and so on.
Cunning little pockets with flaps are on some of
the wide leather belts now on view in shop
windows. But the pockets are too small to hold
a coin larger-sized than sixpence. No hope yet of
the handkerchief having a place wherein to lay
its hem. A good many women have a pocket in
the honi soit, but it’s an awkward arrangement
when there’s a sudden call for cash.
A frivolous correspondent:—
You have mentioned, more or less seriously, open work
stockings as a possible cause of eye-strain to women. I
have made careful observations, and find, quite seriously,
that they are responsible for many cases of eye-strain—
but the victims are men.
Mrs. Bertie Kelly and her golden-haired infant
returned by the Sierra on Sunday. Hubby had
fone as far as Auckland to meet his valuables.
le bustled ashore with baby (carried like a foot-
ball), and the ex-star followed, looking in the top
of condition and wearing a jaunty, red-cloth frock
with red buttons. Miss Stevenson, the ingdnue
of the Andrew Mack Co. that arrived by same
steamer, made shoreward in a short, dark-blue
cloth skirt and silk “waist ” and toque of mink fur
with big cinnamon-hued roses. She is a pure
pink-and-whiter, with her very own natural golden
hair, and a mouth that looks too small to say any-
thing but words of one syllable.
Miss Nance O’Neil has, it seems, become quite
slender, weighing, probably, less than 10 stone.
Nance’s former vastness suited some of her big
and weighty roles.
London Sketch publishes portraits of the
Maorilander, Miss Margaret Dowling, now Prin-
cess Ghica, and her husband. Latter wears a
kind of bandsman uniform. The girl seejns
to have a nice figure inside her severe tailor-
made outfit. The Imperial Ottoman Government
has found it necessary to communicate to another
Government that
Mr. Albert Ghica, a Roumanian subject, born of Rou-
manian parents, and who styles himself, “His Highness
Prince Ghica,” has no right whatever to represent himself
as having claims to a so-called Albanian throne.”
Mister , indeed ! No wonder there are earthquakes
in M.L.
Andrew Mack, the Irish singing comedian, tells
“bully” stories of American stage-life. His
“ laff ” is infectious, and he says he hopes people
won’t ask him Mack what ? He is a big, deep-
chested young man, with slab-black hair, a bullet
head, and a clean-shaven dark face, capable of 60
expressions, many of them infantile, to the minute.
Mr. Mack’s voice is that rarity, a male alto. It has
altered little since his knickerbocker days, and
ranges easily to E in the upper register. His
singing of children’s songs of the ring-a-ring-a-
rosy type of innocence might make him a kind of
Pied Piper person. But his way of giving Those
Endearing Young Charms and other Emerald
gems will palpitate the heart of that section
of the public that read its Tom Moore in the days
of its youth.
Lady Northcote, who is said to take great
interest in women’s work, from sock-knitting up to
their interpretation of Beethoven, is booked to
view the doings of a group of artists (Misses
Cusack, Meston Stephens and Norton) at Mr.
Walter Bradley’s rooms this (Thursday) afternoon.
An American writer discussing Maude .Darrell,
the London musical comedy actress, said to be
about to marry Lord Gerard: “Her face has a
massivelookthoughherdarkeyesare most effective.
There is one very curious thing about her. She
never lets her legs be seen. She wears a certain under-
garment so long that it reaches to her ankles, and
when she raises her skirts to dance, instead of the
usual display of silken hosiery, you see this gar-
ment. At first the plan caused consternation
among the gilded youths of His Majesty’s house-
hold troops, but now the fashion is regarded as
rather chic and fetching. ‘By Jove, don’t you
know,’ remarked one youthful subaltern, ‘one
got rather fed up on stockings.’ Lord Dalmeny
(Lord Rosebery’s eldest son) was one of Maude’s
most ardent admirers. Maudie wouldn’t smile on
him.
From a passenger on the Orontes, writing from
Fremantle : “Lady Rawson is already much
better, and looks her improvement. She and
Miss and young son Wyatt, who goes hence to
enter the army, proved capital sailors. But the
passage was as smooth as the average to Manly.
The N.S.W. Governor and Adonis Wilson will
stay with Governor Bedford in Perth previous to
visiting places that the gold is dug from, and
districts where oranges are grown and water
schemes flourish. May will find him back among
Sydneysiders.”
Another marriage announced is that of Miss
Huthwait to Mr. Mansfield. Bride in this case
is exchanging fashionable dressmaking for matri-
mony. She is a grand niece of late Chief Justice
Alfred Stephen and sister of Mrs. Mathew Henry
Stephen.
Writes my Melbourne correspondent:—
“Aide Buxton (a recent addition to the Ston-
ington staff) is clanking a spur in public and
licking on stamps in private as temporary private
secretary. In Cloyne circles, it is believed that
long, lean Capt. Vallange, just about returning to
these shores, will be offered the permanent billet.
He has private see’ed before and sports rooster
raiment as to the manner born. But a later rumor
has it that Lord Dick Nevill, having failed to find
a ‘ situation ’ in London, is likely to be Gov.
Talbot’s final selection.
“It looks as if the Cliveden dowager were like
to usurp the vice-regal honors. Day after day
that dame is announced as having opened the
Surplus Spinster Home, the Discarded Infant
Retreat, or the Thistle Show. I have looked on
the starched widow blessing a kennel foundation-
brick, and been awed into reverence by her per-
formance of the service. No Ex. has ever put
half the devotion and dignity into such a cere-
mony. The band can scarcely restrain its brass
pieces from banging out the Anthem as the trained
dignitary departs. She has more than the official
manner—she has the divine-right-of-kings manner.
Small societies and associations for the conversion
of the distant nigger never dream now of requisi-
tioning the lady of Stonington to perform their
blessings or bazaar openings. The Dowager is a
more impressive stage effect.
“A globe-trotter sends word that the best ballet
of the world to-day is to be found in Vienna. She
found there a ballet of most dazzling beauty and
Srace; also a nest of Australians cooing amicably,
liss Lovie Mueller among them.
“ Did you ever notice how the feminine wrinkles
advertise their particular care ? The dint mid-
way in the brow, just over the nose-bridge, is
there through thinking things out till the addled
mind aches. The little lines flaring out from the
eyes tell of frivolity, shallowness, and over-much
giggling. The lines about the mouth itself confess
to temper, irritation, love-troubles, sickness. The
furrows in the cheeks spell ill-judgment (mostly in
the use of bad powders), the morbid temperament,
and a long dearth of kisses. Wonderful how
round kisses keep the face.
“ One may have too much of a good thing. The
Melba records are becoming a trial. The gramo-
phone is everywhere. So is ‘ Caro Nomo,’ the
‘ Lucia ’ mad aria, and other Melba gems. They
are switched on at at-homes to save the expense
of engaging warblers in the person. And our
hard-up vocalists are wailing, and crying curses on
the vocal ’phone.
“ Few consider the after-effects of a breach-of-
promise case on a sensitive defendant. I never
did till the charge of fiancee desertion against one
of Melbourne’s richest bachelors came up for trial
last year, and the swain, who didn’t know it was
loaded, had to pay damages that would about
buy the poor girl a handsome set of sables. The
aged bachelor never recovered from the shock of
the charge and the exposure of his innocently-
intended amourette. For months he avoided all
his old haunts, and lived out in a distant suburb
where the publication of his expensive vows had
not spread. The other day he took up his pyjama
case and stepped aboard an ocean liner for a far-
off spot, where he may gain consolation.
“ ‘ Buttons ’ in all the surgeries of Collins-street
has given place to smart Cap-and-Apron. That
neatly-caparisoned Miss is found to attend
with better results. She opens a door to a
patient with a bright, hopeful, reassuring smirk.
She is interested. The patient with the neuritis
twitches, or the knife pain in the chest, or the in-
growing toe-nail, is ‘ bucked up ’ by Cap-and-
Apron. When leaving the chamber of horrors
the sympathetic Miss shows the treated one out
gently, cheers up the confiding patient, deals out
her highest-grade smile to the he-sufferer, who
isn’t suffering so much that he can afford to over-
look smart Cap-and-Apron. Think of Buttons in
the old days ! He was so sharp, alert, impatient,
old-mannish, and despatchful that she-patients
often wanted to smack the little brute, and nervey
men were tempted to kick him.
“ The she-florists have a complaint to make.
Most fashionable patronage goes to two men in
the foliage line. Yet, by way of contrast to the
man florist, in the same street are girl-barbers,
girl-doctors, girl-dentists, girl-janitors, girl-clerks,
girl-waiters, girl-brokers ! Is it any wonder some
desperate males, in irony, start wiring the
forget-me-not and building bosom-sprays for
Beauty ?
“Psychology has no significance for the prac-
tical she-mummer. I asked one of her the other
day if another dazzler of the silk ranks was happy
in her matrimonial state with a ‘ love of a man.’
I was sentimentally interested, and hoped for a
story of exquisite happiness. But my friend
brusquely opined they were ‘getting on right
enough.’ ‘You see,’ she went on, ‘he’s a good
sort—an awfully good sort, and she is grateful to
him for all he has sacrificed.’ That constitutes
the connubial bond. Probably it will wear as
well as any other.
“Julius Knight had a birthday last week, and
Toorak quoted poetry and greetings through the
’phone and over the wires, while some other
adorers made him a nice flyspeck cake to celebrate
the day. Mr. Knight, moreover, had the girls
guessing his years, laughing indulgently at every
flattering miss they made. A he-favorite is full
of assurance as long as he thinks he is dangerous,
and he easily flatters himself he is dangerous.
After that period he grows humble and dotes
some.
“ Misses Ruby and Lesly Madden return home.
They have been long on the wing, and, by a
miracle, have escaped all matrimonial snares.
Miss Ruby is a beauty still. She can command
the lists here now easily, since her two former
rivals—the daughter of Justice Hartley Williams
and the eldest hope of Justice Hodges—have both
married with profit, and removed with their
husbands out of the State.
“Baronet Clarke, back from Vienna and the
eye-specialists, made a re-appearance in his old
stall quarters with a fair party at ‘ Pygmalion
and Galatea,’ Saturday night. Behind him, in an
imposing phalanx, was the Dowager, with a
flower-decorated contingent of dames. The florists
bless the widow for her posy-carrying and spray-
wearing all through the blooming year. It keeps
the rose-and-cactus business brisk.
“An interesting feature of the Statue show on
Saturday night, at Her Majesty’s, was the coming
together after long separation of Mrs. G. B.
Lewis, Miss Mabel Lane and Miss Unie Russell
—the veteran elocutor and two of her old pupils.
The three had an emotional statue scene to
themselves.
“The Rebottaros’ family concert last week had
an overflowing audience. No doubt that the
Achille with the baritone warble, the exquisite
tailorings, and the passionate platform style draws
the Melb. girls. His is a bold Serenader too, and
looks right in the kindling eye of sentimental
Misses when he sings in Tosti of falling leaves
and the kiss that must be straight on the brow.
“The recent Teutonic outbreak of song and
sociability at the Verein Hall was interest-
ing for the English translations of German verse,
dexterous and poetic, by Mrs. Clara Linden —one
of the very-musical Lindens of this State. In a
German troopers’ song of Simon Breu, the rush
and roar of militarism (partly in its cups) for
Kaiser and Faderland is capitally conveyed.
“A dancing mistress here advertises the fact
that she taught Lord Dick Nevill his ballroom
capers.
“ The girl-angler is an established sporting fact.
A rod-and-worm competition took place, the other
Saturday, on the upper reaches of the Yarra,
before a fishy set of experts. Each angler was
allowed to have a man-mate to support her (meta-
phoncally) through the long, still noon and help
(practically) m the worm-adjusting and (when ft
happened) the landing of the fish. Some girleen
with the name of a common coin, like Farthing or
Halfpenny, took the trophy with a perch scaling

“There’s a new winter notion—slumber slip-
pers ! What a Guy Fawkes the beauty-seeker
makes of herself for the poetical dream-hour!
Her face is larded, her hair (or a switch of it, at
least) is removed to the delusion table, her face is
tied up and padlocked into a machine for prevent-
ing double-chin, her detachable teeth are secreted
in a hiding-place, her complexion roses have been
demolished by the sponge, her pencil brows have
suffered a similar fate, and when the gowned one
finally thrusts her Trilbies into slumber slippers,
it is a complete picture.
“ Golfers are getting into brier-proof garments
and strapping on their gaiters, preparatory to
storming the links. Kid or leather bindings are
machined to the skirt borders for practical protec-
tion as well as style. The motor helmets and the
caps with a back-fall that is pulleyed in and lashed
under the chin, are being seized on by smart golfers
who have had expensive as well as ghastly experi-
ences of hats and hai r being blown across the horizon
by sudden storm-gales. There is a story from a
fashionable source that one strenuous golfer wears
but one compact garment which cannot blow up
or drop off without due notice, and a hat secured
to head and neck by a scarf of sail-cloth that no
tempest may dislodge.

“ The ’phone is the cause of a horrid split in an
engagement. The man is the younger of the two
—yet he is always in the wrong. Last time he
failed to make an appearance at dinner, for which
he was to arrive at 6.30. Seats at the theatre
were booked for the evening. At seven o’clock a
wire came— ‘ Horribly off-color. Accept excuses.
Writing to-night.’ Then the ire of that lady rose.
She made at once for the ’phone and rang up
every conceivable place where the brute might be
expected to nurse his sick feelings. Without
avail. But at 10 o’clock that night there was a
gentle ting-a-ling. She was near by—she had
been near by, woman-like, cross yet hopeful—the
whole evening. ‘ ’Night, my dear ! ’sthat you ? ’
Then came a horribly clear hiccup. They’re not
Slaying speaks ; and she wonders did he mean it ?
»r was it a cruel accident ?
“What a mass of contradictions a certain brow-
beetling dramatic mummer is ! He is scholarly,
artistic, musical, has creative talent, yet he gloats
over murders, and the shocking kind of cases that
are heard in camera, and even attends trials of a
creepy, sensational, spine-tickling class whenever
rehearsals permit.
“Interested in the case of an ailing Society
widow, I asked her physician was she improving,
or likely to —any time ahead. His reply was that
it was ‘just a common case of refusing to grow
old. She is old, but she manages to conceal the
fact from herself and the world. She has the
appearance of being beautifully preserved, and
mentally she is brilliant and keen. But her years
are, as a fact, those of a retired grannie. She
keeps going with the youngest, lives as vividly,
adopts the same pace, and I know that her bones
must be aching — are aching—and her strength
strained to a painful degree. She won’t grow old—
that is all the matter.’ Hundreds are in the same
fix. It’s game, but the end is swifter.
“ There is a new method of expressing farewell
sentiments to the ocean voyager. The cabin of
the favorite is converted into a rose arbor— a
snuggery festooned with foliage, leaf-green rib-
bons, Cupid bows, flying cherubs, and other
devices significant of love, friendship, and remem-
brance. When the flowers droop, the main
decorations still hold fast, and the fair voyager
breathes in an environment of figurative adulation
all the way to Marseilles. This cabin compliment
was paid a Sydney family by some Melbourne
chums last week.
Yours Affectionately,
AKENEHI.


WORN-OUT HEARTS.
Sudden Death Averted by Dr, Williams’ Pink Pills -
“ I ,IOR months I lived in fear of sudden
death,” said Mrs. Ellen Skehan, 3 Bel-
JL more-street, Balmain, Sydney. “Every
beat of my heart told me 1 had Heart
Disease. It seemed to be worn right out with
weakness. Nothing gave it strength or rid me of
the fear of death until I took a good course of
Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills.”
From birth (says a medical author) the heart
beats 70 times a minute, so it is not surprising
that hearts wear out oftener than people think.
Seventy beats a minute—more than 30,500,000 a
year ! No wonder there is unsuspected Heart
Disease in the world !
Unsuspected Heart Disease may mean Sudden
Death. Does your arm grow numb? Do your
eyelids tremble ? Have you momentary faintness
and confusion ? If so, the heart is a little worn.
Does your breath come short and fast? Do your
temples throb ? Have you a sharp, sudden stitch
in your side ? The heart again ! Are you flushed
after eating? Does your heart jump and thump
and flutter at any sudden excitement ? These are
all signs of Heart Disease. Again, the heart is
easily disturbed by Anaemia, Indigestion, Ner-
vousness, Rheumatism, Gout, and the after-effects
of Diphtheria, Influenza and Fevers, especially
Scarlet or Rheumatic Fevers. Dr. Williams’
Pink Pills for Pale People are a perfect cure for
heart weakness. They actually make new blood,
and so strike right at the root of Heart lJisea.se.
“Twelve months ago my heart started to go
back on me,” continued Mrs. Skehan. “At
times I could hardly walk two steps. My whole
system was run down. I don’t think my blood
was much better than water. My stomach was
too weak to digest my food. But, worst of all, was
my heart. It thumped like mad if I took half-a-
dozen steps quickly. An 3' sudden sound set it
beating furiously. A fright made me ill for days.
My head used to go round and round, and a
horrible sickly faint feeling would come over me.
I often thought one of these fits would take me
off.
“Every morning I woke with that horrible
tired feeling,” Mrs. Skehan went on to say.
“ Everything was a trouble to me. At last a
friend told me to try Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills.
They made a wonderful difference in me. The
second box gave me a great appetite. Gradually
they filled my heart with rich, strong, healthy
blood, so that it worked regularly and well. Mv
nerves grew steady, and I slept better. To-day
lam a strong, robust, healthy woman. My heart
gives me no trouble now. I believe they saved
my life—so I cannot say too much in praise of
Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills.”
how Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills build up the
strength in just one way—they actually make new
blood. That is all they do, but they do it well,
ihey don’t act on the bowels. They don’t bother
with mere symptoms. They won’t cure any
disease that isn’t caused by bad blood. But then
nearly all common diseases spring from that one

aches, sideaches, backaches, kidney trouble, lum-

ailments that growing girls and women do not like

salutary effect of six months’ hard, physically
speaking, and the inference is obvious.

brated her 95th birthday on April 4th. She was a
daughter of Hurtle Fisher, pioneer colonist; and
all the Fishers are noted for longevity. The seven
surviving members of the family total 565 years
between them.

Mr. H. C. Russell, Govt. Astronomer 6f
N.S.W., has now finished his long retiring leave,
and quits the service on a pension. In Mr.
Russell’s hands the billets of Govt, weather-
prophet and official star-gazer were combined,
and he did not make a success of the former.
The office will now be split in two, the meteor-
ology going to Mr. H. A. Hunt, who has already
shown promise of good work in the position.
Concerning one of Maoriland’s tragedies :
George Fisher, just dead, was probably the very finest
speaker ever heard in M.L. Parliament; a brilliant
debater, too—keen, accurate, logical, convincing.
From reader-boy in Sydney. Fisher became com-
positor in Maoriland, then reporter on the In-
dependent (Wellington), chief of Hansard staff,
Mayor, M.H.K., Minister of Education, Trade
and Customs. A dozen years ago there was no
more picturesque figure in public life; and he
would have been a serious rival to Premier
Seddon had not his later career been marred
by his overmastering failing. Though, simply
because of his drink excesses, he was not taken
seriously' during the past half-dozen years, he
was always sure of return. Inthe election cam-
paign of 1899 his expenses amounted to 7s. In
19)2 he had a one-inch advt. in the Times at the
eleventh hour. “lam a late starter,” he said;
“but a desperate finisher,” and George Fisher
wa? again junior member for Wellington. One
night last session, just before the House re-
sumed after supper, Fisher stood up and ad-
dressed the Strangers’ Gallery 7—“ Gentlemen of
the jury 7, I appeal to you on behalf of this
unfortunate man. I tell you he is not guilty 7.
Therefore I ask you to give him his liberty, in
the name of all ” —— Here Mr. Speaker brought
the incident to a close. Fisher’s funeral was
attended by thousands.
Randolph Bedford has not been idle since
landing in the Cold Country. He has disposed of
two books to London publishers, “Explorations
in Civilisation,” and a novel; has found a Cockney
manager to produce a play of his making; and has
won a big case for damages against a rich syndi-
cate that set him to do something in Australia and
then bucked. The Reckless is on the way to
secure St. Paul’s for his watchchain. By the
way, he differs so strongly from Lawson re literary
prospects for Australians in London that he writes
energetically assuring one Australian journalist
that he is neglecting a certain fortune in remaining
away from Fogville.

for many a day. When no one seriously con-
sidered his chances, he used to say what he would
do in the nature of reforms if he got the oppor-

make Cahill’s row hard to hoe.
A case in Kalgoorlie (W.A.) Circuit Court,
the other day, shows-what it is reckoned to be
worth to manage a gold-mine ; Gleisberg, lately
manager of the Associated, was, it was shown,
paid £25)0 a year, with house, furniture, fuel,
w iter, and a few extras thrown in. And there are
admittedly bigger men in the mining line than
Gleisberg. and bigger mines than the Associated.
Yet. for managing the finances of Australia,
Treasurer Turner is paid £1650, and there are
heaps of people who growl that he is overpaid ;
while the State of Westralia pays its mining
inspectors, who are to some extent placed in
authority over Gleisberg, and are supposed to
know as much about mining as he does, £420 and
£4BO a year.

retired usher of the Black Rod in
N.S.W. Leg. Council, at the ripe age
of 83 is still hale and hearty ; and he
now regrets nothing more acutely
than the fact of his retirement, and
the lack of his long-accustomed occu-
pation. He still haunts Parliament
House. Throughout his 22 years of
office as Black Rod he had never once
been absent from his post, and he
patiently absorbed every speech made
in the Council during that period. In
his opinion B. R. Wise is the ablest
speaker the Council has possessed
since it lost by death W. B. Dailey.

Concerning T. M. Slavery :
Some legal friends called on T. M. Slattery at
Darling-hurst gaol last week. He came to see them in
prison garb, saluted the governor of the gaol, and
t.ien entered keenly into the business of the interview.
He did not seem “ broken-up,” but strong and resigned.
Possibly he may partially retrieve himself, despite
his 61 years. Many will hope so. In some mat-
ters Slattery was profoundly selfish (in politics
he would sacrifice anybody and anything but him-
self), in others he was open-handed and generous.
Never a case of distress came to Slattery without avail,
and it wasn’t by any means al ways other people’s money
that went in relief. After gambling, charity was Slattery’s
chief extravagance ; he didn’t drink expensively, live
ostentatiously, or keep a harem.
At one time T. M. Slattery was Tiie Bulletin’s solicitor.
For many years also he was solicitor to the Trades a,nd
Labor Council, and was generally a prominent figure
in Radical and quasi-Itadical circles. At the time of the
Broken Hill strike, when most public men were howling
for the workers’ blood, Slattery’s voice was lifted on the
other side. Allowing the justice of his punishment,
Radicals can’t help feeling sorry for Slattery.
Over-vaulting ambition it was that put the man pro-
strate on the turf. Slattery was of the never-satisfied
sort. As a solicitor he won a big practice ; in the civil
service he was once prothonotary of the Supreme Court;
in politics he rose to be a Minister of the Crown ; yet he was
never satisfied, never recognised that his merits were suf-
ficiently rewarded. He exaggerated his abilities. His
last effort to win fame was by the turf —the most foolish
of all efforts, for on the turf the layman comes into con-
tact with the most thoroughly organised and completely
skilled of all rogueries. As well might the lamb go hunt-
ing for wolves’ hides as the ordinary citizen seek to wrest a
fortune from horse-racing.
One strong factor in the Slattery dhbftole was a race-
horse named after Slattery’s initials, “T.M.5.,” and
which ultimately came into Slattery’s possession as a pre-
sent. To those whom the gods would destroy they give
a racehorse. The Slattery moke was a brute which
always nearly won, but never once managed to get its
nose right in front on any remunerative (occasion. And
so “ T.M.S.” was interpreted by the crowd as “ Too Many
Seconds ” and “ Too Much Sorrow.”
A generous, but misguided, attempt was made to col-
lect the amount of Slattery’s deficiency, with a view to
the settlement of the case. A w.ell-known turf man
headed the list with £5OO, but the total amount, ’tis said,
only reached £llOO. One prominent politician refused
to subscribe on the ground that a man “who had
held the high position* in the King’s service that he had
could not ass.st in striving to compound a felony.” In
R.C. Church quarters there was a complete lack of sympa-
thy with the movement, for in the matter of Slattery’s
accounts there had been one matter over which the
Church had a special grievance. Nothing like the amount
required could be raised. It was well that it should be so,
though, to those who offered to subscribe, credit is due
for goodness of heart f not for worldly w.sdom.
The quiet dignity of the Slattery trial must have struck
fear into the heart of many another offender. There was
no venom in the prosecution ; the Crown Prosecutor did
his unhappy duty with firmness but without malice ; the
jury hesitated no longer than was, so to speak, due to the
difficulty of understanding how a man who had filled such
high positions in the community should fall so miserably ;
ar.d the Judge awarded a just, but not vindictive, sen*
tenoe. No criminal trial on the records of the State better
vindicates the majesty of the law.
Slattery convicted himself by his own pen. His appeal-
ing letters to Mrs. Scanlon—in one of which he said that
if she pressed him it might mean gaol for him —were the
strongest evidence against him. That opinion—his own
opinion, and the opinion of a lawyer—damned him. Un-
doubtedly had he played cunning, “ laid low and said
nuttin’,” made a counter-claim for services rendered,
turned the whole matter into a question of accounts,
and written no benedictory letters, Slattery could have
escaped.
Ic's a mistake to think that Slattery was an extra-
cunning man. He was rather an extra-vain one. A
correct self-appraisement would have kept him honest by
showing him that he hadn’t the cuteness to be success-
fully dishonest.
Worth noting that the Watchman, the Sydney organ of
D.ll-Mackyism, has no diatribes about the Slattery case.
Even religious rancor has its reservations imposed by
human feeling.

Aboriginalities.
Ancient miner, after listening to several
steep stories about “fossil finds”: “Them ain’t
worth speakin’about! Why, when I was work-
ing in the west drive at the 201-foot, in the old
X shaft at M ,me an’ my mate come across
a whole tree fossil. Yes, a whole pootrified tree,
with pootrified birds sittin’ on the branches a-
singin’ pootrified songs.”

A Bogan station crank was heard by one of
the boys (entering his hut late at night) making
vociferous appeals to the Lord to save him.
After much noisy entreaty, and a little expectant
silence, the supplicant changed his tactics.
“Well, if You’re not going to save me, I’m going
to have a blanky smoke ! ” And he did.
* * *■ *
“G.T.L.” ; Once when I was in company
with a Queensland aborigine, he asked me if I
could see any stars. It was about 10 on a sunny
forenoon, so I laughed at the idea : but the black
pointed to one here and another there. I then
asked him to pick out a big one. He did so, and,
on looking up through the thick foliage of a tree
in the direction indicated, there, sure enough, was
the star. I was therefore convinced that the
darkie could see the others even in the sunlight.
Is this a case of abnormally strong sight or an
aggravated form of hypermetropia—the common
Australian visual defect ?
IDEAL AND REAL.
Ironbark Bill, the celebrated stockman—as
pictured in the London journals, and in actual life.
“F.A.W.” writes about cattle-throwing:
The beast (cow, bull or bullock) is “run” by a horse-
man until it is winded ; then the man dismounts and
chases the beast on foot until he can get hold of the tail
one hand near the butt, a turn of the brush round t’other!
He then “sits back,” and the beast, in attempting to
“ hook ” him, circles round until it falls. If it doesn’t fall
so, it is turned on its side and a man sits astride of it at
the loin and, placing its hind legs together, straps them
at the hocks. The beast is then turned on its belly. If
intended to leave “ in straps ” for any length of time, the
beast is hobbled (with a strap on the'front legs instead of
the hind). It can then stand, which it cannot do if hind
legs are tied.
There is not much danger attached to this method of
throwing, as, while the man retains hold of the tail-
keeping a steady strain—it is impossible for the beast to
“ book ” him, and want of wind prevents it kicking.
There are several other ways of throwing, such as
catching the beast by the near foreleg and off horn or
vice versa ; or catching by both horns, one hand on top
of one horn (palm downward), t’other below horn (palm
upward), and twisting the beast’s neck. This is rather a
risky way, and requires considerable nerve and dexterity.
It is seldom or never used in throwing bulls or bullocks'
but is occasionally used to throw cows, usually when as
sometimes happens, the cow turns on the man before’ he
can get hold of her tail; or when the stockman wishes to
exhibit his prowess.
If impossible to throw a beast single-handed, then two
or more men tackle it. Have seen four men half-an-
hour throwing an 18-months-old heifer. But as a rule
one man to one beast is plenty.
Throwing and tying is the ordinary daily occupation of
scores of stockmen on part of the Dawson River country
(Q.), especially in winter, when they go “ nuggetino- ”
i.e., “scrub-running” for clean-skins, a lot of which can
onty be got by tying, and then coaching them with quiet
cattle.

It was at an amateur prayer-meeting in
the early days of the W.A. fields, when the
uneven camel was one of the most prominent
institutions in tho land of wild-cats and Bigjohn.
Outside the few hard-working religionists organ-
ising the affair, the “ congregation ” was mostly
prospectors who had come, to look on. AH went
well till the meeting was asked to join in prayer.
Then there was an awkward pause, the audience
feeling that something—no one quite knew what—
was expected. At last a kindly Groper in the
back row solved the situation with the Afghan
camel command, “ Hooshta ! ” and the prospectors
“hooslied,” and the teamsters “hooshed,” and as
the preacher man was already in a state of hoosh
the situation was saved.

“ Way back ” : The announcement that
sugar and tannin have been found in gum-bark
reminds nie of an old chap in the backblocks who
used to make grog from gum-bark with a very
primitive bush-made apparatus. The stuff was
much better, too, than wliat is sold in many pubs,
as whisky. The distiller didn’t sell the spirit,
but used it as a medicine when he felt ill, or to
treat a traveller who might drop in.
* * * »
“ Edyson ” : Went rabbitting one after-
noon, with a small boy companion, down Haddon
way, Cardigan, Ballarat. We took with us a
double-barrelled, muzzle-loading shot-gun, loading
with half-pints of shot and large overdoses of dia-
mond grain. We also took a small, inexperienced,
but willing, mongrel named Noah. During a
trying day we succeeded in shooting two lizards
and a large green frog ; and were returning in the
evening, weary, quarrelsome, and utterly dis-
gusted, when we raised our first rabbit. I let fly
one barrel, and missed, and was kicked over on
my neck. Noah took up the running, and gave
bunny a bright and busy sprint for a big hollow
100- by the roadside. Into the log dashed the
rabbit, and Noah stood at the entrance and
yapped savagely. I assumed command, and
stationed my mate, Billy, at the other end of the
log with a piece of wood large enough to “dofor ” a
working bullock. Then, having one barrel loaded,
I took my stand, and ordered Noah to “ sool ”
him. If the rabbit came out at Billy’s end,
Billy was to bash him good and hard;
if he came out at my end, 1 was to
destroy him with about h tlf-a-pound of swan-
shot. Noah went in somewhat reluctantly, and
presently there were muffled sounds of a scuffle in
the hollow. Billy stood ready, I had the old duck
gun at my shoulder, and my finger on the trigger.
Suddenly something brown and hairy emerged
from Billy’s end of' the log, and Billy biffed it.
It was no fault of ours that it happened to be the
head of a weary wanderer who had crawled into the
log to take his rest, but that did not retard our
masterly retreat. I have often wondered what
the sundowner thought when he came-to, pre-
suming he did come-to, and I’ve never ceased to
thank God he was lucky enough not to come out
at my end, where the blunderbuss was anxiously
waiting.
* * * *
“ Stacky ” : Pushing the “ knot ” down a
Darling tucker track, some time back, I was
struck with the sign outside the pub., “Licensed
to sell Foamented and Spurtous Likors.” Had
sampled almost all the varieties of mulga mad-
ness, but had never “took my booze” at the
Spurtous and Foamented, so I dropped in. An
old sundowner sparred up on seeing me approach
the bar. He said he had had several rums, but
the mulga madness brands never upset his diges-
tion, as he always carried an antidote. We had
more rums, and the old sundowner was still cool
and collected. I was getting a trifle mixed, so I
adjourned outside, where the man who sold the
foamented and spurtous was talking to his daugh-
ter. I managed to catch her words : “ What you
think, Dad ? I’ve given that old sundowner five
drinks out of No. 9, and the wretch is walking
yet. What do you think I had better do ? ” Then
replied the vendor of foamented and spurtous
likor: “ Give the Cove one out of No. 13, and if
that don’t settle him we’ll have to try the sand-
bag.” I hastened back, and told the old chap
what I had heard. “ That s all right, sonny,” he
said; “I’ve an antidote for sandbag,” and he
showed me a six-shooter. I thought him well able
to look after himself, so I left him to lubricate on
foamented and spurtous drink, with a dash of
sandbag. But what a land it is, where a man has
to defend himself from his own rum with a pistol !

“ Hamer ” : Flying foxes do not seem to
have foxed to any great extent this year. There
have been no reports from orcharding districts of
shooting-parties, and from leafy places about
Sydney, where they used to swarm and wake the
night with their squeaking* and metallic elashings
with telegraph-wires, they have practically dis-
appeared.
*' * •
“ V.D.” : Some unobservant persons have
wondered how it is that Sydney, and not Mel-
bourne, should have been, with but few excep-
tions, all through Australia’s brief history, the
habitat of the poet and the home of the Bohemian.
I see no cause for wonderment—-the poetic tem-
perament abhors mathematics and loves not
straight lines, rectangles, squares, rigid uniformity
and strict conventionality. You cannot zig-zag
far down a wide, straight, sparsely-occupied
street without being detected and brought to
shame. On the other hand, the ovals and the
curves, the sinuosities and the spirals, the quaint-
nesses and unexplorednesses, the browns and blues,
the greys and golds,'the winds and the wefts of
the city of many waters lend themselves admirably
to the corkscrewings and unconventionalities of
Bohemianism, and are moreover sources of inspi-
ration far surpassing anything to be found in the
right lines, unbending bluestone, and the Puritan
Sabbath of Melbourne.

“Talkin’ of jibbin’ horses,” said Droney,
“reminds me of Spanker. Took him down the
paddock for a load of wood one mornin’, an’ when
I’d loaded the cart the old dog wouldn’t pull a
pound. Well, I jest tied him to a tree an’ let him
stand. Guv him another shot at sundown ; but
no, he wouldn’t draw worth a cent. I made up
me mind that the old crawler wouldn’t go ’ome
till he took that wood with him. The night
warn’t very cold, an’ I was down to see him purty
early in the mornin’; but he hadn’t changed his
mind. I guv him his own time. Paid him another
visit at noon, an’ he was lookin’ hungry an’ lone-
some like. He drew that wood ’ome at the fust
askin’. I went back an’ got another load ’fore I
fed him. Then I patted the old chap, an’ guv
him a reg’lar bellyful. I’ve drawed tons with him
since. He jibbed once; but, as soon as he saw
me start for ome, he put down his ’ead, an’—my
oath ! —he fetched that load alone- full lick.”

“War E.” : Some splitters on Erinvale
Station (N.S.W.) in bursting open a solid box log
came upon a lizard imbedded in the timber. It
was alive, and lived for several minutes after being
released from its prison. With the usual careless-
ness of the busliman, they put the curiosity into
the fire. If the usual bushman found the original
Ark of the Covenant he would boil the billy
with it.

“ Syddod ” : “ Oktis ” (B. 23/3/’O5) forgets
that N.S.W. has three well-defined climates —
those of the coast district, of the
tablelands, and of the great plains.
I have lived on the coast for 48
years, and having been connected
with the timber trade all my life I
can speak with authority about ring-
barking. There are fifty times as
many trees in the coast district now
as there were 60 or 70 years ago.
What was then open, park-like,
well-grassed country is now densely
timbered and quite grassless. The
hills and ranges all round here are
densely wooded, and, except where
the trees are ringbarked, there is
absolutely no grass. Yet old resi-
dents remember when these ranges
were well-grassed, lightly timbered,
and carried much stock; but so
thick is the timber now that there
is neither stock nor any native fauna,
except in the brushes. The timber
has killed the grass, and the animals
have perished for want of food.
Hargreaves, the gold-discoverer, had
a cattle-station on Tuggerah Lakes, and there
were cattle-stations in my time all the way from
Gosford to Maitland. Examination will show a
few, very old trees, but fully 90 per cent, of the
growth has sprung up since the coast district was
stocked, and but that constant war is made
against it, the timber would in a very few years
take complete possession of the soil.

“ A.P.R.” : Bushmen watch ants at work
(B. 16/3/‘O6) for the reason that Darwin and
Lubbock watched them—because they are intensely
interesting. After a heavy fall of rain, I recently
saw an ant colony feverishly sinking fresh shafts.
Each hole had its “worker,” with several smaller
ants carrying away the earth he brought up.
When the big ant remained too long boring, the
others would haul him out of the hole. In one
place, about a dozen ants had pinioned a “worker”
down, and were apparently trying something
stronger than moral suasion to make him work.
Perhaps they were enforcing a common rule of
their Arbitration Court.

“ Vigilans ” : Nearly every horse you see
here (Kimberley, W.A.) this time of the year
(March) has huge holes under the eyes, eaten into
the head by the flies, and it’s quite common to
see a mob of horses rushing madly towards
the marshes from this country, in the endeavor
to escape the pests. Strange to say, flies clon't
worry cattle, which thrive wonderfully here.

“Mars”: Youngsters can often ask their
elders posers. This, from a seven-year-older, is
about the best I’ve heard: “If there were no
stars, no moon, no earth, no people, no nothing;
and if I wasn’t there to see that there was
nothing, how would I know there was no no-
thing ?’’ Will some Aborigin&liar kindly answer ?
THE RISKS OF SOBRIETY.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
They may talk about the risks of gettin’ boozed,
An’ the virtues of sobriety, an’ what,
But blokes, I sez, should stick to what they’re used,
An’ the rest is just eternal tommy-rot.
There was Geordie Smith, the trucker, up at Clunes,
He didn’t drink in a small way like you or me ;
He could pitch it to these Rechabitish loons
’Bout the dreadful dangers of sobriety.
Smith, for years, went home on Sunday, rollin’ tight,
By the hill-track, at a most terrific bat,
But one day he hadn’t touched it, jso at night
He concluded he could cut across the flat.
Well, the blessed flat was riddled like a sieve,
An’ by dark ’twas sort of ghostly an’ forlorn,
An’ the night was rather cloudy. As I live,
Little Smith he turned up missin’ in the morn.
Men went after him, an’ plumbed the deepest shaft
Where they found him floatin’ queerly, like a gull,
But concussed, an’ bust, an’ reg’lar howlin’ daft,
With a litho-blessed fracture in his skull.
He was put to bed at once, an’ lingered there,
’Twas months afore he set foot on a plat;
But the lesson wasn’t wasted, you can swear—
Geordie never went home sober after that !

“G.Y.”: Your correspondents, “J.S.”
and “W.W.”, may be interested to know
that in these islands (New Hebrides) dyna-
mite is a regular trade article with the natives,
who are very fond of fish. Their method
of using it is to make a hole in a plug, insert
cap and fuse, varying from two to six inches,
according to the amount of pluck possessed
by the operator, and then tie tightly in paper
or plantain leaves, leaving only an inch of the
fuse showing, which is fired by means of a
fire-stick. Should the Kanaka lose his head
and throwaway the fire-stick, while retaining
the dynamite, his relations go in force —
accompanied by a Snider each—to the trader
who sold the dynamite and demand payment
for the death or mutilation, as the case may
be, of their dear relation. In three cases
which have come under my notice, the pay-
ment in case of death was three pigs ; for
the loss of half a face and one arm two
“man pigs.” and for the loss of a hand only,
“ two woman pigs.” Therefore it is cheaper
to kill the man right out. One gets more
/or rhis pigs. During the past seven months
two white men and one native have been
killed by hanging on to dynamite, and two
mor.e natives have lost various limbs from the
same cause. A few days ago a white trader,
accompanied by a Kanaka, went shooting
fish, and, just as the man raised his arm to
throw in the plug, the dinghy gave a lurch,
and the dynamite fell into the boat. The
rapidity with which the nigger got into the
water was only equalled by his rush to get
out of it after the white man had made a dash
for the lighted charge and thrown it over-
lf one goes shooting fish where
there is a good deal of dynamite used, a very
short fuse is necessary, as the fish swim away
as soon as it touches the water. Should, however,
there be very little dynamite used in any particu-
lar place, a good long fuse is best, for, though the
fish swim away at first, they afterwards return to
investigate, and cluster round thirsting for infor-
mation, which thirst “dinny” satisfies.
* * * *
“ Jan or ” : When the swaggie dumps
“Matilda” at the cocky’s back door and asks
for toil, that amiable anti-socialist looks hard at
the applicant’s boots, and bases his wage-offer on
their appearance—the worse the boots, the lower
the figure. If a bootless man happens along, as is
sometimes the case in Victoria, he is offered a
“ tucker” job; and a real good-natured cocky has
been known to throw in a pair of his own cast-off
“ crabs.”
* * * *
“Thickhead” : Savages don’t go bald
simply because they never cut their hair. Civilised
man cuts his hair say six times a year, which, at
an inch each time, would give 25ft. in 50 years.
Now Nature never intended a man’s head to grow
25ft. of hair. Hat-wearing has little to do with
the matter. Men of indoors-employment, who
only wear a hat for a couple of hours a day, are
often as bald as pumpkins, and stockmen, drovers
and men who sleep in their hats often have a
thick thatch. Civilised negroes cut their hair,
and go bald ; and wild ones don’t. Also, men of
the “ Beardy ” sect, who let their hair grow,
and twist it round their heads, and probably
never wash it, never lose it. Women don’t go
bald, as a rule, because they don’t cultivate their
scalp sufficiently to grow 25ft. of hair where 25in.
will grow naturally.
* * 4 »
“Duffer”: What eyes a fish-hawk must
have ! When fishing one day I caught a whiting
about 3in. long, and threw it up on the bank.
High in the air above was a fish-hawk —a mere
speck, just discernible—yet in less than a minute
that bird was eating my fish. They drop like a
stone to within a few feet of the ground, then
swoop on the fish. They never miss. Have seen
a 21b. fish carried for fully 200 yards by one of
these birds.
DR. F. F. MUECKE (who marries Miss Ada
Crossley this month).
I have been told that com-
plete immunity from mosquito-bite is enjoyed by
the colored hawksbill turtle-catchers at Lon"
Island (a hell for mosquitoes), Torres Straits?
These men are inveterate opium-smokers, and it
is supposed that the resultant elfects on the blood
gives them protection. It might be possible to
evolve from a basis of opium or nicotine a skin-
preparation deadly to the damnable little insect.

KUBELIK , THE FAMOUS VIOLINIST,
AND HIS WIFE.
Australian hospitality is also sometimes
casual. Brother Ted went to see Tom. They had
not met for years. Ted walked unexpectedly up
asTom stood on the verandah, hands in pockets.
“ Ted, by ! ” cried Tom. “ You’re the last
7 —7 man I expected to see in the Mallee. Go
inside. The women’ll make a hell of a fuss of
you. I’m just off to look at the sheep.'’ Teel
went in, and was treated like a lord—driven here
and there and feasted. But he scarcely ever saw
Tom. At last he grew sick of this treatment, and
sought out Tom. “ Here,” cried he, “ I’m sick of
all this fuss the women are making. I’m
coming out with you to-day.” “Oh, do as you
well like, old boy,” replied Tom. “I don’t
care a cuss. Come along.”
* * * *
F. B. Smith, the ’Murkan evangelist now
in Sydney, says that there are three classes of
swearers—the ignorant, who swear as an aid to
conversation because short of words; the liar,
who thinks that the use of strong language will
help a weak case ; and the thoughtless, who swear
from habit. Classification very faulty. There
are others ; for instance, those who swear only
when they lose their temper, and those who never
swear except when hurt or greatly surprised.

“ Dyckolt ” : “ Chasselas, replying to
“A.P.R.” (B. 2/G/’O4), knows what he is talking
about when he says kurrajong is not the only
native tree on which cattle and sheep will thrive.
He justly mentions the she-oak (bullocks are par-
ticularly fond of she-oak, and prefer it to grass).
But he doesn’t go far enough, and forgets that
there are numbers of other native scrubs and trees
on which stock will thrive. Young mulga scrub
will fatten sheep, so will gidyea, while myall will
be rushed by both sheep and cattle. In ’97 I and
two mates were cutting down myalls for 1500
sheep on a Castlereagh river (N.S. W.) station.
We were seven weeks at it, and when the rain
and herbage came, our mob was fat. For days
they waited for the familiar sound of our axes,
turning up their neses at the green herbage !
Then there are leopard-wood, and quandongs, and
several others.
A.P.R. : Rats !” to that superior
person (B. 23/3/’O5) who can poison dingoes whole-
sale, when the rest of Australia cannot. In every
district of N.S.W. there are dingoes which cannot
be got at in any way ; and men who lose £3OO-
- of sheep every year (as many station-
holders do) are not likely to fail for want of hard
trying. I have myself been trying every kind,
strength, and solution of poison for 15 years, and
1 have found it very difficult to get dingoes.

“ Prooshan ” : ’Twas under the bridge at
West Bourke. There had been a sudden thunder-
storm, and we had rushed there for shelter. Ho
was chiefly rags and grease, and we talked of the
old days of big cheques. “I was in Wilcannia
bout 87, ” he said, “when the rabbitin’ was in full
swing, an’ two blokes 1 knowed came in with a
cheque for 300 quid. They goes to the leadin’
pub and buys up all the champagne in stock—6o
bottles at £1 a bottle. Then they hires an old
horse and dray and gets the bellman to lead the
orse an ring his bell, an’ they breaks them bottles
of fi v.y. into a big tub, and serves it out in panni-
kins to the crowd. But I don't see that it done
em any good. They’re whalin’ now—an’ so
am I.”
HANKERING.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Bush bards sittin’ in town arm-chairs writes a heap of
blithering - rot
About sunrise in the mallee and early mornin’ rides,
Of thoroughbred stock ’orses and bush girls and a lot
Of poetic, Weedin’ idiocy besides.
And you’ve noticed maybe, cully, that the bush is always
callin’,
But they sits tight in the city and allows the bush to
yell,
While the paper’s splashed with grief-drops all the time
them blokes is bawlin’
Of the old days and the bright days—oh, the “copy”
pays ’em well.
They can have it all for my part, for, as far as I can tell,
What the parsons call the “ fleshpots ” is a-beck’nin’ me
to town ;
An’ I’m leavin’ jinglin’ bridles, glowin’ morns, and wattle
smell
With a cheque that I’ll endeavor to knock down.
Give me girls, an’ gaffs, an’ races—give me smokes, an
booze, an’ houses.
Blast the razor-backed old stock-horse 1 . . . Lot o’poet: y
in him !
Give my eyes a rest from moleskins—let ’em linger on
some blouses !
Give me lots o’ fun and frolic for my hundred lovely
“ jim.”
Them's the words I wrote in April. May is here, an’ I’m
in Sydney ;
An’ the girls, an’ gaffs an’ races got me down and took my
gonce.
I am thinkin’ there’s too many of the bloomin’ spieler
kidney
Waitin’ round to welcome bush blokes what has got a
little means.
P’raps the blokes as wrote the verses ain’t as ratty as I
reckon—
An’ the razor-backed old stock-horse ain’t a bad ’un as
prads go—
P’raps the sunrise ain’t so dusty, an’ the bush does some-
times beckon
To a man what’s born and reared there—it’s my pocket
tells me so !

ANSWERS to CORRESPONDENTS.
No notice (except of a hostile character) will be taken of
communications insufficiently stamped. Name and
address must appear on MS. No liability is under-
taken in any case re contributions voluntarily sub-
mitted, whether sent by post or handed in ; and in
no case will MS. be returned unless stamps (of any
State) accompany.

A story told of Jap Admiral Togo as a
youngster in the British mercantile marine, goes
to show that a stoical brown heathen can be moved
by emotion to a strictly Christian form of utter-
ance. At the time when little Togo was a mid-
shipman aboard the Hampshire, he had hardly
begun to talk English in an ordinary way. The
ship—one of the Money Wigram line —was run-
ning from the Cape to Australia, and on this trip
it met a first-class gale. A heavy sea swept
away the deck-house where the midshipmen
had their berths. They were left homeless,- with
their personal belongings in a very vvrecky state.
After the storm had calmed down, the first m ite,
visiting the scene of desolation, found Togo squat-
ting amidst the ruins, and asked him how he liked
a life on the rolling deep. The unhappy foreigner
rolled his eyes in agony, and waxed eloquent,
“Nobunkee; no sleepee ; 1 die. I O, blanky
Money Wigram, damaridblastee !”
“ Gr.H.” gives his view on a subject about
which almost enough has been said :
Writers who condemn Tait’s management of Vic. rail-
ways seem to forget what the Kanuck was imported for.
Bent’s instructions to T.T., before lie started in, read
something like this : “ Make these blanky railways i>ay,
and to with sentiment.” And Ta t'has filled the
bill. Goods-trains don’t leave Spencer-street now with
about one-tenth of their proper load behind them. In-
stead, they haul such strings of cars as to look like
anacondas. Tait has Americanised our railways, and is
getting the last ounce out of staff and rolling-stock.
Responsible clerical officers toil in that big Spencer-street
building nowadays with their coats and vests off. There
was none of that before Tait came. Give him credit for
doing his best to make things batter for the working staff.
He has rowed with the Cabbage-Man more than once on
behalf of the men on the foot-plates, but Bent is a sort of
globular Omnipotence. The paragrnphists who sling so
many rocks at T.T. arc apparently the offspring of those
who ruined Dick Speight. R.S. was imported by the by-
gone Boomster crowd, and his instructions were exactly
the reverse of those given to the present boss. Speig-ht
was told to open up the country. He was instructed to
run railways anywhere and everywhere, and to blazes
with the expense. And, like his ultimate successor,
Speight did his work well. But, when the Boom collapsed
with a sickening thud, the men who denounced
Speight’s extravagance were the men who had
urged him to spill coin around by the dray-load.
The Age, cynically unscrupulous, and determined
to advertise itself at any cost, slew him. Syme
should have flayed the Gillies gang, for Speight was
only obeying his bosses. When Tait has been similarly
slang-whanged into oblivion, I expect that the Victorian
public will discover the true facts, viz., that Tait was
a first class manager and a fair man ; but that
Bent followed him up like a large, library-edition
Old Man of the Sea, and blocked every move that Tait
made for the benefit of the trallic-liandiers. Bent stands
or falls by the result of his railway administration ; and,
so that the returns show a substantial profit, he cares
little whether the staff works 10 hours or 1000 hours
without a spell, bet Bulletin paragraph i sfcs fire theijr
blue-metal at the real Sweater. Tait is all right, and, con-
sidering the respective ministries, is giving Victoria as
good a return for its money as Mathieson did.
MISS NELLIE STEWART (another old-
fashioned photograph).
War-correspondent Douglas Story, in his
book, “The Campaign with Kuropatkin,” con-
cerning the Russian officer :
As a student, the Russian staff-officer is a gentleman
and a soldier of rare intellectual attainment. He has
been allowed to follow his bent, has been stimulated in
the studj' of tongues, has been encouraged to investigate
the psychology of the people dwelling upon the borders
of the Czar’s dominions. A born linguist, among the
French he is a Frenchman, among the Germans he is a
German, even among the Chinese a Chinaman. . . . The
adaptability of the Russian is amazing ; the result of a
wide humanity and ready sympathy. The Russian is as
devout as was ever any Commandant of Boers, and as
scrupulous in the observance of his religion. His near-
ness to nature is at all times apparent; his faults and
his virtues are those of a strong race, of a man whose
blood runs warm in his veins. As a soldier, the Russian
officer is a strangely nervous fighter, a thoroughbred
pawing at tho starting point. His nerves are all a-tingle
his face flushed, his speech thick and voluble. There is
none of the steady calm of the British company officer •
but there is courage—plenty of it—dogged, as well as hot-
blooded. He loves a fight at.elose quarters, and it is sig-
nificant that in war time the- private goes about all his
business with his bayonet fixed. With all his excitability
the Russian officer is commendably reticent with respect
to the affairs of the war, and his own part
in them. There is no boasting, no despondency,
no unprofitable regret. He is content to do his duty as
he sees it, leaving the extrication of the forces from their
difficulties to those placed in authority over him
One’s general impression is, that the officers are a set
of great-bodied, big-hearted, good-natured schoolboys
sadly pestered by the stone-throwing of the small hoys
from a neighboring inferior, but rival academy. I have
not heard, in all these weeks of trial, one ungenerous
word of their enemy from a Russian.
Both gents and dames, pulpit and press,
Hailed Tommy king and prophet;
But when they him do now address,
’Tis in diction lit forTophet.
So the Deform League are swearing
That Tommy they will slay,
While Tommy keeps on saying,
“ All right, some other day.”
Bat Tommy has at length reneged
On all his bans and cusses ;
And where he should have sorely plagued
He only smiles and blesses.

To show the confidence with which the public regard STEELE RUDD’S
future, it may be mentioned that the purchase-money for the rights of
“SANDY’S SELECTION" amounted to £5OO, which is a record
price for any book of fiction written by an Australian and published locally.

“As a lively, crisp statement of the life of an Australian selector, it rings true.”—The Daily Telegraph.
“ ‘ Sandy’s Selection ’ will be read with interest by the many admirers of this promising author.” —The Sydney
Morning Herald.
“ You can’t do better than to fill yourselves up with the adventures of Sandy.”—The Queensland Grazier.
“ The. reader will laugh almost to exhaustion over the absurdities presented, but he will understand that
there is something beneath the fooling.”— Melbourne Leader.
“ Lionel Lindsay and other artists have contributed a number of illustrations as laughable as the text.’’—The
Australasian.
“We heartily recommend the book to young and old. Many a hearty laugh will result from reading it.” —The
Tasmanian News.
“‘Sandy’s Selection’ does ‘Steele Rudd’ credit, and is a worthy successor to his earlier books.”—The
Bulletin.
“The book should have a record sale, and will only serve to whet the public appetite for more such readable
writings from the same author.” — Sydney Wool and Stock Journal.
“These sketches make absolute, ‘Steele Rudd’s’right to the title of leading humorist of Australasia.”—
Christchurch Times.
“ A fine piece of descriptive writing ; every line of it a sketch of human thought, view, and feeling, bold,
forceful, concrete, and it stands on the same plane as the work done by Phil May.”—The Queensland Grazier.
“ ‘ Sandy’s Selection ’ is a book to buy, not only for the fun wrapped up in the pages, but for the germs of
truth so well set forth.”—The Brisbane Herald.

pictures of country life are humorous and truthful.” — Auckland Star.

the broad humor of the narration.” — The Melbourne Age.

POLITICAL
POINTS
Johannesburg was prophetically named.
It is now truly John’s town.
It’s not an anti-Socialist party, but an
anti-suicide party that Mr. G. H. Reid leads.
The erstwhile rabidly Jingo Melbourne
Age begins a fierce exposure of the Boer war job
with this :
The wretched story of unabashed cupidity which has
peopled the Rand with Chinese is probably one of the
most shameless of modern times.
The S.A. Government wants someone to
put ice on its head while negotiating for the pur-
chase of private estates under the Closer Settle-
ment Act. In several instances it has paid more,
privately and at auction, than the same estates
were Under offer for to outside people.
A London correspondent :
Federal Hansard is not available here, nor the Federal
statutes, nor the decisions of the High Court! Westralian
Agent-General -James has tried to remedy this crying
disgrace by getting such publications for his office. But
at present no information at all in regard to Common-
wealth matters is available in London.
Makers of base coin should have the
sympathy of Melb. Argus in their rejoicing over the
fact that the Commonwealth is not going to coin
its own silver from new designs. It looked like
another attempt at a gross interference with
private enterprise.
N.S.W. Railway Commissioners’ new wool
rates, which took effect on March 1, reduce the
charges on scoured and increase them on greasy
wool. The rates will have the good effect of
causing much more of the wool-scouring to be
done in the interior.
“Wrest the apple trade from Tasmania,”
says the provincially-minded Carruthers, at
Mudgee. “ Want a waistcoat ? ” asks Carruthers.
“Nothing easier—cut up your coat, and make
one.” But not a word about wresting the building
of locomotives from the foreigner.
“P.D.” : Reasons why Mr. Denny, M.P.
(S.A.), is being opposed by the Labor party at the
forthcoming elections are that he was one of those
who votod for the Land Grab Railway Steal.
Also he is pledged to the capitation grant and in
many other matters takes the Tory attitude.
Kyabram (Vic.) has just discovered that it
is a sufficiently important place to have a ceme-
tery of its own. It seems to have only now found
out that the Kyabramapootra is dead. The
deceased ’pootra has reached its birthplace at
last, after poisoning all the rest of the State.
If Australian flannel, too, is adulterated,
there is a simple remedy for the fraud : inspect
the Australian flannel-builder’s mills and legisla
tively compel him to label liis stuff as what it
actually is. Then absolutely shut out the foreign
defrauder-—you can’t inspect his mills or pass laws
that will bind him.
“ S.S.” : One of the estates the Vic.
Government seems to have determined on buying
is the Mt. William, near Ararat. The estate is to
be used for closer settlement, and the price men-
tioned is half a million. It is, much of it, old-
time mining land, and not at all suitable for the
purpose. Who are the experts who advise this
“bargain”?
Without more Protection to help new fac-
tories into life, the outlook for the N.S.W. coal
trade grows steadily gloomier. Newcastle’s
exports for the first three months of the year were
only 608,129 tons, as against 668,479 tons last year,-
notwithstanding that shippers are scouring the
world for markets. They are at present struggling
for an opening in Canada.
While the Adelaide Scottish Regiment
was kicking up a shindy because the Customs
Department didn’t see eye to eye with it re duties
on imported kilts, tartans, and other fripperies,
the Melbourne “Scotchies” were quietly helping
along Australian industry by having their tog-
gery made at a local manufactory. Their reward
comes in the fact that—as the men unanimously
agree—the locally-produced uniforms have pioved
satisfactory in every way.
N.S.W. had a great harvest again this
past 5ea50n —16,463,680 bushels of wheat, accord-
ing to acting-Statistician Hall’s estimate. The
United States’ harvest in 1904 was 2,457,480,000
bushels of corn, 542,399,000 bushels of wheat, and
894,595,000 bushels of oats, not to go into details
about rye, buckwheat, barley, and other things.
These, make the N.S.W. figures blink. But it
only ".hows what it means to the farming business
to have a big manufacturing population to sell
things to.
The Employers’ Federation, at the Adel-
aide conference, passed a series of enthusiastic
resolutions condemning the tyranny of unionism,
and upholding the rights of the non-union working
man and the sanctity of freedom of contract.
Then, having enumerated a platform of Tory-
encrusted doctrines, the Employers’ Federation
passed a final resolution denouncing each and
every employer who would refuse to join the
employers’ union to carry out this programme, as
a blackleg of the blackest dye. Also it howle 1
dismally at employers who dared to advertise in
The Bulletin, suggesting that that pernicious
publication should be boycotted. Fat’s notion of
“freedom of contract,” like most of his other
notions, is rather lop-sided.
Palmerston North was the first Maoriland
town to adopt the unimproved land value tax for
local rating, and after seven years’ trial the town
clerk was recently asked to set out the results.
Almost immediately after the Act was adopted,
absentee owners cut up large areas for sale. In
other words, the absentee decided that it was a
socialistic place, and unworthy of his confidence,
so he “withdrew his capital”—which is another
way of saying that he gave up his little game of
sitting down tight and waiting for a favorable
opportunity to bleed people who wanted to use
the land.
Talking to the Employers’ Federation Con-
ference at Adelaide last week, one of the great
Smith family, president of the central council,
enunciated the Fatman’s policy for the Common-
wealth. “It is absurd to think,” he declared,
“that immigration can be restricted into this
country. Was it likely that the world was going
to let them monopolise this great country ? ” He
then declared that if he had his way he would im-
port labor, “colored or white” ; and his admiring
colleagues immediately elected him president for
another year. In other words, the Eatman’s pre
scription to lay the spectre of armed invasion by
the Asiatic, is to make Australia a present to the
yellow horde at once.
“ San Remo ” writes—perhaps not more
strongly than the subject demands :
If the Japs ever reach Australia, it will be through
the connivance and assistance of our “Motherland.”
Look what England is doing- for the Japs in British
Columbia, where a servile Dominion Government sacii-
fices its own children (the S(ate’s) in the (alleged) interests
of Britain. Look what the “Motherland” has done for
the Chow in S’Africa. What has she done, what is she
doing, for “The White Race”? Let White Australia’s
backbone relax, and we shall see—and sorrow. But
thank Heaven, our National spirit is spreading and
strengthening.
British Royal Commission on the £ ‘ Taff
Yale ” decision considers B. R. Wise’s Bill, intro-
duced to N.S.W. Parliament, Nov., 1903, “by far
the most practical effort to relieve the difficulties
created by that decision,” and probably that
measure will be recommended for adoption by the
British Parliament. The tribute to the Australian
is the greater because, before that measure had
been brought under its notice, the Commission had
been spending some time in discussing the best
means to relieve trades unions of the disabilities
following the Taff Yale decision.
“Shaker” : It is time to put an end to
the comedy of errors for which the use of the word
“ Socialism ”is responsible. Surely the value of a
word is in direct proportion to its capacity to ex-
press precisely and convey to the person addressed
the meaning of the speaker (or writer). Judged
by that standard, the word “Socialism ” is a mis-
chievous impostor, for scarcely two people seem
to mean exactly the same thing when using it.
Therefore, I propose that a brand-new name be
invented for the particular brand of Socialism
affected by the Australian Labor party, and that
it shall be so constructed that it will be impossible
to confound it with anything else.
The Freetraders got little satisfaction from
Mr. C. E. Harrison, a Ballarat tailor, who wrote
to the Tariff Commission objecting to higher!
duties, and was forthwith invited to give evi
dence. He said :
Tweeds were produced more cheaply now than in the
days of sweating, because men receiving high wages pro-
duced more, and new machinery had been introduced.
As long as an employer gets cheap labor he will not look
round for improved machinery.
Finally he suggested a 50 per cent, duty on tweedy
invoiced up to 2s. a yard, 40 per cent, between 2s.
and 2s ._ 6d., and 30 per cent, above that price.
And this was the man who had originally objected
to increasing the duties !
The Employers’ Federation has discovered
that the Arbitration Act scares capital from
putting itself into new machinery and plant.
Well, why hasn’t it done it in Maoriland, where
Arbitration has been in force for years? The
lately-published “ Statistics for 1903 ” gives these
figures :
Materials. Buildings. Plant.
1890 .. ..£1,483,902 ..£2,491,189
1895 .. £3,285,247 .. 1,743,073 .. 2,988,955
1900 .. 7,399,698 .. 2,419,803 .. 3,826,574
Capital had 80 years without Arbitration in which
to turn itself into machinery, plant, and factory
buildings, and it did it to the extent of only
£3,975,091; but in the next 10 years, with Arbi-
tration and all the other horrors of “socialistic
legislation” at work, £2.291,286 was added, ex-
clusive of much more that was put into State
enterprises. Either Australian Capital is a very
timorous creature compared with that of Maori-
land, or the Australian federated employer is
talking rubbish.
Max Hirsch, the whirlwind
of faked fiures and misquotations,
was up before the Tariff Commis-
sion the other day, bringing with
him some alleged Victorian flannel
(supplied by he wouldn’t say
whom, made be wouldn’t say
where), and with it a certificate
that it was 70 per cent, cotton.
Hirsch was horrified, and to
chairman Quick, who asked what
they were asked to deduce, said
that the State should not help an
industry which went in for such
things. Qn the same grounds,
when a baby is so thin that,tucks
have to be made in its clothes, its
mother should sternly refuse to
give it any nourishing food.
Chamberlain, in comment on
the speeches of Campbell-Banner-
man and co., the other week :
What they denounce in Opposition
they will condone when they come into
office. They will accept what they
execrated.
A truism indeed ! And the game
is what the players make of it,
and the players are what the
game makes them. Every old
politician, at any rate, is a har-
dened humbug. In some cases,
he has an exceptional Gladstonian
capacity for humbugging himself—
for persuading himself, sst La-
bouchere said of Gladstone, that
the ace he lias carefully concealed
up his sleeve was really placed
there by the Almighty.. As a
rule, the hardened old party-poli-
tician knows himself for a hum-
bug, and naturally becomes as
mean and vain-glorious a fellow as
any other professional mummer
who performs on a public stage,
and struts and bellows and ‘Avorks
the press ” to get wrorable.notices.
A N.S.W.. r epresentative atx
the anti-Arbitration Employers’
conference in Adelaide sighed for
the days when the employers
mounted the wool-drays, drove
them through the streets of Sydt-
ney, and were stoned by the
crowds —he forgot to mention that
the Biot Act was hiecuped, and
the streets cleared by men with
drawn swords, and every third
man had a baton hidden in his
clothes, and fire-insurance policies
were suspended. It’s just as well
to know what the men who want
to abolish the Arbitration Adi
sigh to put in its place.
The retirement of Malcolm
M‘Eacharn from Melbourne City
Council caused a quite lively con-
test for the vacant seat. Mr.
Frank Davies, of Gordon and
Gotch, tried conclusions with Mr-
Charles Atkins, the pushful presi-
dent of the Chamber of Manu-
factures. Collins-street on the
day of the election was brightened
by the smiles of the rival candi-
dates. Even a non-voter could
reckon on getting a smile.
Davies, being the more genial
smiler, gained a popular victory,
but Atkins scored a thousand
votes.
“ Viking ” points out in the
D.T. that if the Australian Labor
party is socialist in the sense of
the “ Internationale ’’—one of the
strongest of the socialist bodies
in the world—it must drop a
White Australia and all other
national ideas. Which is true
enough as an argument, and only
makes it clearer that the Labor
party, strictly speaking, isnt a
Socialist party, though some of its
aims are socialistic.
The borrowing, policy of Australian
Governments is based on Artemus Ward’s advice :
“ Be virtuous and happy;,and try to live within
your means—even though you have to borrow the
money to do it with.”
Griffith and Allen, Patent Attorneys of
Sydney, have opened a branch of their business in
Melbourne —(Broken Hill Chambers, Queen-st.),
under the management of Mr. H. P. Keogh, of
Melbourne.*
WANTED, A STRONG MAN.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Mr. Donald Mackinnon, leader of the third party to
the X ictorian Parliament, in reply to a question as to
whether he was in favor of abolishing the Upper House,
said that he had no very strong opinions one way or
other. —Daily paper.
We are weary, friend Mackinnon, of the man with weak
opinions—
We are weary of the wobbler with the undecided mind;
We are hunting high and low, sir, through our broad and
brown dominions
For a man with strength and reason underneath his facial
rind I
We are looking for a leader with opinions made of granite.
We are tired of men of putt3’, and we look for one of grit;
We have had enough of Yes-No, and we hereby curse and
ban it,
And we give the neuter notice that it’s time for him to
quit.
Poor, debilitated spooklets of the order wish3 r -wash3 r
Rubber-minded office-seekers, pun}' puppets of the press
Reeling off their tabby speeches and their perorations
boshy—
Oh I for one man, strong and able, filled with grit and
earnestness !
G. H.
