Tumor on the Spine
and Kidney Trouble
Removed by
VITADATIO
A Remarkable Recovery I
llth June, 1904.
Dear Sir, —About fifteen years ago my back was struck
by a piece of scaffolding, which caused a severe bruise,
and eventually a tumor formed right on the spine. It
grew larger and larger. I saw my doctor, and he told me
that the growth would have to be cut out. I declined to
undergo the operation, and went on for some years, the
tumor steadily growing larger the while. Then I began
to suffer from weakness of the bladder and kidneys. I
was getting worse gradually, till blood commenced to
come away. I was very much alarmed, and when telling
a friend about it he strongly advised me to take VITA-
DATIO for the bladder and kidneys. I acted on his
advice, and after a time the bleeding ceased, and I got
quite well. During the time I was taking VITADATIO I
was greatly surprised to find the tumor getting smaller,
until it entirely disappeared. I took the remedy only
for weakness of bladder and kidneys, and never for a
moment thought that it would cure the tumor. I am
happy to say that I am now in the best of health, thanks
to VITADATIO. I freely give you this testimonial, and
can with confidence recommend VITADATIO to any person
suffering as I did.
Yours respectfully,
GEORGE MARRIOTT.
Acre-place, off Malvern road, Malvern.
1 have pleasure in substantiating my father’s testimony.
GEORGE MARRIOTT Jun.
For further particulars ,
S. A. PALM Eli,
Head Office: 439 Flinders Lane,
Melbourne.

MESSRS. ELLIOTT BROS., O’Conn ell-street, Sydney,

Missing Friends.
WILL A. G. CHAMBERS, of Warminster,
England, who returned to Australia
12 years ago, please communicate with his
people.
Black and White.—l was so tired this
morning that had it not been for the ever-blessed
Scrubb’s Ammonia in my bath Ido not believe I
should have been able to get up till night. It is
wonderful stuff, and would waken the Seven
Sleepers, while it is altogether pleasing, too, in its
effect. I believe it has various advantages besides
this one. Once I took a stain out of a dress of
mine with it, a feat every other condiment had
failed to accomplish. I remember, too, that
Julia confided in me that she owed her fine com-
plexion to its influence in her hand-basin, but
then she used it in combination with Scrubb’s
Antiseptic Skin Soap. I do not know why I
chronicle these facts, but I am grateful to Scrubb
for having wakened me up, for I am just in time
to attend a lunch party, where I am to wear a
new gown contrived out of various remnants
snatched at the January sales.*

IN the HIGH COURT OF JUSTICE (Chan-
cery Division).—Mr. JUSTICE HARWELL.— 1904, S.
3,9o7.—Between ARTHUR BINGLEY SPAWFORTH
Frederick William Humble, and William Scholefield the
younger, Plaintiffs, and GEORGE ABERCROMBIE
HASKEW, Algernon Osmond Miles, the trustee of the
property of John McMillin, a bankrupt, and John James
McMillin, Defendants. To the above-named defendant,
GEORGE ABERCROMBIE HASKEW, take notice that
this action was, on the 19th October, 1904, commenced
against you, and that on the 18th January, 1905, a con-
current writ for service upon you in Australia was duly
issued, and that the plaintiffs, by their writ of summons,
claim :
(1.) A declaration that under a deposit with the
plaintiffs by the above-named John McMillan of a mortgage
to him executed by the defendant, George Abercrombie
Haskew, and dated the sth August, 1881, of that
defendant’s share and interest in certain funds in Court
to the credit of a cause, Haskew v. Downs, 1874, H. 41,
and in all other the estate and effects of Richard Downs,
deceased, to secure the sum of £330 and interest owing by
the said John McMillin to the plaintiffs, the plaintiffs are
entitled to a charge on the funds and interest included in
the said mortgage for the amount due to them and
interest.
(2.) That the plaintiffs’said charge may be enforced by
fore-closure or sale. j
(3.) That for the purposes aforesaid all proper accounts
and inquiries may be directed to be taken and made.
(4.) A Receiver.
(5.) Further or other relief.
And that the Court has, by Order dated the 27th
February, 1905, authorised service of the concurrent writ
upon you by sanding a copy thereof, together with a copy
of the said Order, through the post, prepaid, in an
envelope addressed to you, care of Mr. Selwyn Haskew, of
32, Castle-avenue, Hale End, Chingford, Essex, and by the
publication by advertisement of the said writ of summons
and of the said Order once in the London Gazette, once
in the London Daily News newspaper, and once in the
Sydney Bulletin, and Australasian newspapers circulating
in Australia, be deemed good services of the said w r rit of
summons upon you.
And further take notice that you are required to appear
to the said writ of summons within 100 days after the
last insertion of this notice in manner aforesaid, inclusive
of the day of such insertion, and that in default of your
so doing the plaintiffs may proceed therein, and judg-
ment may be given in your absence.
Dated the 7th day of March, 1905.
JAQUES and CO., 8 Ely-place, London, England,
Agents for Scholefield and Son, Dewsbury, Yorkshire,
Plaintiffs’ Solicitors.

How to Make the Liver Act Properly
Rev. Charles Wincott
WRITES :
“ Gentlemen, —About three years ago I
was confined to my bed for a short time with
a very acute attack of Liver complaint.
After other medicines had failed to take any
effect, I was recommended by my doctor to
try Kutnow’s Powder, and I am glad to say
that after taking two bottles I was com-
pletely cured, and my health has been
better ever since. I have since recommended
your excellent preparation to quite a number
of sufferers, who testify of similar results.
“(Rev.) CHAS E. WINCOTT.”
(Incumbent of St. Phillip’s Church,
(Adlington, Lancs.)
Dr. THORPE wk.™:
“ Grant Building, Los Angeles.
“ I prescribe Kutnow’s Powder and take
it myself occasionally ; it never fails to bring
about the most gratifying results in the most
pleasant manner.”
Sir J. B. MONCKTON
(Late Town Clerk of London) wrote :
“Guildhall, London, E.C.

organs by which it may be changed into flesh and bone. We
are, therefore, furnished with teeth to chew, palates to taste,
stomachs to digest, livers to aid digestion, and bowels to carry
off the refuse matter. The Liver is the very gateway of
health, the sewage farm, where the poisons are neutralised by
the action of the bile ; it exerts an all-powerful influence on
the mind, temperament, and disposition. If your Liver is
active you are happy and light-hearted, if sluggish you are
crabby and miserable. Kutnow’s Powder will keep the
liver in perfect order.

Dreadful Sores
Yield to the Marvellous Blood-Purifying
Properties of Dr. Morse’s Indian Root Pills.
No person can be afflicted with a greater mis-
fortune than having a running sore that will not
yield to ordinary treatment. The sore may
originally be caused by accident or otherwise ; but
if it does not heal it is because the blood is impure.
The foul humors, instead of being carried away
through the regular channels, burst through the
skin, causing suffering and humiliation. The case
published below is that of Mr. Henry Bramble,
of North Broken Hill, who met with a serious
accident, causing a wound that would not yield to
either internal or external remedies, and which
nearly cost him his life. “Nearly eight years
ago,” says Mr. Bramble, “when working on the
New Chum Consolidated Gold Mine, Bendigo,
Victoria, a rock-drilling machine fell on me,
making a large wound in my back, exposing the
kidneys. After twelve weeks in bed two doctors
operated on me, and then it was eighteen months
before I was able to work, the wound refusing to
close. In spite of all the medicines I took
internally, the oils and ointments used externally,
the wound never closed up, and I had to go to
work with a large towel tied round my middle.
For the past four years I have been employed on
Broken Hill mines, with the wound still refusing
to heal, until nine months ago, when I started
using Dr. Morse’s Indian Root Pills with great
results. They improved my blood so rapidly that
the wound healed quickly, and now is quite closed,
only leaving a large scar. I thoroughly believe
that the pills are the sole cause of my cure, and
can honestly recommend them.” Mrs. Bramble
endorses everything her husband says about the
pills.*

SPORTING NOTIONS.
After all, the Australian Race problem is how
to pick the winner.
* * • *
John Cleeland, winner of the Melbourne Cup of
’75 with Wollomai, and now living on his estate on
Phillip Island (Vic.), recently attained his 80th year. He
is a J.P., and regularly attends the sittings of the San
Remo Court.
* ■* • •
A.J.C. had summer weather for its autumn
meeting. The increased accommodation, the freshness of
the lawn and the splendid state of the track made it an
ideal day. Gladsome’s win of the April Stakes was a
beautiful exhibition of the marvellous sprinting powers
of Seaton Delaval’s wonderful daughter. Jumping
away to a perfect start, the mare slowed down
immediately, and the pacemakers swept to the front,
with them going the Doncaster favorite. Marvel Loch.
Lochiel’s daughter had an advantage of 81b.
over Gladsome, but the betting told its own story.
10 to 9 on, Gladsome ; 6 to 1 v. Marvel Loch. Marvel
Loch ran in the middle of the front bunch, Gladsome led
the second. Then Lochiel’s daughter moved up and took
charge as they entered the straight. Gladsome moved
with extraordinary speed. At half distance the two
chestnut mares were abreast, but Gladsome had plenty in
hand, while Marvel Loch was all out, and Jockey Barden
was content to win comfortably by half-a-length.
* * * *
Only five horses remain in A.J.O. St. Leger, to
be run at coming Autumn Meeting, and probably two of
those will not sport silk. Fact remains that except on
rare occasions fields have not been bigger. Forty years
ago there were 10 starters. The biggest field in the last
29 years was six.
* * * *
The real trouble at the recent S. A.J.C. meeting
was want of backbone on the part of the club. It
personally heaved out the small fry bookmaker with grim
decisiveness, but when the big gun declined to remove
himself it put its hands in its pocket and yelled for the
police. Police said they could not act as chuckers out—
they were there to see that the peace was kept, and would
prevent the solitary bookmaker assaulting the whole club,
but nothing more. Then the club just sat down, the
bookmaker lifted up his voice and yelled the odds, and all
was gamble. The same trouble occurred at Hooligan’s
hash-house in Woolloomooloo recently. Hogan had n’t paid
his “ rint ” and was requested to deposit himself and be-
longings on the kerb. He said he hoped the empyrean
might fall on him if he did. Mrs. Hooligan appealed to
the police. The Force said it was powerless unless
Hogan assaulted anybody. If young Hooligan threw him
out and shut the door the Force would see that he didn’t
get in again, and if Hogan assaulted Hooligan in the
process of ejection it would “dale wid” him. It was
only there to prevent assault. Hooligan, junior, ran Hogan
out. As he did it Hogan bashed him badly, and the two
fell into the arms of constable Murphy, who promptly
locked Hogan up.- Then there was peace. The S.A.J.O.
might do worse than hire a young Hooligan.
* * * *
Spite of Rupert Clarke’s 3000 gs. purchase, Can-
teen, coming over from Melbourne during the present
week, for the A.J.O. Autumn meet, probabilities are that
he won’t line up, as a very shaky leg necessitates a lot of
nursing. Heaps of public money have gone on Canteen
in doubles and straight-out betting for both Doncaster and
Sydney Oup, but, at the moment of writing, 1000’s to 10
and 15 are freely offered against him for either event.
The books rarely make a mistake when they are so liberal.
* » * *
All Fours’ high jump of 7ft., at West Maitland
show, is an inch over the height cleared by Moral, New-
haven, and Strathmore, at Bendigo, in 1903—then an
Australian record ; but it is a long way below American
and Canadian records. For instance, Heatherbloom
jumped 7ft. 9in. at the Philadelphia Horse Show of 1903,
and the next best jump is the 7ft. 6fin. of Pearl in the
prior year. All Fours is by Ithuriel, and was bred at
Bogamildi.
• » • *
Perhaps the silly crawl of the free people of
England was never more painful than in connection with
the sale to the King, for £2500, by Spencer Gollan of
Maoriland, of Moifaa, the favorite for the English Grand
National. Another of Gollan’s in tne same race was Sea-
horse 11., and, a few days after the sale, the rumor got
round that Seahorse was better than Moifaa at the weights.
At this half the sporting papers in the Cold Coun-
try promptly raised an awful cry that a man had actually
dared to make a good bargain at the expense of the King.
But they crawled a little further down on their stum-
micks the next week by announcing that Mr. Gollan
would be sorry to win the Grand National if there was a
chance of the King winning it, and that he actually
intended to scratch Seahorse so as to give Moifaa a better
chance. Seahorse, as well as Moifaa, had been well backed.
If an owner out here did that sort of thing, the A.J.C.
would very probably refuse his next entry, but at ’Ome it
was loudly applauded as British fair play. However, a
good deal of it was probably moonshine, as both started.
Doubtless just before the start, Seahorse grovelled along
on his stummick and licked Moifaa’s hoofs.
* * * *
The obliteration of betting news from news-
papers in public libraries would, if adopted in Australia,
reduce a good deal the expense of maintenance. The
newspaper department of the Public Library in N.S.W.
carries about 90 per cent, of betting-news patrons. The
hours at which the papers with special sporting intelli-
gence are available is known by the rank and file of these
readers, and the rush is prompt and exacting. The news-
room, being in thje centre of the city, is to the hand of the
great mass of youths and men who fringe the professional
betting circles. They bet when they have the money and
bet in spirit when they haven’t, and retire from the news-
room primed with the very latest in jockey and prad.
* * * *
Champion runners Duffey and Shrubb are now
in Sydney. There has been a lot of preparation of dark
horses lately, and there are many Cornstalks who believe
that both men will strike trouble here. Still, if those
Maoriland times are anything like correct, we’ve got no
hope, unless Carruthers is chased round the track by his
Reform Policy.
* * * *
English and ’Murkan amateur runners—Shrubb
(distance) and Duffey (sprinter)—are widely dissimilar in
structure. Duffey (sft. 7in.) is strongly put together, but
lissom-limbed, and owns a pair of well-moulded legs that
might belong to a wrestler, and a physique generally
which would stand to him in that capacity or as a boxer,
swimmer, or even weight-putter; he is inclined to be
ohubby-looking, and will probably run to fat quickly when
he gives up active exercise. Shrubb (sft. 6Jin.) is lightly-
framed and spider-limbed, albeit he shows good chest and
hip development for one of his build ; he is a hard, cheery,
genial-looking little customer. The work of the .pair on
the track is opposite. Shrubb leans forward, and gets
most of his power from behind, moving along with a
regular, easy swing that makes the effort seem nothing.
Duffey’s body is rigidly erect, inclined slightly back if
anything—he throws his legs out well in front and lifts
himself with each stride, making work of it all the time.
The’Murkan has all the “style” a runner can bring to
bear, and though there’s no show in Shrubb’s way of
travelling, there’s heaps of effect.
* * » #
Duffey and Shrubb, the sprinting and running
champions from Yank and England, like beer, and say it
does them good as a training beverage. G. D. Clark, of
Sydney cold tea circles, should pick a pump man to go
against them.
* * * *
“ G. 8.”: They badly need someone in M.L.
who can properly hold the watch. Duffey and Shrubb
are there beating world’s records on grass courses which
they and others made elsewhere on cinder tracks—
notably the faster. In addition, some of those who
finished close to them could never make such
times. I remember once an upper-class schoolboy
at the Edinburgh Academy being timed by his mates to
run 100 yards in lOsec. dead. I went down with the
pistol-flrer, a professional runner, at our own school
sports to see this boy tried out by a lad, a professional,
who could run his hundred in 11 sec. dead. The young
pro. beat the school champion by two yards, and the
latter’s time was lljsec.—both all out. The amateur
time-keeper mostly gets his watch going after the men
are running, and he usually stops it when he sees the
leader throw his arm out at the worsted. As a rule he
presents the performers with an allowance of five yards
at least. Watch-holding is as much a trade as is watch-
making.
* * * *
After years of inertia, caused by public apathy,
Sydney Amateur Athletic people ventured a sports meet-
ing on Sydney Cricket Ground Saturday last, East Sydney
A. A. running the show. Western Suburbs (Brisbane)
Club sent over a team —most members of which were
just recovering from dengue, and consequently more or
less short of form—to assist. The thing was advertised
and boomed largely, but the public mustered very
sparsely. Sydney beat the Bananalanders 6 points to 4.
Had the visiting contingent not paid their own ex’s, and
had it not been arranged that no prizes should be given,
East Sydney club would have been badly bankrupt, but
as it is they may scramble through without having to
borrow. Things were widely different 20 years ago.
Then an important amateur sports meeting drew thou-
sands of spectators, and was talked and looked forward
to, weeks before; yet the amateur of to-day is much
superior as a performer, and the conditions under which
meetings are run have improved a good deal; also, there
is considerably more variety in the programmes.
* * * *
Melbourne lias discovered, in the annual balance
sheets of its metropolitan football clubs, that, although all
the first-class footballers foot for the honor and glory of
the thing, they require a fearsome amount of coddling in
the way of boot-money. In fact, Jim’s boot-money
threatens to crush the clubs. Travelling expenses and
cab-hire form an important item in the expense list, but
it is in the boot expenditure that the trouble is most
pronounced. In some cases members demand not only
football boots, but foot-gear for ordinary wear, and some
of the players from the rowdier portions of the city seem
to levy on the club for light boots for correcting the
donah, and heavier ones for persuading the police that
the liberty of the subject is a sacred thing for which the
push is prepared to lay down the life of the other fellow.
The clubs are now contemplating declaring any player
who demands more than six pairs of boots in a season a
professional.
* * * *
S.A. Football Association has made up its dif-
ferenees.with the Adelaide Oval, and has contracted to
play there for the next five years. Last season all
matches were played on the Jubilee Oval, belonging to
the Government, but it is too small for proper football.
* * * *
“Vulcan”: Swannell, forward in the lat®
visiting English football team, is still in N.S.W., and has
applied for leave to play in Sydney metropolitan football
—preferably for North Sydney. Two other members of
the team, ex-Oaptain Bedell-Sivright and Dr. Orowther,
also remain here, but it doesn’t transpire whether or not
they propose to play.
* * • •
Among the “converts” gathered in by the
recent “ revival ” in Wales were several footballers, who
didn’t see the fun of counting the game one of the
worldly pleasures to be given up for evermore, amen. So
they compromised with Satan by forming a team “to
purify football from bad language and brutality.”
• * * ♦
J. J. Calvert, chief clerk of the N.S.W. Legis-
lative Council, and president of the Rugby Union, at the
Sydney reception to the visiting pedestrians, Shrubb and
Duffey, told how in 1851 he ran 100yds. in 9jsee. But it
was down hill—Calvert was covering about five yards in
one stride.
The Yic. Automobile Club is not showing much
dignity in connection with the Buchanan Cup. . Tho
Buchanan Cup is a rather handsome trophy, which a
whisky firm offered as first prize for the winner of the
now famous Dunlop Motor Reliability Test, which was
won by H. Tarrant, of the Vic. club. As the cup is to be
contested for annually, it was understood that the club to
which the winner belonged (only members of recognised
clubs could compete) would act as custodian, and keep
the trophy on view in the club-rooms. Then it dawned
on some of the members of the Auto. Club that the whole
thing was an ad. for Buchanan’s whisky—which was no
doubt true enough. A meeting was called, and it was
decided that the club should anticipate a request to act
as custodian, and decline the intended honor in advance.
Then the club thought it over for a while. It had nomi-
nated a number of members to compete in the race, and,
therefore, it was urged, tacitly accepted the trophy, and
to refuse the thing house-room was an insult to the
donors. So it held another meeting, and rescinded the
former resolution, but did not carry one that it would
give the trophy a niche. Another meeting will have to
be held to decide if the club will look after the trophy,
the former meeting having decided only that the club
would not rush into the offices of Buchanan’s people and
tell them that the club wouldn’t listen to any request to
take care of Fame in her winged motor-car. In all prob-
ability the cup will not be offered to the club, but merely
handed to the winner, on the understanding that he will
hand it over if another man should win the next reli-
ability test.
• • • •
Sydney has now got a Motor Bicycle Club of its
own. Being the first in Australia it has called itself the
Pioneer, and seems to consist largely of men connected
with the trade, who take an active part in the running of
it. This is in contra-distinction to the Melbourne body,
which has just decided that no one connected with the
trade can hold office. Looks as if the trade in Sydney
had organised with a rush to prevent any such develop-
ment ; but it is bound to come. The club held its first
road race on Saturday, over a 10 miles course on the
Homebush-Granville road. A baker’s dozen started. The
winner turned up in Mr. L. Saunders, on a 3ih.p. Minerva.
In the near future Australia will be turning out quite a
number of motor-bikes of local manufacture ; several new
patents of local invention are just now being registered.
* * * *
The recent motor race-meeting on the Ormond-
Daytona beach in Florida, U.S.A., saw some wonderful
records put up. The cars were all abnormally high-
powered vehicles: an 80 h.p. De Dietrich car, whizzed
over the 100 miles in lhr. 18min. 24sec., or at the rate of
over 76J miles an hour. Two other cars, French ones,
were only between three and four minutes behind that
amazing time. The driver who thus defied the Devil was
named H. W. Fletcher.
» * * *
Frank Grimley Limited are landing a new
model of their Sarolea motor-cycle, 3 h.p., with magnetic
ignition. This is an important departure for country
residents, as the ignition is automatic and more reliable
than that obtained by the use of accumulators, a con-
venience well worth the additional cost.
* * * *
In France the motor-car has won such a vogue
that in all the big cities one can be called off the rank, as
a cab can in Australia.
* * * *
Big Dunlop Tyre Co. will hold the Warrnam-
bool-Melbourne road race some time in September. As
usual, there will be the preliminary State representative
contests.
* * * *
Evidently there is still money in riding like a
whirlwind on the edge of a vacuum behind pace. German
whizzer Robl, who was in Australia a year or two ago,
made £2112 at it last year, and £4O a week is hard to get
at, sav, humping coal.
* * * *
The old rivalry between the single and married
men broke out at Ooogee (Sydney) Aquarium baths on
Saturday, when the married members of the Sydney
Bicycle Club beat the celibates in two out of three swim-
ming races, and carried off both the diving events. The
hand that helps to rock the cradle grows the best muscle
and the man who has a wife acquires the art of holding
his breath.
* * * *
Sydney has now settled down seriously to raise
the money to keep swimmer Reran in shoe-leather while
in England. The Royal Life Saving Society has offered to
defray half the cost of the journey to England, and to
treat Reran as a guest while he remains in the Cold
Country. So the required sum will not be a very large
one.
* * * *
This rushing of champion swimmer Reran off to
England illustrates a curious kink in Australian charac-
ter. No sooner does an Australian native prove himself
to be head and shoulders above his countrymen at any-
thing—swimming, racing, or drawing good, horses and
pretty women—than somebody starts a movement “ to
send him to England.” There are some people who would,
if they could, ship our Jenolan Caves to England. But for
the immovability of things in general everything that
makes this country worth living in would have been sent
to England long ago on some pretext or other.
* * * *
Mr. Watkin Wynne, manager of Sydney Daily
Telegraph, won the first swimming championship of
N.S.W. (half a mile), at the Corporation Baths, Domain,
about 23 years ago ; and, a little latter, captained a team
in the first inter-club water-polo match contested in Aus-
tralia—Port Jackson v. St. Leonards S.C., at Lavender
Bay baths.
* * * *
George Read, long-distance Australian cham-
pion swimmer, left for the Cold Country a day or two
ago. Only engagement more or less definitely made so
far is with Hobbs, 3-mile champion of the Navy; but he
has his mind also fixed on a Channel swim. Read, who has
charge of the Broken Hill City Baths, has only six months’
leave from the Municipal Council, but it will be extended
if he strikes form.
♦ * * *
“V.D.”: My memory is certainly not so
reliable as a sundial, but it appears to me that Sydney
swimmers and bath attenders should not be surprised at
anyone keeping up the crawl stroke after 200 yards has
been covered. I fancy that Rich. Cavill, brother of its
inventor, used it at almost all distances and certainly
over every foot of 880 yards on one occasion.
* * * •
There was a ladies’ double-sculling match
in Sydney harbor on Saturday. The contestants
were the Misses Lewis, of Middle Harbor, and the
Misses Oounsell, of Annandale, and the affair aroused
extraordinary enthusiasm in the two districts. They had
not long started when the boats collided, and, while the
Annandale girls stopped rowing, the Middle Harbor girls
went for their lives. Then the Annandales went in pur-
suit, but had no hope. They claimed a re-row on the
score of a foul, and it was, after a most tremendous
amount of discussion in which the ladies showed a great
capacity for talk, allowed, on a date to be fixed.
# * « *
The N.S.W. inter-State eight-oar crew put in
an exhibition appearance at the North Sydney Rowing
Club’s annual regatta last Saturday afternoon. The crew
looked very spick and span in its new boat. There was a
nice, fresh, affluent look about the men, and their rowing
was distinctly stylish. Unfortunately there was a trace
of rawness about a couple of the crew and a general lack
of lifting power on the part of the whole of them, which
suggests that they will have to pub in a lot of hard work
during the next month to get fit for the tussle before
them.
* * * *
The Victorian tennis-players who stayed on in
Adelaide for the championships had no chance against
Parker, the small, spectacled Maorilander. Bowen and
Dr. Harbison, both local men, fought out the singles, and
the former, winning, went down badly to Parker. Then
the two in partnership lost the doubles after a great
fight with Parker and Cecil Heath (Vic.). Adelaide, of
course, retained all the ladies’ championships, Miss Payne
—alone, with Miss Parr, and with Parker—taking the lot.
* * * *
Sydney Sailing Club’s final race of the season
was remarkable for the way the umpire dealt out dis-
qualifications, no less than three of the 13 starters being’
ruled out. The umpire’s action should have a good effect
on the skippers. They had no right to get bunched up
in such a manner. A jumbled race ended by Australian
winning, with Keriki and Vera placed in that order.
The same afternoon also saw the last of the Port Jackson
Skiff Club’s season. Out at Botany Bay the St. George
Club was holding motor boat races—probably it will
go on holding them, for the motor boat craze is like the
morphia habit.
* • « *
The Lenton-Frost controversy at Sydney Gaiety
Athletic Club on Saturday night drew a most substantial
house, and the gentlemen in the ring regarded the well-
filled rows of seats with intense satisfaction. There was
a difference of only 21b. between their declared weights,
Lenton being the heavier with 9st. 21b. Of the two,
Lenton at first seemed slightly the more aggressive, and
the men who were howling the odds wanted to lay them
on Lenton. Then Frost woke up. After much sparring
he suddenly landed a neat uppercut, which drew blood.
He essayed to repeat it, but was stopped by a straight
poke in the face. He staggered, Lenton tried to follow
up, missed, and ran into Frost’s extended fist. Both men
seemed to be of the opinion that the other fellow was
done, and only wanted one good clout to send him right
out. When neither went out there was a look of genuine
surprise on each pug.’s face. It was an expression of
annoyance, a sort of what-the-devil-are-you-standing-
there-yet-for cast of countenance. At the end the referee
said it was a draw.
* * • *
The Harold .Smith-John Blackmore fight ct
Sydney National Sporting Club last week went the full
20 rounds. No genuine fight lasts 20 rounds. A “ mill ”
of that duration is a boxing contest, not a fight. Smith
appeared to be suffering from stage fright, while Black-
more was in his glory. After finding himself prone a few
times, Smith’s tail began to rise. He seemed to get
tired of the recumbent position, and preferred to get up
and fight. Left drives and right crosses got home
regularly, but there was no hitting power behind them.
Most of Blackmore’s “ wharf-laborer ” swipes went round
Smith’s neck or missed altogether. Had Smith been a
hitter, Blackmore would have been knocked out again
and again. But he wasn’t; and as Blackmore had him on
the defensive all the time, Blackmore got the verdict on
points.
* • • *
Generally understood that Dan Oreedon and W.
McCall will shortly meet again at Newcastle. Last time
they fought, at Sydney National Sporting Club, it was an
unsatisfactory scrap. Half-way through, Creedon had
McCall thoroughly beaten. The then champion had been
stretched out, but staggered to his feet and stood dazed,
in a stooping position, near the ropes. It was an ugly
situation. If Creedon waded in and smashed him
up he would win the fight, and McCall was obviously at his
mercy. As McCall was on his feet, Creedon had every
right to smash him—but it looked brutal. So he hesi-
tated, and then his fighting instincts triumphed. He
crossed over and dealt M’Call a sounding smash. M'Call’s
seconds jumped into the ring and shrieked “ Foul !
foul! ” Amidst the uproar Creedon howled that he had
every right to hit. The result was that McCall got a good
breathing spell which saved him, and he pulled himself
together and eventually won. Creedon feels that he
didn’t have a fair go. And he didn’t; though it wasn’t
McCall’s fault.
» * * *
Roller skating has started at the Columbia
Rink, Sydney—otherwiselthe Exhibition building. The
latest luxury in roller skating is the ball-bearing footgear,
which is said to be a more slippery contrivance than the
original, the only genuine skate on a greased ice-floe.
* * * *
Again the Australian inventor given an oppor-
tunity, sails ahead. In the old days a year’s guarantee
was the most any tyre people would give a purchaser.
Bu,t when the Dunlop people started a big local enter-
prise, put money into it, and let it be known that they
had more to spend on good ideas, things began to im-
prove. The Doughty hydraulic process was invented,
and acquired by the Dunlop co., and, in consequence of
the improvement it makes in the construction of the
tyres, the big co. will now give a 15 months’ guarantee,
even if the tyre is to run on Bent’s roads.*
* * * *
“To Let” already figures over several Sydney
betting-shop doors. Tenants mostly have departed Wes-
traliawards, where the police are not so active and magis-
trates are very much more lenient. That “three months
without the option ” created more consternation than a
£5OO fine would have done. Present week saw two shop
clerks mulct in the sum of £lOO each, and they left
the court smiling—the money won’t come from their
pockets—but until they knew that magistrate Donald-
son wouldn’t be on the bench sleepless nights were
common. Latest move is to form clubs—some only
thinly cloaked, while others have enlarged premises with
billiard tables, etc. Strongest among the forces against
the betting-shop is the bookmaker, who doesn’t run a
shop, but would were it not for the fear of being struck
off Tattersall’s roll; his grievance is that the shops get a
lot of betting which would otherwise come to him.
Another power working hard to send the shop into
oblivion is the racing club, which complains that, were
opportunities to stay at home and bet not available,
many more people would go to the courses and swell the
takings materially.
* * * *
“Lachlan” : Re par. (B. 9/3/’O5) about picnic
race clubs degenerating into trained-horse meetings. In
Forbes they have a picnic race club which is moving in
the other direction. The chief events at the club’s
meetings have hitherto been for trained horses, but this
year all races are for grass-feds. There is humor in the
fact that in drought districts the committee turn out
stable-fed horses into grassless paddocks to qualify as
“ grass-feds.”
THE BETTING FINE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
" Heavy fines were meted out on March 30th by Mr.
Macfarlane, S.M., at the Water Summons Court, in two
cases in which persons were charged under the Betting
Houses Suppression Act.”
If I were Treasurer of the State,
This is what I would do :
I’d let the gamblers jubilate
And bet till all was blue !
Until, of course, when short of cash
I stood in need of tin ;
And then the police should make a dash,
And scoop the shekels in 1
If I were Treasurer of the State
This system would prevail:
The thief who faced a magistrate
Should straight be sent to gaol!
Until, of course, I felt the smart
That want of coin had bred ;
And then the magistrate should start
Imposing fines instead.
W. T. GOODGE.

The Royal Horticultural Society of London
has been exhibiting a rare orchid, named Cyripe-
dium Chamberlainum, so named by a collector
who sent it to England some ten years ago. Since
then it has flowered, and the significance of the
title is made more apparent by the screw shape of
its petals.

A note from “Genung,” which seems to
have some bearing on the recent pronouncement
that beer and devotion to the ladies can’t run to-
gether in quantities:—
Cairo is the most sober city I have ever visited. The
inhabitants are Mohammedans, to whom whisky is an
abomination and beer a crime. During the months I
spent there I fell over only two drunks ; and, judging- by
their language, they were Australians.
The keen, alert Cairoite turns away from wine and
whisky, and satisfies himself with two substitutes—coffee,
black, strong and delicious, and ladies, likewise black
and strong.
Down in the fish market, behind fashionable Cairo, one
can realise how low down a man can be dragged by tee-
totalism. Lill’ Buk-street, in Melbourne, and Broken
Jug-street, in Sydney, are side-walks in Paradise in com-
parison. But there are no pubs.
Here the gentlemen from the land of the Sphinx sits
nightly sipping his coffee and watching nude girls doing
the bestial “danse ii ventre.” This dance but This
Bulletin is not that sort of paper, and takes a little
liquor. And the sober Egyptian goes along the
narrow lanes of the fish market, smoking his cigar-
ette, and jokes with many an ugly, half-naked
Phyrne as she sits at her grille by her naphtha lamp, or
for a change visits “ ye lady from ze Bruzzel ” round the
corner—or worse.
But he never gets tight. His religion won’t allow him.
A story of early Bendigo. At the time
of the White Hills rush, in the fifties, three
diggers were the collective owners of a horse
named Thomas, which, in those days of big nug-
gets and scarce fodder, was worth about £100.
With oats at 18s. a bushel, two of them grew tired
of feeding the neddy, and decided to sell. The
third, however, refused to part with Thomas, and
squared matters by paying his mates £33 6s. 8d.
each. It was not an easy matter to retain horse-
flesh in those roaring times, and the sole proprietor
was accustomed to sleep with Thomas haltered
alongside him. One morning he woke up to find
himself in the company of a foot or two of rope,
but no Thomas. The ordinary horse-stealing
story ends there, but this one didn’t. A month
or so later, when horse Thomas had been almost
forgotten, that faithful animal turned up at the
tent-door clad in a brand-new saddle and bridle,
and a saddle-bag containing 221b. weight of gold.
The embezzler had sold Thomas to a digger, who
was preparing to carry his gold to Melbourne when
the animal took a yearning for home and -straight-
way satisfied it. The gold wasn’t returned.
There’s a moral to this legend, but opinions differ
about the exact nature of it. Probably it reads :
Be kind to Thomas, the horse, so that he Will
want to return to you, and may bring some one
else’s property with him.
The first passes in the recent examination held
by the Public Service Board for shorthand writers and
typists were gained by students of Stott and Hoare’s
Business College.*
An advertisement appearing in our columns
directs attention to the concert to be given by the
Philharmonic Society on Good Friday, in tlie Melbourne
Town Hall, in the presence of Lady Talbot, when Gounod’s
“ Mors et Vita” will be performed.*
The new medical remedy, Laxatine, is, as the
name implies, an aperient, and that it is not in the ex-
perimental stage is evidenced by its being the favorite
laxative throughout Europe. It is introduced to Aus-
tralia in the belief that it will prove peculiarly suitable to
this climate. In small lozenge form, of delicate flavor, at
one shilling per box. It is acceptable to ladies and chil-
dren, being pleasant to take, yet gentle but sure in opera-
tion.*
Another of the Reid Government’s
triumphs seems likely to be frost-bitten in the
bud. With Treasurer Turner’s help the
had, it was lately announced, “settled the long
standing difficulty with regard to silver coinage in
Australia.” Now it turns out that the kind
Motherland, which has been supplying Australia
with silver coins at 100 per cent, profit, won’t
agree to withdraw more than £100,000 of the
coins a year ; and as there are £2,300,000 worth of
silver tokens in Australia, it would take 23 years
to complete the process. Meantime the kind
Motherland wants Australia to take back the gold
coins with which we supply her (at a loss), when
she wears them out; and Treasurer Turner isn’t
swallowing any one-sided arrangement of that
sort. But heaven knows what the may
not be swallowing when the little grey Treasurer
isn’t looking hard that way.
Oppositionist Mackinnon points out that
Bent’s electric line to Brighton was previously
condemned in unmeasured terms by the Railways
Standing Committee. Where estimated costs
are under £20,003, the Standing Committee is not
called upon to advise, so this trip the estimate for
Tommy’s tram is carefully kept down to £19,500,
and there is no official pointing out of the fact that
it is a “mug’s game” to build an electric line to run
in competition with the State railway line and
liberally feed the tram co.’s service at St. Kilda
terminus. The electric line will terminate within
a few yards of where the tram company’s line be-
gins, and yet Mr. Bent calls this “feeding the
railways.” The railway station is about a mile
away.

North White Feather, Kanowna (W.A.), has
gone the way o{ its immediate neighbor, W.F. Main Beefs,
the reef being split up and poor at 500 ft. It is a great
disappointment, as reef looked so strong and solid at
400 ft. that manager Mills was quite satisfied it would live
to any workable depth. Apparently quartz reefs in W. A.
are a delusion and a snare. Up to date the only quartz
reef in country that has lived down and carried its gold
is Great Fingall’s.
* * * *
S. Australia is working up one of its periodical
spurts, assaying 10 per cent, mining and 90 per cent,
syndicating. Two old goldfields are having attention ; one
of them in the north-west, towards Broken Hill; the
other at Tarcoola. In the former big, droughty district,
S. Aus. had its chief alluvial gold-rush, at Teetulpa, 20
years ago ; and in the hills gold veins can be found in
scores of places. Many small crushings have been put
through, but nothing big has been found. Tarcoola, in
the far-north, has given £BO,OOO of gold in four years ;
but expenses are heavy, and the £BO,OOO must have cost,
paying fairly for prospectors’ time, £200,000. Lately a
new reef has been struck by the Tarcoola West syndicate,
and a 7oz. surface result is boomed ; but it is just as well
to add to the published hifalutin’ that the prospectors
picked out pieces showing gold, and dollied them, and got
the result mentioned. It is, nevertheless, a promising
show, a long way off.
The Adelaide people who gave the gem-stones
(tourmalines chiefly) of Kangaroo Island a good trial have
thrown up the sponge. Some very beautiful stones were
got; but they couldn’t strike anything except very low-
priced gems.
* * * »
The Bulletin would like to hear how the gem-
miners on the Anakie (Q.) field are getting on. Australian
sapphires have hitherto been chiefly exported to Europe,
but they now promise to become a local fashion. Some
Anakie yellow sapphires shown in Sydney and Brisbane
are of beautiful color and far more lustrous than the best
Ceylon article.
• • • •
The chairman of the Emu Bay Railway Co.
scattered a few grains of comfort to the shareholders in
that distressful concern in Melbourne the other day ; the
sweetest of them was that receipts from the main line had
been £9961 for the first three months of the year against
£BO3O for the same part of last year.
* * * *
Mildura still remains the one Australian inland
irrigation settlement which has battled through years of
bitterness to reasonable success. Total area irrigated is
less than 9000 acres, on which a population of about 4000
is supported. N.S.W. homestead lessees, in similar coun-
try, reckon that nothing short of 20,000 acres will support
a family without irrigation. Fruit (fresh and dried) to
the value of £150,000 was produced last year. There have
been some astonishing individual returns. Ordinary
raisin vines do not yield more than £25 or £3O gross value
per acre, and the average would not be more than £2O
gross. But there are rare growers who have had over £lOO
per acre from table raisins. Gross returns from sultana
vines have ranged from £6O to £l2O per acre, and
in one case returns from a small vineyard have reached £l4O
per acre for three years running ; but the returns will
slump as soon as production exceeds Australian consump-
tion, which will shortly be the case. Mildura output of
raisins last year was 1700 tons, about 300 tons in excess of
total Australian requirements ; and Mildura growers were
obliged to export nearly 700 tons of the fruit to London, “in
order to prevent the glutting of local markets,” as it was
neatly put. Early shipments were generally admitted to
be superior to the European and Asiatic article, but later
lots coming into competition (as old season’s fruit) with
new season’s Valencias fell flat; and 160 tons are now
being re-imported into Australia to make good the
deficiency in this year’s crops. Mildura growers were
very hard hit by the midsummer heat-wave, which
reduced the raisin crop from a probable 2000 tons to 600,
and sultanas from a possible 1000 to a little over 300 tons.
Many of the orchards originally planted proved unpro-
fitable, and have been uprooted, and it is to the raisin
and sultana vines that the settlement owes its recovery
from the slump that for years threatened to wipe it out.
T. M. Slattery’s case will give the trustee co.’s
a lift —and, probably, also the movement to establish a
public trustee’s office. The trustee co. has at least its
capital to guarantee its fidelity ; and in most cases, if its
paid capital is small, its subscribed capital is considerable.
The Perpetual of Sydney, for instance, has £25,000 paid
and £975,000 uncalled ; and when shareholders are liable
for £9 15s. a share, they can be depended on to keep a
moderately sharp eve on the management—which takes a
lot of the responsibility off the man who is dead or has
gone away.
* • 4 *
The Randlords are beginning to think that
they never knew what trouble was till they met Ah
Sin. The Johannesburg correspondent of the Mining and
Engineering Journal (N. York), a mine-owners paper,
wails as follows :
“Bret Harte’s story about the ‘wily Chinee’ is no
exaggeration, as we are beginning to find out. A certain
percentage were ‘ born tired,’ and no matter where you
put them they seem to do very little work. They do
quite well at forging. It is the custom of the mines to
pay the hammer men so much per inch drilled. One of
these never did more than 12 inches. Suddenly his
ticket handed in at the office showed 72 inches. This
record kept up for several days, until the head time-
keeper discovered that the Chinaman had found out the
art of changing a 1 into 7. At first the number of
‘Chinese who flocked to the hospital was embarrassing.
A very tedious drill was instituted for convalescents,
some violent medicine was given to the sick, the con-
valescent were given light work for a week, receiving no
pay whatever. The percentage of sick dropped as mer-
cury does before a blizzard, and now it is no exaggeration
to say that very few Chinamen go to the hospital unless
they are really ill.”
By the way, it is so like the Randitti to include in their
course of medicine “ light work for a week without pay.”
* * * •
D. : Argument that a co.’s capital is borrowed
money, and that there isn’t any more harm in a country
borrowing than in a co. issuing shares—that, in fact, the
two processes are the same—is all wrong. If a co. raised
money on its assets, and then sold those assets, and used
the proceeds as revenue, and thereupon went out and
borrowed more money to run the business and pay divs.,
instead of making calls on the shareholders, or if it didn’t
charge shareholders a reasonable amount for services it
was rendering them, it would be doing something like
what Australian borrowing States have done.
‘ ‘ 535 ” : Most primitive school I ever saw
was at Twelve Mile (Q.). Rough slabs, iron roof,
unlined, unpainted ; undressed boards on stumps
for desks and seats. The tank was a bucket. The
roof sloped so that at the back the teachers crown
was five inches from Hades. Teacher lived at
“ one of the parents’,” where her room resembled
“ the school,” and a newly-blazed track connected
.the two.
New “ have,” so far as this paper is con-
cerned : The story of a cross-counter :
It was the first “ smoko ” after dinner. We had all
finished rubbing our “ tongs,” and were spread about the
(board in more or less picturesque attitudes, when Speewa
remarked to the Brolgan :
“ Is that the pair of pants you bought out of the
pawnshop ?”
“No!” snorted the Brolgan, “I never bought any-
thing out of a pawnshop in my life.”
“ Bet you a quid that’s where you got them, anyway,”
persisted Speewa.
“ What’s the good of bettin’,” said the Brolgan—“ you
ain’t got any proof?”
“ Never mind,” said Speewa ; “ put up your stuff, and
we’ll get Burke to act as referee.”
The Brolgan reluctantly rose, took down his coat, from
an inner pocket extracted a “ quid,” and handed it to
Burke. This was covered by Speewa.
“Now,” said the latter, “ did you buy those pants in a
pawnshop ? ”
“ Tell yer I didn’t!" replied the Brolgan.
“ Then,” yelled Speewa, triumphantly, “ you must
have bought them out of one, and the tvvo quid’s mine.
See ? ”
“ Hold on a bit,” said the Brolgan, quietly—“l never
bought ’em at all; I shook ’em. See ? ”
Then the bell rang.
AT POVERTY POINT.
A syndicate is to give Kaigoorlie (W. A.) a new theatre.
Building is to cost £15,000.
“ Woodki ” : There’s this to he said for the coon song
(B. 6 4/’05)), that it very rarely descends to “dirt,” unlike
the ordinary “comic,” with its invariable lodger-and-
missus gag.
Actor George Coppin completed his 86th year on
Saturday, April 8. He and that other George, who is now
J-Prime Minister, are among the most successful comedians
Australia has known.
Miss Margaret Thomas, mezzo-soprano of the Parkina-
Foldesy Concert Co., makes her debut on the stage as
Nauova, a Oingalee maid, in the “ Cingalee,” at Sydney
Her Majesty’s, Easter next, unless recent law proceedings
prevent the production.
Andrew MacCunn, conductor at Her Majesty’s, Sydney,
is a Scotchman, son of a Greenock shipbuilder, and
brother to the well-known composer, Hamish MacCunn,
whose operas, “Diarmid” and “Jeanie Deans,” were
performed successfully some years ago by the Carl Rosa co.
The girls who flock to matinee shows in Melbourne are
much interested in Miss Maud Jeffries’ matrimonial affairs.
“Where is her husband?” is the first whisper that you
hear along the circle. When they have at last spotted
the plump Osborne, the sentimental girleens watch for a
chance look between the pair, and they go away quite
angry with their Maud if she doesn’t give a wifely glance
at her way-back actor-hubbie.
Veteran Howard Vernon talked interestingly to a
Wellington (M.L.) interviewer. He mentioned the
“stars” he had starred with—the majestic Barnett, the
gentle Leonora Braham, Clara Merivale, and, in answer
to the query as to which was the best of the bunch, said—
“ Oh, Nellie, of course—Nellie Stewart. God gave her a
great many gifts, I know, but she has supplemented them
with hard work, and she now is without a doubt one of
the greatest all-round artists in the world. Her last act in
‘Camille,’ as I saw it in Sydney, is a very fine bit of
emotional acting.” Vernon likes Vernon in “Rip Van
Winkle ” best.
“ Drift ” : For the good of his audience it is quite
possible for a man to have too good a reputation as a
joker. Witnessed the “ Orchid ” from a back-stall seat,
and scarcely heard Lauri through a single line, owing to
ante-appreciation. As the grin preparatory to a joke
swooped across his face, and the first word of it exploded
among the scenery, a roar of laughter from audience
drowned his sally a hundred fathoms deep. A lOh.p.
shrieker at my elbow invariably smothered at its birth
what might have been a good joke ; then asked through
her hysterics what it was. The low-spirited and dys-
peptic, who have to hear the witticism in order to raise a
smile, are robbed of their best medicine by frivolous people
with a hair-trigger laugh.
“ Melb.” : Miss Alice Pierce, at the Opera House, is an
object-lesson in the overwhelming applause lavished on
her, as much as when Cinquevalli holds up a man, chair,
table and newspaper in his mouth or balances a spring
cart on his chin. Alice is intensely human. She drops
into the programme just as Madame Slapoffski did once
at this theatre, when her song of Jerusalem was sand-
wiched in between two banalities and electrified the
gallery. Miss Pierce gives that one touch of nature which
makes the whole world kin, though she only does it in re-
productions of Mrs. Leslie Carter, Ellen Terry, Ada Reeve,
Edna May, and the New York hoodlum. It has often
been pointed out that Mr. Rickards is in danger of heap-
ing up the merely curious. When Disraeli saw the
woman shot from a cannon at the Aquarium, Montague
Corry remarked, “ Difficult feat.” “ Yes,” said Dizzy, “ I
wish it were impossible.”
Harrie Chambers, the youth with the Higinbothamically
monumental forehead, who used to be a pressman in
Sydney, while his elder brother, Haddon Chambers, was
scoring as a dramatic author in London, has had a play (a
4-act comedy) produced most successfully in New York.
His “Abigail,” written round the love and other adven-
tures of a New England girl who seeks her fortune in New
York, gets great praise from the best local papers. The
Evening Journal describes it as “ a very nice play, very
witty, and sometimes touchingly human, light as a snow-
flake, but just as pretty and as glistening as a snowflake in
the sunlight.” And the Herald says it is “ dainty, refined,
now pathetic and again amusing, with bright lines. A
clean and wholesome story.” The Evening Sun says there
are those pearls of greatest theatrical price—real tears—
in the second act; and all the papers agree that the young
author’s speech in returning thanks on the first night was
the neatest of utterances.
“Snell”: Met an Australian ex-actor in a Melbourne
suburban market the other morning. He was still clean-
shaven, and he was selling poultry to bargaining women
from boarding houses, and bringing to the work all the
arts and graces and the elocution that made his second-
rate parts in Shakspeare noteworthy in a day that is
gone. The way he declaimed, “ Excellent chickens,
ladies, excellent chickens, on my word of honor as a
gentleman—born of my own eggs, bred on my own lands,
tender as mercy, plump as partridges, and two for twc-
and six ! ” was really fine, and accounted for the excellent
business he was doing. I asked him why he had taken to
chicken-fanning. “It was the eggs,” he said. “Old
associations, you know.”
“Tot”: “ Autolycus,” discussing players of the seven-
ties in Melbourne Argus, is delighted with the smart
replies in Charles Mathews’ farewell speech at the old
Royal to a gallery boy’s interjection, “ What about Ben-
digo? ” where Mathews failed to give satisfaction. Three
minutes after the delivery of the address, present writer
asked Mathews for the written document, and found it
contained the gallery boy’s query, smart replies and all,
properly punctuated and ready for the printer. Like
many other humorists, Mathews was an artist in the pre-
meditated impromptu line.
Lottie Lome has been giving imitations of her husband,
the late Johnny Coleman, at the Orpheum, Manila.
Some local theatre goers resented this. Miss Lome
in defence states that she knows almost nothing out-
side of what her husband taught her and asks time to
prepare a repertory.
Augustus Thomas has dramatised Charles Dana
Gibson’s “Education of Mr. Pipp” series of drawings;
the play is now running at the Liberty Theatre, New
York. Pipp, the tiny multi-millionaire, played by Digby
Bell, is, of course, the hero, and the fun of the play lies in
the crushed worm attitude this bulwark of the Steel
Trust assumes in the presence of his gigantic wife and
bouncing daughters.
At a suburban theatrical performance in Melbourne
recently the even tenor of the play was interrupted by a
sudden dash on the part of a stout red lady, who climbed
up the side steps leading to the stage, and laid violent
hands on the soubrette, claiming her in a shrill, fierce
voice as her run-away daughter, and hanging on so
valiantly that it was necessary to run down the curtain.
Loud arguments were then heard behind, and eventually
the performance was resumed, but the rosy face of the
determined mother was visible as she mounted guard
in the wings, keeping a watchful eye on the sad
soubrette.
“Tip”: A Poverty Pointer (B. 9/3/’O5), mentioning
some names of Camilles on the Australian stage, omits
the first and not the least distinguished—Mary Provost,
who flourished in ’59, with Neil Warner as Armand Duval.
Madame Majeroni, in more recent times, was alse very
fine in the character.
Sydney contralto, Miss Bertha Faning, is making a
good impression on the critics of the Cold Countrj-. This
tall, tuneful Australienne recently sang at Plymouth in
place of late Belle Cole, then ill, and got much ‘ local
press-patting.
Died suddenly in Adelaide, early this
week, John Darling, sen., founder of Australia’s
big wheat-milling and exporting firm, and father
of John, jun., S. Australian politician, and of
cricketer Joe. The elder Darling was something
in the engineering business in .Scotland 60 years
ago; coming to Australia he got a job humping
wheat; then he began to mill it; finally the firm
got so far into the business in four States that it
is said to have handled as much as 20,000,000
bushels in a year.
Alleged now that the sentence years’
imprisonment with hard labor) imposed by Mr.
Justice Pring on T. M. Slattery is technically
wrong. Under the Crimes Act (N.S.W.) the
lorigest term with hard labor that can be given is
for two years. For any greater term than two
years the sentence should be with penal servitude.
Received from the Law Book Co. of Aus-
tralasia “N.S.W. Justices’ Duties,” by barrister
D. S. Edwards. The purpose of the book is good
—to guide the J.P. in the discharge of his duties ;
and it seems to have been carried out conscien-
tiously and well.

“ Speciality ” he defines as follows : “ That which is the special or particular calling of any person or business;
that for which a person is distinguished or which he makes the object of special attention.
According to these definitions, the followers of particular pursuits become Specialists-. In the early centimes
there were tio Specialists ; as the world advanced they became a necessity. The World discovered that the “ Jack-of-all-
tra<tes T Jfdes haV^dowTeeome individualised, knowledge centred in various pursuits, the twentieth century gives us five
thousand Specialities of occupation, Each man attends to his own business, and becomes perfect through following out his
amblt The Public recognises quickly the greatest good for the greatest number, especially in Medicine and Surgery, where
immediate good results to ills of Humanity. Medical science was once practically at a standstill, .proved by terrible
epidemics that swept across the world now subservient to scientific method . To-day we have the Specialist m Diseases of
Men, Who', naturally apt in studying certain forms of premature decay, combines with his aptitude that experience which he
has gained ifl lorig years of active practice.

The Camel.
“ W. J. 8.” discourses about camels :
Let me supplement “G.E.’s” statement (B. 16/2/’O5)
that the white man manages camels better than the
nigger. Even as far back as '96, when I left W.A., the
white man’s superiority in this respect had been clearly
established, as was also the superiority of the camel bred
by white men to the nigger-bred, imported animal.
Oanil's bred by Thomas Elder in S,A, carried a 30 per
Cent, heavier load than the imported beasts, ftnd the
game breeder’s riding-camels easily travelled as far in twd
clavs a,s th,e .best Egyptian or Asiatic camel did in three ;
the .buying price of. a S. A.-bred camel being- often, twice
that tit thfe bthersi... .this.superiority .was due to the
. ©
White man's more scientific breeding; care bein
taken In. the selection of sires-whereas, the niggers
allow -their stock to breed haphazard. On Cue
goldfield once we saw a crowd of Afghans m?em
from a “must” bull-camel that had broken loose.
The niggers were t.oo scared to do anything but run
every time the brute charged ; but one Angus Nicholson
-an Australian-born white man—waited for the beast,
stepped aside, and, tempting him to turn got a grip'in
his nostrils, forced him to his knees, quickly' re-fastened
the staple in his nostrils, and subdued the brute.
Nearing a camel-camp one could always tell from ttie
appearance of the animals straggling after feed whether
White men or niggers were in charge ; nigger camels al
wstvs showing signs of ill-treatment and neglect. i_adule-
sefes oh their backs were often left until the animal
hfecame disabled, and there have been instances in which
an Afghan driver '‘doctored” a huge saddle sore by
covering it with bagging and stitching the bagging to the
poor brute’s skin with sewing-twine. This was to conceal
the sore, because the police were beginning to move in the
matter of protecting the camels from savage cruelty.
But-even With that, the saddest sight on the goldfields
was’a niggOr-drivell camel-train, finishing a long journey,
the poor brutes being covered with festering- sores, the
smell of Which made them unapproachable. The bull
camel, however, smells ugly enough at any time, and his
aroma clings to his loading, Flour, oatmeal, &c. (in fact,
all provisions not hermetically sealed), take the taint
strongly, and retain it in spite of cooking ; and, with a
delicate, stomach, the first mouthful of camel-tainted food
brings on vomiting.
Occasionally the camel is worked in harness. I ve seen
a team of four bringing a waggon-load of goods to the
field, guided by an Afghan, whose driving would have
made a bullocky weep. The animals were all dodging the
collar, and the nigger had no idea of getting them to pull
together. He just yelled like a fiend and walloped the
nearest shatter till it was exhausted. Then be wept and
cursed, and flogged the poor brute more ; till a digger
smote the callous pork-hater with a ham-bone, which ha
had kept on hand for unmistakably conveying to an>
Afghan our unfavorable opinion of him. The camel has
been tried with a buggy, and the Nannine Warden drove
a camel-tandem in this way ; but the animal’s build fits it
emphatically for carrying. The crack W. A. riding-camel,
in my day, was a S.A.-bred beast, owned by Broken Hill
Wilson. Ridden by a white man, this flyer put up records
far above anything done by a nigger camel.
Finally, the notion of the nigger’s special aptitude for
camel handling was invented and is kept alive by a section'
of the press for its own purposes just as the British lew.
and-Jingo press represents white workers as inferior to>
the Chinese coolie.

The Lawyer.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
OT counting those of the fraternity in
the “Beautiful Harbor,” N.S.W. has 165
barristers in practice (exclusive of Judges)
and 875 solicitors. Of the latter, 475 hunt the
mullet in Sydney ; the charge of the other 400-
alas, a full 400—is in the back-block towns;
Mlillet are growing rapidly tndre and more eiusivb
too, Yet seldtihi pdssbs tbriid iri which the
d'eVii's huihbet |i.3) ot bhmd -hew attorneys swim
t)ot into the profession', barristers increase more
slowly ; the average increment being from 6 to 10
a-vear.
» * « •
Ten years ago there was scarcely an attorney in
the State who was not making a comfortable
living. To-day not more than a third earn above
£2OO per annum, This is partly because of the
rapid increase of solicitors, and the. Consequent
diminution of charged dub tt) Cqhipfetitibh; arid tfs
splitting-up tif business; ahq also jn, q, .large
ifleasu.rb t'o 4 developed public tendency to confide
important affairs only into the hands of big and
reputedly vvealthy firms. An unfortunate fact
that, for which the legal profession has to thank
its defaulters, and its own stupidly short-sighted
policy in over-protecting itself. For the Courts
sel.lom punish a rascally and insolvent “gent one M
who has played up his client’s little fortune, more
severely than by striking hint 6ff the rolls. Arid
naturally the jlriDlib; atvare that it takes a rf.sk ir,
trusting a Solicitor at all, prefers to run as small,
fisk.ris possible, and to spread the same risk over
ris great an area of corporate legal avoirdupois as
is available.
» * * *
But even the largest and oldest-established
firms no longer make their ancient incomes. It
is stated, upon the authority of a solicitor of 30
years’ standing in Sydney, that £lOOO per annum
is a rarely justifiable quotation of the individual
profits of any member of the profession. As for
the smaller fry, the great majority live from fin
to mouth. Many of them will now defend an
accused ill the police-bourts for half-a-gUihba, ahd
at the Quarter Sessions for a pound. The Set fees
half-a-dozen yeats agb for Such WOrlc Werb £3 3S;
and £7 7s, respectively; Of a lower grade arb a
number of Solicitors, who, haunt tile lbssbf Courts,
With ah air Of defiant diSrepUlability, and Will
thankfully iiddlbJsiS thfe„ Beak for half-a-brbwri. A
Woirtari Wno fell out Of a tra,ni-car the other day
rind was taken to a hospital, discovered; wheri sli'e
Subsequently tecov'er'ed from tier in§ensibiljty arid
leached home, that thb Business cards of three
different sdlicitols (one of a man in supposedly
bulge practice) had reached her.
• * * *
Barristers, as a class, are better off; chiefly
for the reason that very few are fools enough
to get themselves “called” unless they
possess sufficient money to tide over a briefless
wait of from five to ten years. Of the 166
now practising in N.S.W., 100 earn less than
£5O a year at the Bar. Of the remaitidbrj 30 feafii
about £2OO, 20 from £2OJ to £5OO, id from £5OO to
£1000; find five from. £lOOO to £5OOO.
* * • •
Forbidden by a cast-iron custom of the trade
from seeking the layman client in his lair, the
young barrister’s only means of getting-on is to
Cultivate assiduously the friendship of every
solicitor he knows. This he contrives to do, as a
rule, by hinting a readiness to advise —gratis—on
knotty legal points. Such constantly arise in the
practice of the lower branch, and young solicitors
especially are glad enough to have a cheap
“ pocket counsel ” to assist them in building-up
their own reputations. The solicitor’s gratitude
is expressed in the next speculative action he
brings before the Court. If his client were a rich
one, he would brief a big-gUn, But in speculative
Work the client often is a man of straw, and the
big-gun always Wants eash-dowrt. The young
barrister, however, is glad enough to get a chance
to wag his jaw, With or without pay; and he
thankfully accepts the speculative brief, upon
which he marks proudly a fee he may get if he
wins the case and the other side chances to be
“ good for costs,”
* * * •
The speculative action is, in sober truth, the
only certain ladder by which barristers can climb
to fame and fortune. There are instances, of
course, to the contrary, as, for example, the
man whose father or brother is a Judge or a
leading solicitor; and a few others. But those
are few and far between ; and so, also—unhappily
—are speculative actions, for the majority. And
very lucky is a junior at the bar, however studied
an advocate or however deep his learning, who
can afford to demand his fee on hearing, before he
has been 12, or even 15 years, in practice.
* * w *
Naturally, the junior barrister has much time
on his hands. Dozens idle for years in their
chambers before they get a brief. The frugal or
necessitous bombard the press with MS. The
studious become bright lexicons of precedents.
The better-off collect together and yarn and
smoke, or hold mock trials. All, to a regretable
extent, spoil their tempers and the best years of
their lives in the vain and hideous inaction of
waiting—waiting they come at last to hardly know
for what. A barrister at 35, who is not a philoso-
pher, is a bitter-tongued cynic. The poor among
them, curiously, are the most patient, and the
most self-confident. Writer knows one man—called
about 15 years—who during the first six made but
two guineas at law, by putting through a probate
application. He had not a farthing in the world
when he started, but he just managed to live and
pay his office rent by coaching an occasional
articled clerk through his final exam. Yet,
throughout, his belief in his ultimate success never
wavered. He was twice offered by a commercial
friend a fairly well-paid clerkship, bat he pre-
ferred to live on two meals a day, the more sub-
stantial of which usually consisted of a bloater, an
apple, and a bun. Proud as Lucifer, he was never
known to accept an invitation to a meal, but, ia
spite of his carefully-patched clothes, he was uni-
versally popular by reason of his genial humor and
unfailing cheerfulness. He is now making me. e
than £7OO a year.
A SOLICITOR.
The Australian boy—that curious mixture
of Red Indian stoicism, chivalry, cruelty ai d
moral cowardice —in one of his best aspects. L o
was aged 12 and was the son of a Glennie’s Cre( 1:
(N.S.W.) settler. His horse fell with him through
putting its foot in a hole (mark that the boy was
not thrown, the Australian boy seldom is thrown)
and his thigh was broken and the injured horse
left on top of him. The boy got to his whip and
flogged the horse until it struggled up and set his
maimed body free. Then (with a broken thigh)
he got into the saddle and rode home two miles.
Reaching there safely (he read an illustrated paper
until the arrival of the doctor I
r -the late discovery at Main waring Inlet,
Tas. W. coast, of the remains of the long overdue
barque Acacia, which left Port Esperance in June
last, emphasises once more the incapacity of the
present Tasmanian Government. A half-hearted
expedition was sent up the coast, in the July
following her departure, as far as Elliott Cove,
a few miles south of where the wreckage is scattered
along for miles, and then returned to Hobart.
The burning of a few more cwt. of coal would
have decided the fate of the barque. The Govern-
ment has been, lauded for its strenuous exertions
towards entertaining Lord Northcote on his
recent official landing ; but when the question is
the life or death of a barque’s crew, it sits down
and weeps miserably over the cost of a cockle*
shell’s coal for half a week.

History.
The history of incandescent gas lighting
is the history of the success of Dr. Carl Auer
Ritter Yon Welsbach’s splendid chemical dis-
coveries in the application of the rare earths
thorium and cerium in the preparation of the
Ssrfect incandescent mantle —the Welsbach.
ut it is not only a story of glowing success of
science successfully solving great problems
and bringing new light to the world. There
is a more sordid side—the record of the many
fights against imitations which, if the public
will but understand, cannot hut be inferior.
Success has crowned the Welsbach Company’s
campaign throughout the world. But it has
only been secured by persistent effort.
But what, perhaps, will most interest the
reader is why he must get a Welsbach
mantle and how he is to tell it. When
Dr. Welsbach invented his mantle the
patent specifications read that his mantle was
immersed and treated with a solution of
thorum and cerium, the resultants being a 00 to
70 candle-power illuminant. That stands good
to-day, and no imitation can infringe this
formula. Imitations must utilise ir greater
percentage of cerium, and the public thereby
loses and only get 32 candle-power instead of 70.

An Australian Industry.
It is particularly interesting to note that tha
Welsbach Company’s spirited action in de-
ciding to make Welsbacli mantles for Aus-
tralians by Australians in Australia is receiving
a just reward. Owing, probably, to climatic
influences- —such as the glorious Australian
sunshine —but, perhaps, also to the brightness
of Australian intelligence, the experts of the
English-company find that the mantles made
in Australia are better than those made any-
where abroad This is of great importance.
The Australian Welsbacli Mantle is found
to have an average life of Over 1000 hours as
against less than half that of mantles made in
other countries.
In ’93 the Welsbacli Co. started operations
in a street off Wynyard Square. So recently
as December, 1904, Mr. W. T. Stutchbury
arrived from England to take up the
general managership of the Company in
Australasia. Mr. Stutcnbury was originally
the managing director of the Sunlight
Incandescent Gas Co. in England, which was
bought up by the Welsbacli Co. Within a
week of Mr, Stutchbury's direction of opera-
tions, he determined on the abolition of sole
agencies, and started six branches. The result
has been striking—an increase of 300 per cent,
in the output, and the employment of
double the number of hands, although this
period has been summer-time, and, of course,
under ordinary circumstances, the quietest
for all lighting concerns. Mr. Stutchbury has
introduced special machinery and other im-
portant innovations in the works, increasing
their capacity tenfold, with the result that
they stand now as a most interesting example
of a modern model factory employing girl labor.
The conditions under which work is carried
on are excellent—-a special dining-room is pro-
vided for the girls, w 7 ho participate in a bonus
above their salaries.
* * * *
Mantle Manufacture.
Many use the Welsbach Mantle without
knowing the delicacy of manipulation neces-
sary in the various processes of its manufac-
ture, which are generally illustrated on this
page. Here in Australia the girls employed
by the company are going daily through the
same delicate operations as at the works at
Wandsworth, in England, where they turn
out about thirty million mantles per
annum. In the first instance, one enters
a spacious compartment, where interest is
immediately aroused by a half-dozen girls
diligently mangling babies’ hose without feet
with miniature mangles. Of course it is not
quite this, but something much more impor-
tant—the immersion in a solution of rare
earths, and their subsequent wringing of
innumerable short lengths of strips of tubular
cotton hose that are mantles in embryo
destined to light the homes of a Federalised
Australia. To give some idea of the magni-
tude of operations, these girls can treat some-
thing like 12,000 mantles a da,y. From
wads of figures like this the visitor flees
to another floor where more girls are
fitting the impregnated bases on to moulds,
which are put into drying cupboards.
After the drying process come more
girls who take and pass the myriads of
mantles on to folders, whose duty it is to
straighten out and fold flat in readiness for
fixing in another department. The fixing
operation refers to the top of the mantle. If
you take up a Welsbach mantle you will
notice a pink ring round it—that is the fixing
—to which this part of the mantle is subjected
before the top is gathered, and the asbestos
string, by which it is suspended is passed
through it. One by one the flat, limp fabrics
are picked up, and the tops are just passed
between two little rollers —the operative part
of the machine—and ouc they come with about
three-quarters of an inch of their length im-
pregnated by the pink fluid, and the fixing
process is completed. Then there come more
girls, and the toughening and other bewilder-
ing processes, such as burning off in a fiery
furnace, until each mantle lias gone through
the hands of a dozen girls and singly placed in
cotton wool in bones to go to the consumer.
Burners and Fittings.
One of the greatest triumphs of the Welsbaah
system is the manner in which it has been
made possible to practically adopt all the
advantages claimed for electric _ lighting.
Until the Welsbach system came into force,
electric-light was preferred by many for the
ease in which it could be turned on or off
with innumerable switches, and the decorative
effect that could be obtained through the
manner in which the incandescent lamps
could be suspended. But the Welsbach

TV * * *
For some time past MunicipalitTesTiave been
much concerned with the question of gas
versus electric light, but the battle fought
recently at the Glebe Municipal Council has
practically settled the question, as the
maintenance cost of incandescent gas lighting
was completely and satisfactorily demon-
strated to be considerably less than could be
obtained by a much less efficient system of
electric lighting. The Welsbach Co. of
Australasia, Ltd., have a most complete
arrangement for municipal street and out-
door lighting of every description. To com-
pete with the electric Arc light system,''they
have the “ Million,” 1000-c.p.; the “ Hamah,”
700-c.p.; “ Padova,” 700-c.p. ; the “ Hansi,”
400-c.p. ; the “ Tiber,” 700-c.p.; and the
“Hallwyl,” 210-c.p.—from £3 ss. upwards.
In electric lighting, one of the most recent
and beautiful features has been the adop-
tion of flame-colored arc lamps, and for
some time it was thought that the advance
in incandescent gas lighting had been abso-
lutely checkmated. This, evidently, is not
the case. The Welsbach Co. have, after
the most careful experimenting on the
part of their experts, produced a light—
“ The Oceana ” (the light of the South) —which
is eloquently described by the co. as “ moon-
light made manifest.” It certainly looks a
very much improved arc electric-lamp, with
none of the hard glare, which ruins com-
plexions, and spoils all shades of color, while
the eyesight is greatly preserved by this light.
Types op Mantles. .
The Welsbach Company hav.e adopted in
Australia three types of mantles for general
domestic purposes. The first of ’lthese is
known as the Welsbach, on every one of
which they place their trade mark, the “ W”
in the steering wheel. The special feature of
the mantle is the texture of the fabric, and the
length of time of treatment. The fabric is es-
pecially woven with a cantilever stitch, which
makes it the strongest mantle made. The
price is 7d. per mantle throughout Australasia.
It is of interest to note this, as the company
have adopted two cheaper mantles, the “ Sun-
light” and the “Gladiator,” price Gd. and sd.
each, to fight fraudulent and spurious imita-
tions. These mantles are treated with the
same preparation as their 'standard “ Wels-
bach, ” but in order to cheapen cost of produc-
tion a lesser cost hose has been
which does not absorb so much of the rare
earth preparation in its manufacture.
* * « *
Heating. .
lt might be thought that the utility of the
“Welsbach” incandescent system would have
ended with lighting, but the principle was
too simple not to extend to cooking and
heatin'? purposes. In the splendid show-
rooms of the Company throughout the States
and New Zealand, amongst the latest lighting
fittings, can be seen the most artistic and
convenient heating radiators and cooking
apparatus. The Welsbach-Kern patents are
employed in gas raditors of the most beautiful
description in pottery, copper, and hammered
iron from 32s Gd upwards—a combination which
completely does away with all the objection-
able features of gas stoves. In gas cookers
every size of family is studied, with the result
that the baby’s food can be heated as easily as
a theatre supper provided for the most
bohemian-inclined epicurean.
