I sit on the verandah at the closing of the day
And compare myself to Ovid in my modest little
way—
To Ovid in his exile, dreaming evermore of Rome,
And in vain beseeching Caesar to forgive and take
him home.
He dwelt amongst barbarians, and sang his
mournful song
Beside the frozen Ister and the Euxine shore
along ;
But T, ’midst kindly Irish, dwell upon an upland
plain—
And still I long for Sydney and its narrow streets
again.
The wheat is cut and garnered, and the ploughing
has begun ;
The ruddy soil lies naked to the kisses of the sun ;
There’s harrowing, and burning-off, and other
sights to see,
And great potato-digging in the fields of Arcady.
The farmers use, to break the ground, a fine four-
furrow plough.
Their ancestors would smile if they could see the
Irish now—
For they wrought hard with wooden shares their
frugal crops to raise.
When Ceclit, the Plough, they worshipped in the
old Dedauaan days.
In spite of new machines the Earth is full of won-
der sweet;
There’s still as much of magic in the springing of
the wheat
As when around the fields at night, the ancient
legends tell,
The Naked Maid in darkness walked and wove a
magic spell.
A liomely-looking folk they are, these people of
my kin ;
Their hands are hard as horse-shoes, but their
hearts come through the skin ;
They are all right well-connected in this land of
Arcady;
And if your name’s not Hogan here it must be
Hegarty.
And Nature, God preserve her well, is kindly
Irish too ;
The winds croon Irish melodies the swaying gum-
trees through ;
And ev’ry little hill about, with green cap cocked
and curled,
Says “ Come upon the top of me and look around
the world ! ”
The stream goes singing on its way, and well I
know the tune—
’Tis “Slantha” in the morning, and at night
“ Eileen Aroon ” ;
The magpie warbling in the woods with rich, clear
purple note,
Pretends that he’s a blackbird with a Cork brogue
in his throat.
They love the land they live in, all these folk that
I esteem—
But the land they left behind them is an everlast-
ing dream.
Old Michael Cleary said to me—his age is seventy
seven—
“ There’s no place like Australia, barrin’ Ireland
and Heaven.”
There’s rest and peace in plenty here, and eggs
and milk to spare ;
The scenery is calm and sane, and wholesome is
the air;
The folk are kind, the cows behave like cousins
unto me . . .
But, please the Lord, on Monday morn, I’m
leaving Arcady.
N.s.w.
VICTOR J. DALKY.
Not Much of a Woman.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
THERE were very few in the shop, as it
was not yet mid-day. And the big,
flapping blinds of white canvas softened
the light on the pyramids of fruit.
Maggie was waiting for her brother. She had
her veil pushed up to her forehead, and her red
face was trickling all over with perspiration. Her
purse, with its brass clasp and elastic band, lay on
the table beside her.' and was crossed protectively
by her umbrella. She held a check-ticket in one
.hand and a glass full of “ squash ” in the other.
A brooch, with her name, gripped the two ends
of her collar together so tightly that her throat
creased into a little pink fold.
Presently her brother came in, rolling his eyes
at the waitress and chewing his moustache.
He carried a very thin little stick, and wore a
rabbit’s ear in his hat, which was tipped far back
on his head.
Maggie stared at him in admiration, and put her
squash on the table till he was seated.
He took the chair opposite to her, and rattled
the medals on his chain as he drew out his watch
and held it up to her.
“Ha’-past to the tick,” he said. “IleftEdie
outside because I knew you wouldn’t like to see
her.”
“ I’m glad you’ve got that much sense,” said the
■girl, smiling to soften the effect of her words.
“Well, you’ll have to get used to her,” he said.
“Dad’s in town, and he’s going to help us get fixed
up to-morrow.”
Maggie’s mouth quivered.
“You an’ Dad are a pair,” she said—“ the
maddest wretches on earth ! ”
The man twisted his moustache, and made a
great show with a silver buckle ring on his finger.
“If.you’re going to ‘perform,’ ” he said, “jest
say so, an’ I’ll clear out.” ' .
“Oh, Bill, don’t bo so nasty,” exclaimed the
girl. I only don’t like seeing you throw your-
self away. Edie isn’t straight. No! No ! !
Whatever you say—she isn’t straight”— —
“Damn it all ! be careful, now,” snapped the
mm, dropping his paw with a whack on to his
knee. But the girl looked into his eyes so that
he kept the lids lowered, and the lashes quivered
over bis hairy cheeks.
“Mind you,” she continued, “she’s as bad as a
woman can be. She lives in a bad house. She”—
“Well, I know all that. Hasn’t she told me
herself?”
“Yes—well, then?” and the girl stretched out
her hands in expostulation.
The man moved his head like some great beast
straining at a chain.
“Can’t I raise her?” he said. “Eh ? Can’t I
raise her ?_”
“ Yes—if anyone can.”
“ Weil, and so I shall.”
“0, Billy, you don’t know what you’re doing,”
entreated Maggie. “Just think—every one
knows about her. Tom Bourke an’ Arthur John-
son. And all that lot. You are too good ”
The man was bending his stick over his knee.
“Think of her awful nickname,” persisted tho
girl.
“ I —tell—you—what,” said the man, as he leant
right over the table, jerking out each word
separately ; “if —we —weren’t—here—l’d —break
—your —neck!”
Maggie shut her eyes, but didn’t give way an
inch.
“ You—you will marry her ? ” she said, her hands
stiff out in front of her.
“ I’ve told you so a billion times ! ”
“Then, if you do,” she said, “I will live the
way she has lived, and I will live in the house she
has lived in. And my nickname —my nickname will
be”
But she broke-down, her tears flooding in fire
over her cheeks.
The man tilted his hat further back. “ Bah ! ”
he said. “ You ain’t much of a woman.”
H. McC.
"Une Petite Affaire.”
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
WHEN the betrothal of Jules
Legrand and Berthe Bossier was an-
nounced, not a soul in the sleepy little
French Alpine village but saw the hand of God
in aso happy contract. For would it not heal the
quarrel that had existed for the last 10 years be-
tween old Legrand and Bossier, whose properties
adjoined ? Then, as if that were not enough to
ensure the happiness of any marriage, did not
the young people love one another ?
When the day fixed for the wedding arrived,
with it, in all manner of ramshackle conveyances,
came the guests who had been invited to partake
of the bullock, the two sheep, the 10 turkeys, the
twenty ducks and fowls and the barrel of wine.
After four days of feasting, the last morsel of fleth
and last drop of wine had disappeared ; the guests
departed, and Jules led his young wife to the
farm he had rented high up on the bleak, pine-
clad mountain side ; for Pfere Legrand and Fere
Bossier were far too lively and active to think of
going out of business for some time to come.
That was in the late summer, and as the sum-
mer came round again the little one arrived.
It was the evening of the anniversary of their
wedding, arid they were sitting by the door of
their house, looking at the moon rising behind the
snow peaks, far away on the other side of the
valley. Berthe was singing the baby to sleep,
while Jules unconsciously puffed his cigarette in
time to the little nursery-rhyme, perhaps better
known to Frenchmen than the Marseillaise :
Au clair de la lune,
Mon ami Pierrot,
Prete toi ta plume
Pour 6crire un mot.
Ma chandelie est morte,
Je n’ai plus de feu ;
Ouvre-moi ta porte
Pour l’amour de Dieu.
The baby slept, and Berthe ceased.
Jules asked in a low tone, “Why art thou so
fond of that song, Berthe ? ”
“And why not?” she replies; “’tis a simple
song, and (shyly) it has the name of my baby.”
And then she added, with the suggestion of a
tremble in her young voice, “ Poor little Pierre !
he will be a great man some day.” And Jules
laughed deep down in his broad chest, and ran
his toil-hardened fingers over the baby’s head.
• * * *
A narrow lane a hundred yards long, joining
two streets in Noumea, canopied with overspread-
ing trees. In the middle a gas-lamp that casts a
glimmer in the blackness. Near this a small
crowd of seven or eight. A fat man in pyjamas
runs out and seizes a Kanaka, who has just arrived,
by the shoulder.
“Bun for the police ! ”
A crowd clusters in front of two men who stand
by a large iron gate. One is tall, black-bearded,
with a stern face. In his right hand he holds
a revolver whose nickel barrel glimmers in
the moonlight. With the other hand he holds
the right arm of the second, who, abject with fear
or weakness, clings to the gate to support himself.
It is easy to see that the latter is a relegue, a small
morsel of the flotsam scum of France flung out of
the way on to the waste-heap of New Caledonia,
liis hair is grey and tangled and clotted with
filth ; his eyes are shifty and blood-shot with alco-
hol; his face is pale, shrunken with hunger and
stained with dirt. He wears an undershirt almost
black with filth, tattered canvas trousers and rope
sandals that cling by a miracle to his bare feet.
Presently he begins to shake violently, and with-
out any warning drops in a heap on the ground.
'The man beside thrusts his revolver into his belt
and begins to roll a cigarette, while a girl among
the half-a-dozen sightseers giggles.
“ How did it happ n, friend?” a man asks.
“ Why, it is very simple,” the man with the re-
volver replies. “I hear a noise in the fowl-yard ;
I go down and find this man ; I seize him by the
arm ; he struggles; I point the revolver at him ;
he thinks to knock it from my hand, and hits it
clown with his fist, and— roild— I think the ball is
in his stomach.”
The man on the ground groans faintly and tries
to move. A gendarme arrives and sends for a
doctor and ambulance. He kneels by the stricken
man and lifts his head.
“ What are you ? Free man?”
There is a titter in the crowd What a devilish
droll dog this policeman is, to be sure ! No libere,
even though ex-forger or murderer, would think
of committing a petty theft such as this.
“ No—relegue.”
“Number?”
‘‘-Seven—Thousand—and fifty—six.
He speaks in gasps.
“ Name ? ”
“ Pierre Legrand.”
The policeman has finished, and lets the head fall
back on the ground. The relegue lies silent, and
a bystander, perhaps a little less heartless than
the rest, bends over him and tries to arrange him
more comfortably.
Presently the relegue begins to speak—huskily
and unintelligibly at first, and then clearly :
Ma chandelle est morte,
Je n’ai plus de feu ;
Ouvrc-moi ta porte
Pour l’amour de Dieu.
He relapses into silence.
The crovvd look at each other wonderingly, and
one taps his forehead with his finger. The man
who has mistaken the poor wretch for a human
being bends closer as the relegue again begins to
whisper, thinking that perhaps he may wish to
give one last message. The words come faint yet
clear—
“Ma chandelle est morte —tant pis—tant pis”
—and then he adds, so faintly that to the listening
man it seems almost a sigh—“ tant mieux.”
He says it with his last breath.
The bystander rises with a scared face, for there
is something terrible even in the death of an
animal.
“ Friends, he is dead ! ” .
“God have mercy on his soul,” says a devout
Catholic, crossing herself. “And to think of
going into the presence of his Maker with a
nursery-rhyme on his lips.”
Rut she is wrong. Pierre Legrand had offered
up the only prayer of his life.

Children of the Gulf.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
IT lay like a piece of charred driftwood
stranded at the inlet’s mouth. Mimosa
watched it, half ta.M, „ ,he tide
sucked into the narrow creek at the rear of the
hut. At high-water the black length would vanish
into the darkness of the mangroves. Mimosa had
learned to watch for its return, and there were
times when she thought that the long, square
ij . . . , , , 1
snout would push its way into her kitchen.
Whenever bustu Ma was angry he would run
out with a boat-hook and threaten it savagely.
Beyond the grey loneliness of the sand-hum-
mocks lay a township of tin hovels and shanties
Crowds of half-caste children scrambled about the
pier ; children with white bodies and Mongolian
eyes; children with brown eyes and the blue-black
hair of Malays. There were the podgy brats
belonging to Sam Foo and “ Chinese Kate ” ; the
i & r it t' it , ’
slim, dank offspring of Jim Maroola and
I nsco Sal ; and they sat between hills of
oyster-shell and paddled in the brown Gulf slime,
“Big Jack” was the king alligator of the Gulf
creeks; his hiding-places were innumerable, and
... . . ,
the township dogs knew him for a treacherous
scavenger that trapped their tribe in the dark
places and tide-flushed holes. Occasionally they
followed him up the inlet, barking furiously ; but
the bravest cur never disputed his right to be first
m Sam boo s blood-hole on slaughtering day. Foo
was head butcher in the township.
There was plenty of life in the little Gulf port.
Across the sand-hummocks was the opium-house,
kept by Wah Sang, the noseless Tonquinese,
whose verandah swarmed with Manila-men and
Cingalese, with here and there a myall from the
Jardine country. There were times when the
hasheesh-den was filled with white-faced French-
men, who came like starved wolves from
Noumea, by way of the Barrier and Cape York.
One day a big, red-cheeked Englishman strolled
along the beach and stopped before Mimosa’s
hammock. He raised his pith helmet slightly,
but Mimosa turned away her head and frowned,
E“f Englishman looked puzzled. _
“ Many pardons, madam, but I mistook your
residence for a tea-house. I have been in Kobe
and Tokio. My name is Bennet. You will not
refuse me a cup of tea.”
Mimosa, swinging in her hammock, kept her
head away. “Sustu Ma is my husband. I am
his wife a long time ago in Nagasaki.”
“Nagasaki is the front-window of the East,”
laughed Bennet. “It is the bed-chamber of the
moon Lady from the East, give me
so ?l® tea ! ”
Mimosa turned slightly in the hammock. “ My
husband has gone out in the lugger, for pearl; he
will be here to-morrow of the morning.”
“ Happy to meet him,” said Bennet, quietly,
“For sheer courtesy and nobility ot mind, give
me a Japanese husband. Little lady, what is your
_
“ Mimosa Ma.” She glanced at him, and he
saw in her dozing eyes the childish wonder and
amusement that beautifies the faces of Japanese
W rnu. en ' _ _
The luggers in the offing became wrapped in
mist. The booming ef surf came from a distant
headland.
* • * *
“The moon is shining,” said Mimosa. Her
eyes sought the starlit bay, and Bennet, with a
cigar fuming between his teeth, saw that in the
making of her face the gods had lent a hand.
Bennet also noticed that his chair was clamped
with brass, and as he lay back he felt that it would
have accommodated a giant or a family of police-
men. The elbow-rests were of Burmese teak, its
back was ornamented with copper images and
strange, fish-like devils that stared at him with
crystal eyes.
“The chair is from a Manchu temple,” she
laughed. “ My husbmd brought it from the war.
. . . Tell me, O Bennet, of the people in Syadney
that is as far from here as Japan. . . . Beautiful
Syadney.”
Bennet pushed the chair from beneath the
bamboo awning into the open. “ Let us go near
the water,” he said—“l hate dark corners arid
uncertain husbands. In Fort Said I spoke three
civil words to a lady barmaid, and a knife flew
out of a geranium-pot and cut a hole in the wine I
was drinking. I like the open air,” he sighed;
“ it never hits a man unawares.”
Mimosa toddled after him unabashed. “Do
not go far,” she whispered ; “the night-air is not
good for my slippers.”
Eight bells sounded across the bay. From
beyond the distant hummocks came the squeak of
a Chinese fiddle. “ Hi yah, sama choo yah. Hi
yah ! ” The shrill laugh of Chinese Kate broke in
upon the Mongolian song. The fiddle throbbed
on like a maddened nerve.
Bennet stretched himself in the chair like a
huge cat, while Mimosa squatted at his feet,
moving her fan as though it were a flame or a new
emotion.
“ Tell me about Syadney, 0 Bennet. Show me
your beautiful watch that goes ch’k-ch k.”
The moon crossed the headland, like the
Dragon’s Lamp seeking the Queen Beast in her
cavern. All along the Gulf the surf was breaking
with a silken thrash.
“Come with me to Sydney, Mimosa. I will
show you life and money. Come to-morrow,
Mimosa.” He stooped and kissed her fingers.
She evaded his touch with the skill of a fencer.
“You English talk, talk love. Show me your
pretty watch that makes ch’k,-ch’k noise.” She
leaned towards him with sudden tenderness.
The Englishman laughed, half-raised himself,
then something gripped him foot and waist; a
black fist closed on his throat until it seemed to
drive the blood unto his eyes. To Bennet it was
a new kind of garotte. It left him limp and
choking, as though he had swallowed a poisoned
eel. He felt a cord being drawn about his wrists
and ankles.
“ No good to kick, O Bennet. You see by-and-
by that Japan no damn fool.”
“ With the imprisoned blood roaring in his ears,
Bennet felt the wedge-like chin of Mimosa’s hus-
band crunching into his breastbone.
“ Not so rymbuck as you think, eh, O Bennet ?
It is up to you for a new face. Yoshimo ! Hear
me think.”
Through his strangled vision the Englishman
discerned the black close-cropped head of the Jap
sheller stooping over him. A thick halyard drew
his arms to the sides of the chair until they almost
cracked.
“What’s your game, Sustu Ma?” Bennet
coughed hoarsely and wriggled. “You mistake
me, perhaps, for a thief. I was merely talking to
your wife. I’d give something to know that
jitsu garotte of yours. Damn it! you’re not
nine stone.”
By way of answer the Jap knelt on the sand
and carefully examined the knotted cord. “You
are fat, 0 Bennet, but you are not strong. You
have mistake my house for a yoshiwarry. I begin
to have the white man’s feeling in this matter.
Mimosa is my long time wife. You see, eh ? ”
“ I ’ll buy out of it,” grunted Bennet. “ What
is the price of your feelings, Sustu ? ”
A savage arrogance came upon the Jap ; he lit
a cigarette and sat cross-legged beside his prisoner.
“The English, 0 Bennet, can look at death—
when they can buy out of it cheap. But death
cannot look a Japanese in the face—it can only
squint at him. You see me think, eh ? ”
“Good for you, Sustu. Loosen my hands and
feet and I’ll fight any kind of death you’ll put up
for me.”
The Jap’s face grew hard as stone ; an old scar,
wide as a bullet, trenched his cheek from eye to
chin ; he sat as immovable as an idol, and it
seemed to Bennet that centuries moved between
each heartbeat.
Mimosa was silent; she did not plead for his
life ; her attitude was one of pleasant obedience.
While the cord ate into his flesh she merely
smiled. “ The wet sand will draw the colors from
my slippers,” she said.
With nimble fingers Sustu relieved him of his
watch and chain, his diamond ring, and with brutal
cleverness he juggled in the half-light with the
sovereigns extracted from Bennet’s pocket. Then,
beckoning Mimosa to follow, he slipped away into
the darkness.
Bennet listened to the sound of the breakers.
Through the starlit haze he saw the white crests
racing over the near sandhills. It was evident
that Sustu intended he should drown in the com-
fortable rocking-chair. Another thought struck
him cold as ice. The creek at high water swarmed
with alligators ! He strained forward to get his
teeth into the cord that held his arms, but the
Jap. had left no stretchable parts in his hurried
bit of work.
Bennet breathed hard as the first incoming wave
touched his ankles. Within an hour the water
would be over his head. He was not certain even
now that Death’s approach could shake his cour-
age. It was a bleak, foolish end, and his vanity
cried against the way Mimosa had seemingly
betrayed him.
“ A watch and chain; a few coins ! Great Scott,
they’re worse than a pair of Mayfair bilkers ! ”
The sea approached stealthily, and blinded him
with spray. It seemed to him that a Chinese
fiddle was squeaking somewhere in the bed of the
ocean.
Something splashed through the water. A
faint perfume stole upon him. Through salt-
blurred eyes he saw Mimosa standing beside the
chair. A colored toy lantern hung from a stick in
her hand.
“England is getting wet,” she said, demurely.
“ You are a fool-man, O Bennet.”
“The water is as bitter as your treachery,
Mimosa. Have you discovered that I am too rich
to die ? ”
A white wave raced between them and re-
treated noiselessly. Mimosa ran a little way and
then returned.
“Sustu Mais asleep,” she said, plaintively. “ He
takes Chinese opium for his neuralgia. He will lie
until I pour the black coffee into his throat.
Listen, O Bennet.”
She stooped, holding her lantern towards the
inlet. Something was thrashing in the mud-belt.
The dark mangroves seemed alive with crawling
shapes.
“Never mind the alligators,” grunted Bennet.
“ You've got Sustu’s knife in your hand : cut
these knots—cut, cut the flesh if you like, and be
damned.”
Mimosa shook her head, “You will die, O
Bennet, if you put on pig’s head too soon. You
see me think—l talk like Sustu now. lam very
pretty.”
“You are,” assented Bennet.
“I want to live in Syadney. You marry me
quick? No talk, eh?”
From her pocket she took a sheet of paper, and
held it under the lantern so that he could see.
Bennet, with spray in liis eyes, read the neat,
copperplate writing—“ For saving my life, I
hereby promise to marry, at the earliest oppor-
tunity, one. Mimosa, a Japanese lady from Naga-
saki. Signed.”
“Cut this hand free, Mimosa, and give me your
little fountain-pen. Better to be a dry bigamist
than an unassuming corpse.”
Bennet danced in the sand to improve his circu-
lation, while Mimosa blew sand craftily upon his
wet signature.
“ I want to make sure that Sustu is asleep,” said
Bennet, quietly ; “I don’t want any more ji-jitsu
unawares.”
Mimosa, with a finger on her lip, led him to the
rear of the house. A lantern swung from a
bamboo. The Jap pearler lay on the floor, his
garotter’s face limp and drawn with pain ; one of
his large hands hung stiffly in the air, as though
thrusting back the horror of his dreams.
“Heis a bad man,” whispered Mimosa. “He
beats me with a rope, but you must forgive him."
Bennet was silent.
“ When shall we go to Syadney ? Where shall
I see you to-morrow?” She clung to him a little
as he plodded towards the township. “I will
make you good little wife. You will not be sorry,”
she pleaded.
A sudden depression seized the Englishman.
He held her from him so that the starlight fell
upon her face; he saw the kisses of Sustu, the*
garotter, on her lips.
“Mimosa, Igo from here to-morrow. I cannot
take you. A man must keep and marry white, or
his children will look at him with Mongolian
eyes. Do you understand ?”
She clung to him half wildly, and he listened
for a moment to her childish weeping.
“Meet me at the bank to-morrow at 10,” he
said huskily. “ I will give you fifty pounds to
take you back to Nagasaki.”
She met him next day at the bank. Wah Sang
over the way blinked and trembled at sight of the
money passing into her pretty satchel.
The eternal sorrow of Dante’s Beatrice was in
her eyes. “Good-bye, Mimosa.” He kissed her
in the street.
Her sobbing followed him to the steamer. The
sharp misery of it woke him in his cabin at mid-
night as the vessel headed for the straits.
“ Damn it! ” he said, “the look in her face was
terrible. I hope it wasn’t a fake.”
ALBERT DORRINGTON.
It is always necessary to go to a religious
faper for the nakedest untruth in a controversy,
